And it is that you should take a cannula of bronze, or iron, or of a vulture's feather, and place it on the corn, or the callus. Then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula.  Hold your hand and rotate it with a gentle pressure, so that the edge of the cannula presses on the root of the corn and the hot water finds its way deep to the root of the corn.  Let the patient wait a little while on the soaking of hot water for an hour.
Then leave him, for the corn is eradicated with its roots.
Do this with each of them, one after the other, until you have dealt with all those that are on the body.
Then treat them after they are eradicated with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Tremor.
When tremor occurs in someone due to cold in a nerve, or from a quartan fever, or from another cause: then it is necessary to cauterize it with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra with a cautery, and on its chest with a cautery, and on its stomach with a cautery.
For tremor rests with the cautery of olive oil, and the health of the cold ailment hastens.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter LVII.
Sometimes foul Albathir, that is, pustules, are expelled in the body, which are formed from corrupted gross matter.
Therefore, when they appear, it is necessary first to cauterize every head of the pustule with a fine cautery made of myrtle wood, whose tip has been ignited with fire. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner, and they do not increase, and the superfluity which makes them is broken, and they are healed from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done after evacuation with phlebotomy.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a blood flow from an artery in a wound.
Many times a blood flow occurs from an artery which is cut in a wound that happens externally, or at the incision of an abscess, or a cauterization of a limb, and things similar to them; and it becomes difficult to cut it off.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then quickly apply your index finger to the opening of the artery; and press it firmly, until the blood is blocked under your finger, and nothing more emerges from it.
Then place small, olive-shaped cauteries, and many large ones, in the fire; and blow upon them, until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery has been severed, and place the cautery upon the vein, after you quickly remove your finger; and hold the cautery until the blood is cut off.
If it is expelled when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery, and extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from the cauteries prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease to do this one after the other, until the blood is cut off.
And beware, lest you burn the nerve which is there, and some other harm befall the sick person.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, then it is not possible to cut it off: especially when the artery is large, unless by one of the four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the blood is cut off. Or it is tied with a strong ligature. Or medicines of a property to cut off the blood are placed upon it, and it is bound with a suitable constriction with a cushion.
However, whoever administers its excision with a ligature and constriction with a cloth, or by applying burning things and similar items, does not benefit it completely, unless rarely.
If, however, this happens to someone, and a physician or medicine is not present for him, then he should begin with the application of the index finger upon the opening of the vein itself, as we have described, and with its strong constriction, until the blood is obstructed from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger is not to be removed, over it continually with very cold cold water, until the blood is congealed and thickened, and severed.
And meanwhile, let him consider what it needs from a cautery or medicine.
Concerning incision, perforation, and phlebotomy in wounds, and similar matters.
We have already said in the first chapter about all illnesses in which it is fitting to use cautery with fire and burning medicine, and its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I placed there chapters from the head down to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter along the same path, so that the seeker may have his question alleviated.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you, children, to know that in this chapter is the terror of that which is in the first chapter concerning cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary that there be greater caution in it, since in the operation that is in this chapter, frequently evacuation occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision upon an abscess, or opening of an exit, or treatment of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision upon a stone, and similar things: from all of which is associated trembling and fear, and death occurs in many of them.
And I warn you, lest that which causes hesitation fall upon you, for sometimes the ways of men with the ways of infirmities come upon you in this art. For among them is he who is angry with his illness, and death is light to him, because of the intensity of that which he finds from his illness and the length of its malice. And with illness is the fear that signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his money, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not agree with him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be greater than your greed and your rapacity. And do not presume upon anything from it, unless after certain knowledge, which verifies for you that to which a praiseworthy succession has arrived.
And use in the treatment of all your sick, prognostication and indication of that to which health is fittingly to be given.
For in that is help for you in the acquisition of fame and glory, and of memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O children, with His understanding, and may He not take away from you direction and inspiration; for it is in His hand.
And I have indeed arranged this chapter by distinguishing it into chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on the cautery, from the neck to the feet: so that your seeking of what you desire from it, and what you need, may be eased.
Concerning the treatment of water that gathers in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this illness often occurs to children at birth, or when the midwife tightens the child's head without ease.
And sometimes it happens also from a hidden cause that is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this illness in others than children, and death has hastened to every one whom I have seen of them: therefore it seemed to me to omit the treatment for it.
And indeed I saw one child of them, whose head was filled with water, and the head grew in every day, until he could not sit upon himself, because of the size of his head: and the moisture increased, until he died.
And this moisture, indeed, either gathers between the skin and the bone, or gathers under the bone above the sinus.
And the treatment in him, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary that you make a single fissure in the middle of the head, in width: and let the length of the fissure be like two knots, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the form of the lancet.
If therefore the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then place those two fissures, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side, and water is depressed when you press it with your hand towards the interior, and it is not hidden from you, then it is necessary that you make three fissures in the middle of the head, according to this manner.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the fissures with cloths and lint. Then apply a liniment of wine and oil to it until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound equally. And feed the sick person with all dry food with little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is to consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes there is more in the occiput, or in the forehead, or in the right, or in the left.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and collection of water appear to you: and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe, lest you cut an artery, and a flow of blood occur, and the sick person die from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of Arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When catarrhs occur to someone in the eyes, or in the chest, and it is prolonged, and treatment with medicines does not help, then the ultimate treatment in that case is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to cut them, it is necessary that the sick person shave their head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then let the sick person tie their neck with any cloth they have. Then consider where the vein beats. And the places of both are the places that are depressed behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in some men.
Then mark with ink on both sides: and cut both with a scalpel carefully on both sides, the incision reaching the bone: and let it be across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery, and cut it upward with the incision.
And let the length of the incision be like two fingers joined together.
For when a vein is cut, blood flows out with a pulsating exit, which leaps inward with frequent leaps.
If however the artery does not appear to the senses, then it is necessary to measure from the ear a space of three fingers' breadth. Then mark with ink, and cut until the bone.
And that which it is necessary to emit of blood, is VI ounces, and perhaps you emit from it more, or less. All of that according to the quantity which appears to you from the weakness of the infirm person and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains on the bone from the sifac, then cut it, so that a hot abscess does not occur; then bind the wound with linen strips.
Then treat it with ointment, until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man suffers from chronic hemicrania, or acute catarrhs due to acrid humours, and heat in the temporal muscles, or chronic, severe pain, and similar ailments: and is treated with the methods of medical treatment, and it does not help: then we are experienced in these illnesses in the extraction of the arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have narrated.
And the method of the operation in extracting them is that the weak person should shave the hairs that are in the temples, and aim at the artery appearing in the temple. For it indeed shows itself to you by its pulse, and is rarely hidden except in a few people, or by extreme cold.
If it is hidden from you, then let the weak person bind his head with any cloth of his: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or warm the place with hot water, until it appears to you by clear manifestation. Then take the spatula, which I have made a little while ago. Then scrape the skin with it with ease, until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook into it, and pull it upwards, until you pull it out of the skin, and cause it to escape from the layers beneath it on every side.
If however the artery is slender: then wrap it with the end of the hook. Then cut a part from it according to the amount by which its ends will be elongated and contracted, and so that no flow of blood occurs. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not ascend completely.
Then evacuate six ounces of blood, up to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong double thread, of silk, or of al-hohod cords, so that putrefaction does not quickly occur in it before the wound heals, and a flow of blood happens.
Then cut away the excess that is between the two ligatures. Do it at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone, with a knife cautery, until its ends are cut, and it will stand in place of the same work.
And it is a better work, unless the sick person has a fever, or is of a hot complexion. Because cautery is from among those things that help to end humidities. Therefore, it is a better aid.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the place should be filled with old cotton; and cushions made neatly should be placed upon it.
And after the removal, treat it with dry medicines that cause flesh to grow, and with bandages, until it is healed.
If, meanwhile, during the operation, a flow of blood occurs from an artery, then quickly cut it with a cautery, or fill the place with zegi, and bind your hand tightly until the blood is cut off. If none of those are present for you, then place your finger over the place until the blood congeals. And embrocate the place with water of intense cold, until the sharpness subsides, and it is bound as necessary.
And since that which is lighter and easier to remove from an artery is a vein, cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades; and the sharpness of the two blades should be similar to an almond. However, they are of much less sharpness than two blades. For if they are sharp, like two blades, the cold rushes to both of them; and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is held within them, and the flesh is cut quickly; with this, therefore, the work is better than any other work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space between the two blades be the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with ink; and place both of them intensely hot, until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places, so that the distance between its ends may be lengthened. For they do not completely consolidate with this cautery.
Chapter Four.
Concerning the treatment of persistent, acute tear flow from the eyes.
When the tears are persistent, and their flow is from the veins situated on the exterior of the cranium: and no medicinal treatment with remedies helps them, and you see the patient's face already reddened, and he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead, like the crawling of ants: and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that the hairs are already corroded with the corrosion: and his eyelids are heated by the intensity of the tears: then the ancients used this operation in their treatment, and they named that treatment with the Sword.
And it is that you should instruct the patient to shave the hairs that are on his forehead. Then make three equally spaced incisions along the length of the forehead: and let the length of each incision be about two fingers, and let one incision be equidistant to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second slightly offset from the movement of the temples, and the third on the other side: and be careful not to cut the arteries that are on both sides: and keep your hand away from the line of the two lower jaws, and let the space of each incision be the size of three joined fingers: and have a piece of sponge prepared, or many dried cloths, with which you dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, whose following is the form. From the incision on the temple side, up to the second incision: and scrape with it all the skin, which is between the two incisions, with the sifac, which is above the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision to the second incision. Then withdraw this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument, called a sharp scalpel, one side smooth, the other side not sharp. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached with the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone: and drive it, until it reaches the middle fissure: and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes: except what continues the incision up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after an equal quantity of blood runs out, express the places from the clots of coagulated blood.
Then place in every fissure a linen cloth from old cotton, and place over them cushions soaked in wine, or oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the bandage, and administer a plentiful infusion with lukewarm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment, and the other things with which wounds are treated, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs in the eyes from interior parts of the head.
When acute, severe, persistent catarrhs befall someone, and you see their eyes weakened, small, and their gaze already impaired; and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and there is a sharp, damaging pain deep within the head, and a subsequent sneezing; then, from these occurrences, understand that these matters, and catarrhs, do not arise unless from many veins.
And the better treatment for them is this. And it is that you should order the sick person to shave their forehead. Then, make one incision in the middle of the forehead, or another slightly wider, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision reach the bone; and move your hand away from the temporal muscles, which move during chewing, until the bone is exposed; and you will dry the entire blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with old cloth, or with linen strips. Then bind them from above with pledgets: and soak the pledgets with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you loosen it, and see that the hot abscess is already lessening, then you should rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes the growth of flesh; for example, if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of resin, and prepare a plaster from them, and administer it for the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or something similar to a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a liquid substance, such as water, vinegar, and similar things; or some animal.
Therefore, when a stone, or a kind of stone from those that do not adhere nor grow in the ear, falls into the ear, expose the ear to the sun.  If you then see the stone, instill into the ear some oil of violets or sesame. Then administer its extraction, and cause sneezing with condiments, and plug the nostrils at the advent of sneezing, after you have placed in a circle around the ear fragments of cloth or wool, and you extend the ear upward.
For it often emerges with this treatment.
If, however, it does not emerge, then administer its extraction with gestures, that is, with fine tweezers: whose shape is this.
If it therefore emerges with the tweezers, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a fine, single-pronged hook.
If it still does not emerge by that, then make a tube of bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole deeply, and seal what is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so that there is no way for air except through the tube; then extract with your suction device with strong pulling.
For many times it emerges with what we have described. If, however, it does not emerge in this way, then take a small amount of helkalimbat, or of viscus, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, and place it at the tip of the rod after you have carefully wrapped a cloth around it; then insert it into the ear hole easily, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If, therefore, it does not emerge with all that we have described, then hasten to incision, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of the incision is, that the phlebotomist first make a weak incision in the cephalic vein, and extract from it blood according to the measure of his strength. Then make the sick person sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near his fat in the depressed area there; and let the incision be shaped like a crescent, until you reach the stone; then remove it with what instruments you can.
Then close the incision quickly with your suture, and care for it until it is healed.
If the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
If, however, it does not yield to you for exit, then take a thin, slender spatula, of this form.
And administer with it the excision of that kind of grain which falls into the ear, and do not do it when you think that the grain is already moistened by the ear's vapor, until you place many small pieces of it; then extract them with hooks, or with a thin probe, or with suction, as we said, for its extraction is thereby eased.
However, in the water that enters the ear, it is necessary for the afflicted person to use a sneeze, first of all; and already he has filled his ear with some old cotton; and he is lying on that ear, in which the water is. If, therefore, it exits through that, it is good.
And if not, then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them with fire for a short time, and let the afflicted person insert one of them into the opening of his ear; and let him jump on his foot on that side, and strike with another stone the stone that is in the ear. Therefore, he should not cease to do that with stone and stone, until all the water emerges.
And now the water emerges in such a way that you take one from papyrus or from rushes, and insert one end of it into the ear; and set fire to the other end with fire, until most of it is burned. Then again use another rush, and do that many times, until all the water emerges; and extract it with a cannula according to what was previously stated about the stone.
In the extraction of an animal entering it, if it is of small body, like a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which appears to the senses, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks; and its removal is easier than all those that are held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then you ought to look at the ear in the sun. If therefore anything of the worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with fine hooks.
If however nothing appears there, then take a cannula, of which this is the form, narrow below, wide above; and introduce the thin end into the ear according to the amount that the infirm person tolerates; then suck with it a strong suction. Do this many times, until all the worms come out.
If, however, they do not yield to you for exit, then smear the circumference of the cannula with wax, as I said in the stone.
If they do not come out with what we have said, then administer the distillations which the ancients have already tested for the destruction of worms; and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring of oils and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver, or bronze, narrowed at the bottom, having a small hole, wide at the top.
Let the part that pushes, which is in the concavity of the cannula, be made of bronze, properly made. And if you wish, take a rod, and wrap lint around its end with a suitable wrap; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever medicine you wish from among these into the cannula; and let it be in the ear; then insert the rod with the lint on top, and press it with your hand with moderate pressure, until the oil is propelled into the concavity of the ear, and the sick person feels it inside; and that which is poured into the ear should already have been warmed a little with fire; and beware that the thing that is distilled into it be not very cold, for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the treatment of an accidental obstruction of the ear.
Sometimes infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it also happens to some people that the ear passage is obstructed by a wound or flesh grown there.
And this obstruction sometimes occurs deep within the ear cavity, where vision does not reach; and sometimes it happens near the exterior of the cavity, and vision follows it.
Therefore, that which happens deep within the ear, and which the gaze does not encompass, is in many cases difficult to heal.
And in that which is visible, you should place the patient's ear in the sun, and look at it.  Then if you see the obstruction, open it with a slender probe, whose shape is as follows: its end should have a small width, one part being pointed; and the remainder of the probe should have two smooth sides, so that it does not injure the ear.
If therefore the obstruction is from flesh grown in the ear canal, then hold it with a fine hook and cut it out with gentle force, until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, then take a thin, smooth rod and warm it slightly with fire; then insert it into the ear canal.
If, therefore, you feel an obstruction with which the patient experiences a dullness of hearing, attempt its opening with care; and be cautious not to injure the ear's nerves.
Then apply a compress to the ear, corresponding to the size of the wound, wrapped in Egyptian ointment.  Do this for several days, until you are certain of the wound's healing.
Or take a compress and soak it in rosewater, and sprinkle powdered zegi on it, and use it.
If you see the ear already suppurating, then you should remove the compress and apply another one, wrapped in a cloth made with rose oil, until the hot abscess subsides; then treat it until it is healed.
If, however, bleeding occurs, then immerse a sponge or cloths in cold water and apply it to the ear.  And use the remaining treatment, which stops bleeding as described in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the treatment of ulcers of the eyelids.
Chapter VIII.
These ulcers that occur on the eyelids are sometimes moist and sometimes dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with forceps or a hook, and cut them with a scalpel from their roots. Then place pounded zegi on the affected areas.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine, lens-shaped cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
On the treatment of Granularities of the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter IX.
Many times something happens in the eyelids which is similar to Granulation in its strength and hardness, and on account of that is called Granulation: and it is an aggregation of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore, the operation on them is, that you should then consider, if it is Granulation proceeding in that which appears of the eyelid, and moves to every part, and its matter is easy.
Therefore, make a fissure over it by its width. Then scrape it from every part with ease, until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
If, however, its excision is not prepared for you, unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a small incision, then that harms the sick person in some way.
If the fissure is large, then gather it together with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and heals it.
And if the swelling is sloping inward toward the inner surface of the ear, then invert the eyelid, and suspend the swelling with a hook; besides which, you need an incision, and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is lengthened by incision, that will not harm it in any way.
Then wash the eye after the swelling's incision with salt water, and treat the area with what heals, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment of the swollen eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is a fat that is generated in the tissues of the upper eyelid; it most often occurs in children, and it weighs down their eyes, and they have cataracts in the mornings, and cannot look at the sunlight, because tears rush to them, and because of that you see them lying on their faces constantly, and they sleep on them, and their eyelids beneath their eyebrows are moist, which swelling has already covered; and the prominence of the Axirnath is visible to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the Axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the sick person should place his head in your bosom.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare on it strips, and make a circle from that strip according to the quantity that Axirnath contains on all sides. Then place it upon it, and press with your fingers on all sides, so that the Axirnath is gathered in the middle of the circle.
Then make a small incision into the middle of that moisture with a lancet, along its breadth, and let the incision not be larger than that which is made in phlebotomy in the depth.
Or it is necessary to pierce the whole skin until you reach the Axirnath, for in many arrangements the Axirnath proceeds from the incision according to the form of a piece of white fat.
Therefore extract it with the cloth, which you have placed between your fingers toward the outside; and you will turn your hand to the right and left, until it is dispersed; and beware, lest you put in the incision, so that you do not reach the eye with the lancet, and injure it.
However, if Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you shall add a little to the incision with ease, until Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then immerse the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them over the area, and bind it with bandages.
And of men there are those who grind salt, and place it in the concavity of the incision, so that it liquefies that which remains of that moisture.
Then treat it until it is healed.
If however there occurs in the place a hot abscess, then treat it with sedative plasters, until it is healed.
Concerning the methods for Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye is softened, whence it happens that it is wrinkled as it should not be.
Chapter XI.
When superfluous hairs sprout in the eyelids, beyond the natural hairs, and grow old: then they harm the eye, and cause various illnesses, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and white discoloration, until it becomes a cause of the eye's destruction.
And indeed, Ataxmir of the eye is cured according to four methods. Or by cauterization with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what has been previously stated in the chapter on cauterization. Or Ataxmir is cured by incision and suture. Or by tubes, according to what I will say.
It is necessary that you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it is turned, it is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, in which there is thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and cause the needle with the thread to penetrate from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid, where the hairs sprout: and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with the radius.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid beneath the added hairs with a spatula moving from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then pull out the thread, and place a small cushion of cotton, or cloth, beneath the eyelid; then mark upon the eyelid with ink, which shall be like the figure of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the figure be made according to the size and elevation of the eyelid, since it is already diversified in humans.
For among them is one who needs a large amount to be cut from the eyelid, according to the amount that softens from the eyelid.
Then, with a spatula, find above the two lines you have marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct, and let the incision be near the natural hairs, as thick as a ray.
Then introduce the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scrape off the entire thing. Then bring together the two lips with a needle and thin wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and make that which is left over from the threads adhere to the two eyebrows with certain inconspicuous things, if you wish to do it: and if not, do not bother.
Then leave the sutures and threads until three or four days, afterward care for it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures, and care for it with that which dries and constricts. For the eyelid is lifted during the sealing of the wound and its bringing together: but it will be better.
This method of treatment the ancients taught.
However, there is care for the sick person in it, and it is from good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir also: and it is, that you mark on the eyelid a figure, which is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have related.
Then lift the eyelid with three separate hooks, or joined together, according to this following form, then cut what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this form, with equal incision.
If however it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it is not made equal: then take a needle in which there is a thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure; and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct; and join the threads between your fingers with equality.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation; and raise the skin on which the mark is made, as we have related.
Then join the two lips of the wound with sutures: and care for it until it is healed.
If however an abscess occurs at your incision, or your fissure: soothe that abscess with a cauterizing agent, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelids also, that its hairs are turned.
Therefore, you ought to use what we said about the incision: but the suture is more artificial for both. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with canes.
And it is, that you turn the eyelid, and cut the fissure which is within, according to what I have narrated. Then, skillfully make two canes, or two thin pieces of wood, whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width be less than the width of a spatula; and you have already incised at the extremities of each on both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully apply each to the excess of the eyelid, and tightly bind the two canes on both sides with a strong ligature, and leave it for several days. For the tightened skin will die, and turn black, and putrefy, until it falls off by itself.
If it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then treat it until it heals.
When it is consolidated, the eyelid is lifted; and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two canes, which you shall make according to this figure, and quantity in length and breadth.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the lifting of pricking hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation in it is that you shall take a slender needle, and introduce into it a slender, smooth thread of silk. Then gather its ends, and make a very fine knot on each side: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place under it, in a short while, another slender thread in the Alinxotati, and knot its ends if you wish.
Then let the sick person place his head in your bosom, and be close to the sun so that the operation is revealed; because it is a subtle or minute operation.
Then introduce the needle with the alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you draw it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up to the alinxotati: and introduce that hair into the alinxotati: if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it, until the hair emerges with the alinxotati into the eyelid with the natural hair.
However, if the alinxotati is pulled and the hair does not emerge with it: pull the alinxotati downwards, with the thread that you have placed on it, until the alinxotati emerges from the upper opening, and you see it with the vision of the eye: then pull the alinxotati, and the thread; and remove both, and close the eye; and leave it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either associate a long hair from the natural hairs with it, and it is lifted with it; or leave it, until it elongates after a time, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental, therefore, it arises from the consolidation of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and things similar to them.
And the method of operation in it is that you should find that consolidation, and separate its edges, and place in what is between them a linen cloth, and tie it, until it is healed.
And it is not necessary that you use in its treatment things which dry and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: indeed, use in it things which soften, such as fenugreek; and embrocate with water in which marshmallow, linseed has been cooked; and with the ointment dyaquilon, which has been liquefied with some of the oils; and wrap with it a linen cloth, and treat with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you should strive with all ingenuity, so that it is not consolidated according to the form it first had.
And this operation which we have described is only a certain rectification of the Alxatrati, so that the shape of the place does not return to what it entirely was.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs below, is the one truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental one arises from a wound, or a fissure, or a cautery, and things similar to those.
And the method of treatment in it is that you should take a needle, on which a double thread is fastened, and dip it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the flesh at both ends.
Then, stretch the flesh upward with the needle, and cut it with a broad spatula. If, therefore, the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take a probe, and place it in the fissure, and with it turn the eyelid: and make two fissures in the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and form an angle from them: until, when they are brought together, their shape becomes this shape, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that is its sharp lower side, from that which follows the eyelid. Then bring together the separated parts; sew it with a wool thread.
Then treat them with what we have said, about softening medicines, and compresses, until they are healed.
And if a laceration occurs from the fissure, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you make a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, even as before. Then separate between the two lips with a compress, as we said.
And the summary of treatment for Alxatrat, when it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed according to the form of the Alxatrat preparation.
For it has many diversities in form, and a prudent artisan prepares an ingenious method in any way possible to it, until it restores the figure to its natural form, or nearly so, and the weakened one itself, according to what is suitable for it from the operation and instruments in various arrangements.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation occurs in many people, so that the upper eyelid consolidates with the white of the eye and prevents it from its natural operations.
Therefore, you must introduce the tip of a probe under the eyelid, and lift it upwards, or extend it with a hook; then cut the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharp, but slightly blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and injures the eye with its sharpness; and your incision should be like scraping that adhesion away easily, until the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, washing it with the water; or distil it onto it from a sieve made from dissolved ziniar with water.
Then separate the eyelid and the eye with flax thread; and place wool soaked with egg white over the eye; and after three days use a sieve, and with consolidants, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the eminence of the tear ducts.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the nail is according to two modes. Or it is nervous: and it is similar to hard, fine sinew. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white moisture: which when it touches iron, or you try to grasp it with a hook, is cut off, and the hook does not fasten to it.
And both species do not begin, unless from the larger tear duct, until it gradually extends, until it covers the sight, and obstructs the light, and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operation in its incision is: that the sick person should place his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and raise the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a hook of a few folds, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from a horse's hair, or an ox's, or a cow's: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and pierce it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upwards: and scrape with the hair the side of the nail which follows the pupil, as if you were sawing it with the hair to its last part.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a thin spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural tear duct tissue, so that a continuous flow of tears does not occur from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone, or with a thread, as we said. Then scrape with a gentle spatula, whose form is this.
And be careful that the spatula does not touch the corneal membrane, and a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is pierced.
Then, after the incision, put some ground salt into the eye, or a paste made from ziniar, and leave it until the next day. Then care for it with what is necessary until it is healed.
If the nail is not nervous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is a hook fixed in it, then this type of nail is treated many times with medicines, and is healed as I have described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the patient's eye and take a fine, gentle, weakly pointed spatula, according to this form. And scrape the nail from above with delicacy. If you see that it becomes thin and breaks, and the scraping marks it, then distil from your hour glass into the eye a solution made from ziniar, or from red sief, or from crushed salt: and bandage the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation on it, until the entire thing is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in the eye; then leave it, and treat the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the operation on it with scraping, until it heals.
However, if the fleshy prominence of the tear duct causes a great injury to the eye, it should be suspended with a hook, and a portion of it cut away; and do not make the incision too deep, so as not to harm the tear ducts.
Then instill into the tear duct a red, or prepared, ziniari, until it heals.
Concerning the incision of Alguardenic, and the flesh growing in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in some people's eyes, red flesh grows, either composed or superimposed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or presses on the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles a rose-hip.
And the operation in it also is, that the sick person should place their head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks: and hold it with a single forceps, or tongs.
Then cut the flesh in the first place, until its whole is finished with a scalpel. And beware of the eye, so that you do not injure it during the operation: and let your incision, by which you cut it, be either with a lancet, as we described in the incision of the hoof; or with small scissors, with which the asabel is gathered, according to its form after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is completed, and the entire alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt; or distill into it red powder, and similar corrosive medicines; and place over the eye externally a cloth with egg white: so that it is protected from a hot abscess.
If anything of the alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess: then leave the eye, and treat it with that which calms the abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it, until it is healed.
And similarly do with the added flesh which occurs in the eye of this kind.
However, it is necessary that you avoid the treatment of every weak eye by natural or accidental means, operating with iron; and do not put your hand in any of these operations, until you see that the member is tolerant of that operation.
Concerning the collection of Sebel from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Sebel are red veins that are woven over the eye, and they impede the eye's operation of vision; and they weaken the eye with the length of days.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which the Sebel is present is strong, and there is no other illness in it besides the Sebel; then collect its Sebel.
And it is so that you should instruct the sick person to place his head in your bosom.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your prudence; and let the hook be a thin double hook, according to this form, or let there be two hooks graded on one body, according to this form.
Then collect those veins with fine scissors with precision, and wipe away the blood hour after hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye and dissolved; and beware of injuring the eye with the extremities of the forceps.
And let your operation be in the midday before the sun, and be very firm in your operation, so that you do not injure anything other than those veins.
And when you have finished, then instill into the eye a green or red sieve, so that its sharpness corrodes what remains of the scab.
However, if it is not possible for you to collect it all in that hour, then plaster the eye with that which soothes the pain of the eye, and leave it for a few days until its pain subsides and it is safe from a hot abscess; then again over it according to the same method until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps.
Concerning the repositioning of the fractured nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call the fistula Arissati, which you treat with a cautery, or with a burning sharp medicine, according to what its description preceded.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in it; unless you split open the abscess at its origin, and extract all the moisture and pus within it, until the bone is exposed.
When therefore the bone is exposed, and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument, whose shape is this.
And it is called the Asperi head.
Let it be made of tempered iron, and let its head be round like a *azir*, which should be pointed by filing or by a fine *aliskilferi* point, and place it upon the corrupted place of the bone.
Then revolve it between your fingers, and you press your hand for a short while, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away; and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying, astringent medicines.
Therefore, if the place consolidates, and flesh grows in it, and the flow of pus ceases; and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not become an abscess, and no mishap occurs in it; then you shall know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no remedy for it, except to reduce the fistula to the nostril opening, according to this form.
And it is, that you uncover the bone from the second with iron, or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone is uncovered, then take a drill according to this form, let its iron tip be triangular; and let its wood be a tapered pyramid down to the tip, as you see.
Then place it upon the bone itself; and let it be close to the tear duct; and move your hand a little away from the eye. Then revolve your hand with the drill until it penetrates the bone, and the infirm person feels it; by this, that he finds wind issuing from it when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with astringent drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh is born, and the place hardens, then the matter that was flowing outwards returns to the nose; therefore it is lighter for the infirm person.
Concerning the reduction of the prominence of the Eye.
Chapter XX.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety, and no harm or diminution occurs in vision, then you should release the patient; then bleed him; afterward, apply cupping to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then apply to the eye a liniment made of acacia, aloe, olibanum, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above with a plaster with many bandages.
And the method of the bandages is: that you take many soft cloths, and double each cloth over four; make many of them; then place them one over another, according to the amount the eye needs; then bind over them with a hard cloth with a strong binding.
And repeat the cupping with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping, and leave the eye bandaged day and night.
If however the bandage softens again, then it is necessary that it be bound tightly; then remove the bandage after a day and night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has now returned the uvea tunic, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster, and bandages, and binding, and cupping, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the uvea tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the uvea tunic, then it protrudes and proceeds outside the eyelids like a uvea grain, and disfigures the appearance of the person because of it, and one wishes to cut it: then the operation in it is as I describe: and it is to insert a needle into the root of the uvea from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle, on which a double thread is, from the tear duct side, and cause it to penetrate; and leave the first needle according to its position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the uvea in the circumference of the needle; and tighten it a lot.
Then withdraw the needle, and place on the eye wool soaked in the white of an egg; and leave it until the thread falls with the uvea.
Then treat the eye with that which strengthens it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the uvea is tightened from every side with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds great prominence.
Then it is pierced with a thin spatula, which reaches the depth of the eye, and the white moisture runs out, and the eye descends, and immediately enters; then tighten it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnat.
Chapter XXII.
This illness, called Alcomnati, is nothing but pus that collects in the eye, similar to descending water, and is not the eye itself.
And the method of operating on it is to make the infirm person sit on a prepared seat, or upright.
Then take its head in your hands at two sides, and move it, until you see the pus reach the lower parts of the eyes. Then let it be fixed, and not descend, and the light be released: and let the infirm person see things as they were seeing: and if it does not descend lower, know that it is water.
If, however, the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we have said, then make the infirm person sit between your hands. Then take a thin spatula, and cut with it on top of the horny membrane a little at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea, at the crown, until pus comes out.
When it comes out, then instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey; then treat with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Of the treatment of water that descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already discussed the types of water in division, and which is suitable for perforation, the speech stating and explaining it; then take that same one according to health.
Then it is necessary that you make the sick person sit between your hands, square, opposite the light, near the sun; and bind his healthy eye, and tie it tightly; then, with your right hand, if the eye in which there is water is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right. press the eyelid of his eye.
Then take with your right hand an almagda, if the eye is the left; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then place the extremity of the almagda near the crown, by the thickness of a ray, on the apparent white of the eye, of the smaller tear duct; then push the almagda with force; and you revolve your hand with it, until it penetrates the white of the eye; and you feel that the almagda has already reached the empty thing.
And it is necessary that the measure of the almagda's journey to the depth be the measure of the space that is from the pupil to the end of the blackness; and it is the crown of the eye. For you will see them in the pupil itself of the eye's vision, because of the clarity of the cornea.
Then place the almagda up to the place where the water is. Then press it below, one time after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that upon which he opens his sight immediately, and the almagda in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then put it down a second time, apart from which the almagda emerges.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend: then extract the almagda with ease: and you turn your hand gradually.
Then dissolve some of clear gem-salt in water, and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then place a whole piece of lint, or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white, outside the eye, and bind the healthy eye with it.
We, and those who are of our time, place crushed cumin with egg white on it.
If the almagda does not obey you in entering the eye because of its hardness, since it is from humans whose eye is very hard, then it is necessary to take a spatula, which is called Alberid, of which this is the form.
Therefore, scrape with it only the conjunctiva itself, and do not put your hand into the opening; and there is nothing other than to make a subtle path to the almagda's place.
Then introduce the almagda, according to what we said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare for the sick person a properly made sleeping place, in which he sleeps on his back in a dark house; and forbid him all movements and coughing; and place his food that which soothes his nature; and let him not move his head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then loosen it in that dark house, and test his vision; and make him see things. Then reapply the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that he do it at the time of the treatment, or immediately after the incision; rather, it is fitting that he postpone it; because water ascends quickly due to a forceful look.
However, if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary that you loosen the eye before the seventh day; and treat it with that which soothes the abscess, until it is soothed; then, free its vision, and place over his face an oral [something] by which his vision is governed under those days, while he is in that dark house.
Then let him emerge from the house gradually, and let him mingle in his own houses.
And know that in zaltea a student is not excused from vision on any occasion; then, let him dare on the operation.
And indeed, there has come to us someone from those who are of Alayrach, who said that a perforated magdan was made in Alayrach, from which water is drawn.
However, I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that; nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of species from the magdan, so that he who is ignorant of them may stand upon it.
Let them be made properly of bronze; and let their extremities be triangular with this same subtlety, and their extremity pointed.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
Concerning the care of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes diverse growths of flesh are born in the nose, among which is something resembling a scorpion with many feet; and from it is cancerous, stony flesh, of a dark color; and from it is flesh that is soft, not of a dark color.
Therefore, in the soft flesh from among these growths, which is not deceitful, nor cancerous, you should have the patient sit between your hands facing the ground, and open his nostrils; then introduce a hook into the flesh; next, draw it outward, and cut off what you have grasped with a fine, sharp spatule, on one side, until you know that the entire growth has been removed.
If anything remains of it that cannot be removed, then scrape it with one of the fine instruments with ease, until nothing of it remains. However, if blood dominates you, or a hot abscess occurs; then apply to it what is appropriate; or if it is from malignant abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it, until the blood is removed and all the flesh is taken away.
Then, after the incision, vinegar, and water, or wine, should be applied to the nose. If however, the nose opens, and moisture flows from it to the palate, then know that it is now healthy.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that within it is flesh grown on the upper nasal bones, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then it is necessary to insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots in it; and place between each knot a quantity of a finger, or less; and let the sick man, with a rod, or with whatever is possible for him after having made it similar to a saw, place one end of the thread into his nose; and pull out his offensive matter, until it reaches the bottom, and emerges above his throat.
And many times indeed, children in schools do a thing similar to this; and it is an easy thing for him who wishes to do so.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end that emerges above the nose, and the other that remains in the nose. Then remove the flesh shavings with the knots that are on the thread. Do that until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread, and place in the nose, after washing away the blood, a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or longer, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then place at the end of the thing in the nose a lead cannula, for some days, until it heals.
If however it needs a treatment that will dry it out, use that.
For making nasal douches, with which oils and other medicines are instilled into the nose, make it of silver, or bronze; that it be similar to a small lamp, open like a seashell; and its passage be likewise.
And if you wish to make a closed cannula, like a tube.
And the seashell instrument for nasal douching should be full, uncovered; on whose back end there should be a handle, with which you hold it, when heating oil, or whatever you wish of juices, and runny substances, in it.
Chapter XXV.
Often a wart is born at the tip of the nose, and it grows larger and larger with the days, until its appearance becomes unsightly.
And therefore it is necessary, that one cut it off at the beginning of its appearance, and remove the entire thing. Then place on the area either a cautery or a burning medicine in its place.
If, therefore, its excision is delayed until it grows, then consider, if it is stony, hard, of dark color, and with little sensitivity: then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess.
For often I have seen that when someone incised such an abscess, great harm resulted for the one who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that incision is possible throughout its extent, then evacuate the pus and cut it without fear, without hesitation: and treat the area with something that dries and contracts it, until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when a separation of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, little benefit results from any operation, unless in certain individuals.
Therefore, when something of that sort happens to someone, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood: and then bring together the edges of the wound with sutures; then treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of the separation of continuity are already separated, and each part is sound: then you ought to scrape away all the part from its apparent skin until it bleeds. Then bring together the two lips with sutures, and bind them tightly, and powder over it the blood of the dragon, and powdered frankincense: and place upon the powders a piece of a diafinicon plaster, and some other of the consolidating plasters; and leave it bound for two days, or three. Then loosen it, and change the medicine; and leave it until the threads are severed by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suturing is, that you bring together the separation of continuity, either with needles, as we have narrated in the suturing of the belly; or bring the separation together with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
Concerning the extraction of lip nodules.
Many people frequently have hard abscesses like grains of grass in the interior of their lips: and some of them are smaller, and some larger.
Therefore, it is necessary to turn back the lip and cut over every nodule on it, and to suspend it with a hook and cut it from every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with pounded zegi until the blood ceases.
Then wash it with vinegar and treat the place, where there is astringency, with it until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Concerning the incision of fleshy tissue grown over the gum.
Often flesh grows over the gum, which the ancients call abuluss.
Therefore, it is necessary to suspend it with a hook; or to hold it with forceps, and to cut it at its root, and to allow the pus, or blood, to run.
Then apply to the place ground zegi, or other astringent, drying powders.
If that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does, then cut it a second time, and cauterize it; for it does not return after the cautery.
Chapter XXIX.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with an iron.
Sometimes rough, foul, and sometimes blackened, or yellowish, or greenish, hard masses gather on the surfaces of the teeth from the inner and outer sides, and between the gums, until corruption from them reaches the gums, and the teeth become unsightly because of it.
Therefore, you should have the sick person sit between your hands, with their head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth, where the hard masses, like sand, appear, until nothing remains of them, and similarly deal with the blackness, greenness, and yellowness, and other such things, until they are cleansed.
If therefore that which is in them is removed at the beginning of the scraping, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another day, a second time, and a third time, until finally you attain the desired result.
And know that a tooth requires scrapers of various shapes and forms, according to what the preparation of your operation requires; therefore, the scraper with which the tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the scraper used for scraping between the teeth, according to different shapes.
And these shapes of scrapers should be prepared by you, which you see.
Chapter XXX.
Concerning the extraction of toothache.
You should treat the tooth from its pain with all your skill, and be slow in its eradication. For there is nothing to replace it when it is pulled out, since it is a superior substance. Until when there is no excuse for its extraction, then it is necessary when the sick person is forced to pull it out, that it be strengthened, and that you, the tooth, aching tooth, be assured.
For often pain deceives the sick person, and he thinks that it is in the tooth itself and pulls it out.  Then the pain does not recede until the sick person's tooth is pulled out.
For we have already seen that from the operation of phlebotomists.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is assured, you should make incisions around the tooth with a lancet, in which there is something of virtue, until the gum is loosened on all sides.  Then move it with your fingers, or with delicate forceps, at first gently, until you break it.
Then place large forceps in a good position on it, and hold the patient's head between your knees. What you have now firmly held, so that it does not move. Then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under it from every side with ease, and try to move it, as you did at first.
If, however, the tooth is perforated or decayed, then you should fill that hole with cloth and bind it with the end of a fine reed, so that it does not break at the time of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary to carefully make incisions around the gum on every side very much.
And maintain your strength so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns to the patient from that evil which is greater pain than his first pain.
And beware that you do not do that which ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste and boldness in their eradication of it, beyond what we have described.
For they often cause great illnesses in men, which are more difficult to break, and the roots of it all, or some of them, remain; or to extract it with some of the jawbones, as we have often witnessed.
Then it should be rinsed after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If a flow of blood occurs from the places (for it often does), then take some of the zegi and fill the place with it; and if not, cauterize it if the zegi does not help you.
The shapes of the fine forceps with which the tooth is first moved should have long extremities, a short handle, and be thick, so that they do not double when pressed against the tooth; they should be like the handles of thick things, until, when you tighten them, they do not yield or double: with short extremities, and they should be made of strong iron or good steel, with straight extremities; and on their extremities should be teeth, some of which enter into others, and bind with a proper, strong tightening.
And sometimes the extremities are shaped like the mouth of a bird called a stork, and have a strong grip.
Chapter XXXI.
On the removal of teeth, and the extraction of fractured mandibular bones.
When, during the extraction of a tooth, something is left behind, you should place a poultice of butter on the area for one or two days, until the area softens.  Then, introduce algasti, or forceps, whose extremities are shaped like the beak of a stork. The form of these forceps is...
Let their ends be made like a file inside, or like an aliskilfegi.
If therefore it does not yield to you for extraction with these forceps, then it is necessary that you carefully work around the root, and expose all the flesh, and insert the instrument called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let it have a short, somewhat thick end, and let it not be soaked, so that it does not break.
If therefore the root emerges through it, it is good; and if not, it is necessary to assist with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first is triangular with a certain thickness at the end.
The form of a triangular, slender end.
And with other instruments and tools that have preceded in the reasoning about teeth.
Also, there is help with this instrument that is like a large hook, of this form, with a twisted triangular end, where there is something of thickness so it does not break, and it is not soaked.
And know that instruments for teeth are many, and similarly the other instruments are not counted, and a skillful craftsman, subtle in his art, finds for himself the instrument according to which the operation and the illnesses themselves signify to him.
Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not say an instrument, due to the diversity of their species.
If, however, a bone breaks from the jaw, or from other bones of the mouth, or putrefies, then probe over it in its place with what suits it from the instruments and forceps that I mentioned in the extraction of roots, and assist with a pick, whose form is this.
Let there be something of thickness gradually, so that the bone is tightened or held with it, and does not flee and go, until the bone emerges; and restore the place with the appropriate medicines for it.
If however there is a small putrefaction in the bone, then scrape it from its putrefaction, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Of the sawing of prominent teeth above others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, an ugly form is produced by it; and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely those of easy virtue.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. For if a tooth has grown after another tooth and does not have its place, or its sawing or filing is not possible, then extract it; and if it is joined to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, of which this is the form.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made from hard iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision, by which you cut it, be over many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another tooth is not moved.
If however it is prominent, exposed to its filing, then file it with a file of hard iron, whose form is this.
Let the whole be of hard iron, and its handle with very small points, like the file with which needles are made; with which the tooth is filed gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved and falls out; then gently smooth it, and at the end shave it with some razors.
If a tooth has a part that has broken off and hurts the tongue, then it is necessary to file it, until the roughness of that fracture is removed and it is made even, and flattened, and does not hurt the tongue, nor spoil speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Concerning the retention of teeth with gold threads.
When the front teeth are knocked and moved by a blow or fall, and the weak person cannot bite on what they eat so that it does not fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and the treatment was not successful, then a way exists to bind them with gold thread, because silver becomes green and rots after days, but gold never changes.
And let the thread be medium in thinness and thickness according to the amount that it will take between the teeth.
And the method of retention is to take a thread, and insert double its length between two healthy teeth; then weave the two ends of the thread between the moved teeth, whether one or more, until you reach with the weave to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving back to the place where you began: and bind your hand with delicacy and skill, until it is completely fixed; and let your binding of the thread be near the roots of the teeth, so it does not come loose.
Then cut off the excess ends of the thread with scissors; and join both together, and twist them with a twist; and hide them between the healthy tooth and the moved tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue; then let it be left thus fastened as it remains.
If however it comes loose, or is cut, bind them with another thread, and it will hold with it thus for the entire time.
And this is the method for teeth, and the manner of retention of two healthy teeth, and two moved teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that a single tooth, or two, return after falling into their places, and are retained as we have described, and remain; and an artful, prudent, and experienced person does not accomplish this.
And sometimes a suitable piece of bone is fitted; and it is made in the shape of a tooth, and placed in the place from which the tooth is strengthened and bound, as we said; and it remains, and serves with it for a long time.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Sometimes this ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a man is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the mouth of the sick person on his head in your lap, and raise his tongue; then cut that nervous ligament along its width, until the tongue is released from its retention.
If there is something of hardness and knotting in it, and it is from the consolidation of a wound: then insert a hook into it, and split it along its width, until you cut the ligament, and the knotting is loosened.
And beware, lest there be a fissure deep in the flesh, and you cut an artery there, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then it should be rinsed at the site of the incision with rose water, or with vinegar, or with cold water.
Then place under the tongue a linen bandage, which the sick person should hold all night: so that it is not consolidated again.
If however a flow of blood occurs, then place pounded zege over the spot. If, however, blood overcomes you, then cauterize the place with a lentiginous cautery, suitable for it; then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes a boil occurs under the tongue, resembling a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and perhaps it grows so large that it fills the mouth.
And the operation in it is to open the mouth of the sick person before the sun, and consider the boil.
If therefore you see it to be of dark color, or black, hard, to which the sick person has no feeling: then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is inclined towards whiteness, in which there is moisture, then thrust a hook into it, and cut it with a fine scalpel, and free it from every side. If blood overcomes you during your operation, then place on it pounded zegi until the blood ceases; then return to your operation until you extract it with its entirety.
Then wash it with vinegar and salt, and treat it with the remaining appropriate treatment for it, until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes there occur between the throat, that is, glands, which are similar to almonds, that occur externally; and are named two tonsils: which when you treat with what we said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, of a dark color, with little feeling, then do not touch it with an iron.
And if it is of a reddish color, and its root is thick, then do not touch it even with an iron, fearing a flow of blood, but rather leave it until it ripens. And then either you will pierce it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is of a white color, round, and its root is thin, then it is necessary to cut it.
And the operation on it is that you should consider, before its operation, if its abscess has rested and completely calmed down, or is diminishing with some reduction: then, therefore, make the sick person sit in front of the sun, and let his head be in your lap; and open his mouth, and let the servant take in their hands, and press his tongue downwards with an instrument, whose shape is this.
Let it be made of silver, or bronze, which is as thin as a knife.
Therefore, when you press the tongue with it, and the abscess is revealed to you, and your vision falls upon it, then take a hook, and fix it into the tonsil, and pull it outwards as far as you can, besides which, you should pull out with it something from the coverings.
Then cut it with an instrument, whose shape follows this. It resembles scissors; however, its ends should be curved, so that the opening of each is in line with the other's sharp point: therefore, take care that it is made of tempered iron, or soaked in alfuled.
If, however, this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, whose shape is this. One side should be sharp, the other side should not be sharp.
And after you cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same method of incision.
Then, after the incision, let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs, let him gargle with water in which the peels of pomegranates have been boiled: or with myrtle leaves, and things similar to them that are astringent, until the flow of blood is stopped: then treat him until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore, cut it according to what I said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And I myself have already treated a woman from an abscess that had arisen between her throat, which was drawing towards blackness, with few sensations, which had nearly closed her throat: and the woman was breathing from a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her food and water: and she had already come to death if she had remained for one or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent out two branches, until they had emerged above the two nostrils.
Therefore I quickly hastened and fastened a hook to one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion came away from it.
Then I cut it off where I grasped it from the nostril.
Then I did the same to that which emerged from the other nostril.
