Then I opened its mouth, and pressed its tongue; and afterwards I fastened a hook into the abscess; then I cut a portion from it, and only a little blood flowed from it; therefore the woman's throat was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, then took food.
Therefore I did not cease to cut from that abscess many times for a long time, and the abscess succeeded in the place where I cut, until it was prolonged too much and with it that.
Therefore I devised and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
Therefore it stood apart; then I walked away from her, and I did not know what God did with her after me.
Concerning the incision of an abscess of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula, and it becomes an abscess, and it is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help; and you see that the hot abscess has quieted from it, and it is thin; then it is necessary to incise it.
And whatever is collected from it, round, and has no length, and is blood-red, or dark-brown, or black, and has no feeling: then it is necessary to avoid its incision. For in it is fear upon the infirm.
Therefore, when you see it according to the way I have described, from its whiteness and length, have the infirm sit before the sun; and press his tongue with the instrument, of which the account has preceded; then fasten a hook into the uvula, and pull it downwards; and incise it with one of the two instruments which I have said concerning the incision of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary not to incise from it, except that which is added beyond the natural limit without addition.
For if you incise from it more, you will injure the voice and speech.
Then, after the incision, use that which we have described in the incision of the two tonsils, and care for it until it is healed.
However, if the infirm is frightened by the incision, then it is necessary to use ingenuity in cauterizing it without fear and terror.
And the mode of the cautery on it is not, except with sharp water, and it is, that you place the head of the infirm in your bosom. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we have mentioned. Then take from the water I spoke of to you in the chapter on cauterization, and make with it lime not extinguished, and place it neither thick nor thin, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, whose form is this.
Let the concavity in which the medicine is placed be like the concavity of a spoon's radius.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon the uvula itself: and let the sick man lie on his side, that the saliva may run from his mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing may descend from it to his throat, and hurt him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you shall keep it on the uvula for a quantity of half an hour, until you see it already blackened, and the biting of the medicine quieted.
And if you wish to take a clot, and wrap it around the extremity of the radius, and infuse the clot with the medicine, and introduce the radius with the clot into the cannula from above, until the clot adheres to the uvula.
Do this many times, until you achieve what you desire concerning the cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it; for it will dry and fall off after three or four days.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with a clot soaked in butter, and cleanse with it what is around it from the medicine; and rinse with cold water.
And treat it externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
This method of treatment is therefore healthier and further from danger than incision.
And sometimes the uvula is also treated with something more delicate than cautery and incision: for it is treated with fumigation, according to this method. Take calamus, hyssop, oregano, rue, sseah, chamomile, and wormwood, and similar herbs to them: and gather them all, or some of them, in a pot, and immerse them in vinegar, and boil them: and let the pot be smeared with clay properly: and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which a concave instrument is to be placed in this manner.
Let it be made of silver or bronze: and let the end, in which there is a pomegranate, be inserted into the sick person's mouth, until the vapor ascends to the uvula over the cannula, until the uvula becomes very dark.
Then repeat over it many times, until it dries.
And beware, do not perform this treatment at the beginning of the abscess's onset. For it usually adds to the abscess: and you should not do it unless at the decline of its hot abscess.
If however this instrument is not present for you, then take a reed and place the rind of an egg at its end, so that the sick person's mouth is not burned; because the egg rind prevents the vapor from burning the mouth: and this is from good treatment with health.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Often a bone, or a fishbone, or something else, adheres in the throat.
Therefore, it is necessary to extract whatever of them is apparent upon which the sight falls, after pressing the tongue with an instrument near the sun, and it is declared to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary to make the vomiting weak before the food is digested in the stomach. For perhaps the attached thing will come out through vomiting.
Or let the weak person swallow a piece of turnip, or a root of lettuce, or swallow a piece of dry bread; or let him take a piece of soft dry sea sponge, and tie it to a thread, then let him swallow it. When therefore it reaches the place where there are spines, or bone, and it is extracted, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be thicker than a Radius a little, and at its extremity let there be a bend, which he should introduce into his throat with ease; and let him have his head elevated upwards.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis, lest coughing should befall him, and push with it the bone, or spine, or let the physician introduce it with his hand.
But that which the sick person introduces is better, on account of his knowledge of the place of the thing attached; and let him push downwards, or pull his hand with the instrument upwards, the whole of it according to the quantity which is prepared for him, until it comes out.
Concerning the extraction of the Leech.
Chapter XXXIX.
When a leech is medicated with what I have said in the division concerning treatment with medicines, and it does not help, then consider in the throat of the sick person in the presence of the sun, or in the sun, after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument which I have described to you.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with fine forceps skillfully.
If it is not possible with them, then take a hollow cannula, and introduce it into the throat of the sick person up near the throat; then introduce into the concavity of the cannula a heated iron with fire. Do this many times; and let the sick person abstain from water the whole day.
Then let him take a vessel full of cold water, and open his mouth in it; and let him rinse his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it; and let him move the water one hour after another with his hand. For the leech immediately, when it feels the water, falls.
If however it does not emerge with what we have described, then fumigate the sick person with fleas, or with asa, with the instrument that I have described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do that many times, since it will fall itself.
And the method of operation in fumigation is, that you take a pot in which are live coals with fire; and the pot be covered with a cover, in the middle of which be a hole; and the extremity of the instrument be placed in that hole.
Then, direct the fumigation, and let the sick person place his mouth on the extremity of the cannula, and close his mouth, so that the fumigation does not escape, until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech will fall immediately.
If it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the sick person tolerate thirst, and eat salt and garlic, and not drink water.
Therefore, it is necessary that it depart with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat, when it falls upon it visibly.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature that is inserted into the throat. And its tip is like the beak of a bird called a stork, in which there is the roughness of a file: when it grasps something, it does not let it go completely.
Concerning the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and their general care.
Chapter XL.
Abscesses have many species, of many kinds, according to the recollection of them, that is, one, and one in this book: and they are diverse in their perforation and incision by two methods.
One of which is from the species of an abscess in itself, and in that which it comprises from the humors.
And the second method is because of the locations in which it occurs in the body.
Because an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy location is different from an abscess occurring in the joints; and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And among abscesses are some that should not be pierced, unless after the maturation of the pus in them, and its completion; and of them are some that should be pierced while they are raw, and do not mature according to completion.
Such as abscesses that are near the joints.
Because an abscess, when it occurs near a joint and prolongs its being until that which is around it decays, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: therefore, it is a closer cause of contraction of that member.
Or it is an abscess near a principal member: because if you delay its incision until it matures, it will harm either that or the principal member through it.
Or it is near the anus, therefore pierce it unripe; and it is not fitting to pierce a raw abscess, not of complete digestion, except that which is near the anus, so that it does not decay deeply, and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or become in its end that which does not heal.
It is necessary that you know the hour of piercing the abscess, which has matured according to completion: and it is in the calming of the pain, the recession of the fever, and the diminution of redness, and pulsation, and sharpness of the head of the abscess: and the other signs.
And it is necessary that you make the same incision in the lower place of the abscess, and of greater prominence: and let the incision be proceeding in the length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and in places of tendons, and cords, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflection.
In those, however, which are reflected, proceed with the incision according to that member.
When, however, the abscess is in fleshy places: then it is better that you leave its incision, until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to completion.
Because if you incise it before that, the course of the virus from it is prolonged, and there is much pus and filth, and perhaps its lips are hardened, and its depth.
And some abscesses are incised across the body's breadth when necessary, or according to the member it needs.
And it is necessary that you administer a small incision in small abscesses, and a large incision in large abscesses, or many incisions similar to the size of the abscess.
And there are abscesses in which the skin must be hollowed out, and incised when it has already become like a cloth, and reached its limit, that it is already dead, like that which happens in many boils and buboes.
And of them there are some which are incised with an incision having three angles. And of them there is one which is incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like the groin abscess.
And of them there are some in which a round incision, and a crescent-shaped incision, and similar incisions are administered.
That which does not have a head, like mild superficial abscesses, should be perforated with a superficial or merely flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large, and has already collected much pus, and you perforate it, you should not be hasty and extract all the pus at that time; rather, extract a part of it. Then, close the abscess until another day. Then extract some more pus again.
Do this repeatedly according to the degree, until it comes out; especially if the sick person is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an old decrepit person: for the animal spirit of the animal is repeatedly resolved with the discharge of the matter, and perhaps the sick person dies, and you do not perceive.
Therefore, beware of this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, with which you perforate these abscesses, it is necessary that you cleanse the wound: and consider. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then apply bandages of linen, or old cloth. And if the abscess is large, and the perforations' fissures are many, then it is necessary that you introduce a bandage into each fissure, until they meet each other.
And if in the abscess you have removed a piece of skin, or hollowed it out in a circle: then it is necessary that you fill it with old cloth, or as when a cloth is woven, or shaved with flax fibers without moisture: and bind it tight for up to three days.
Then remove it, and treat it with what is necessary of ointment, until it is healed.
If however, bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after which a cloth is soaked in them; and place it repeatedly over the site of the bleeding.
If however the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer the regimen and treatment and powders, which I have described in many places of this book of ours, and in its division.
If however it is in wintertime, and the site of the abscess is near many nerves, then it is necessary that you apply heated pads of wine and oil and place them over the affected areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then it is necessary that you place pads soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and all of it cool. Until when it will be the third day, as we have said, then it is necessary that you open the abscess, and cleanse it, and administer in its treatment that which is suitable for it, until it is healed.
This therefore is what is necessary from the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally.
Following the path of distinction, however, I have already spoken of every abscess, how there is a way to cure it, itemized and individually.
Chapter XLI.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur on the scalp.
Small abscesses occur on the scalp, and they are of the type of bumps, which are contained by the tissues that are like sacks to them; as if they were the sacs of a hen.
And their types are many.
Some of them are oily; and some of them contain moisture like mud; and some of them contain moisture like gruels and porridges; and similar to them, and some of them are hard, stony.
And there is no fear in the incision of all of them, and their extraction, as long as the incision does not encounter an artery.
And the operation in their incision is, that you should test them with an instrument, whose form will come after this, which is called an Intromissum, until you know what they contain.
For if it is that which contains moisture, then cut it with a superficial longitudinal incision, according to this form.
And begin with the incision from line b to line g.  When the moisture is evacuated, then scrape the cyst which contains that moisture, and do not leave anything of it at all.
For it often returns if anything remains of it.
Then immerse the clot in Egyptian ointment, if it is present; and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it, and leave it until the next day. For it will corrode what remained of the cyst.
Then apply the Egyptian ointment again over the clot, a second and third time, and if you need it, until you are confident that nothing remains of the cyst, for then treat the wound with the ointment until it is healed.
If the abscess contains a fatty tumor, then cut it according to this shape, the incision having the form of a cross; and insert hooks between the two sides of the incision, and scrape it from every side; and try your skill in removing the membrane that contains it.
If however the artery resists you, then do what I will tell you later.
And similarly do in the abscess, if it is stony, by the same incision and treatment, according to what we said.
And the incision upon a stony abscess is easier, because there is little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are in the head in some people, in which there is absolutely no moisture.
That is: because I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman, and found the abscess like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken; and if anyone struck it with something in order to throw it, it wounded them.
In other abscesses occurring in the head, however, apart from these abscesses which occur in the heads of children, and near the roots of the ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial incision.
Or make a perforation of them always from their lower part, so that the course of pus is relieved downwards.
Then treat it with whatever is appropriate for them from the treatment.
Concerning the incision of the Strophulae in the neck.
Chapter XLII.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groins: and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And those that occur in the neck from them, are one, and many: and are generated from each other: and every Strophula from them is inside its own proper tunic, as is in the knots and abscesses of the head, as we have related.
And the types of these Strophulae are many. And from them are stony ones; and from them are those that contain moisture; and from them are malignant ones, which do not obey treatment.
In that, therefore, which of them you see well-disposed to the touch, whose proximity to the skin color is manifest, and it moves to every part, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the guidegi, nor to the artery, nor is deep: it is necessary to separate it by a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body from line, b, to line, g, and to scrape it from every side: and extend the two lips of skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need it, as we said in the abscesses of the head, and gradually extract it.
And it is a precaution that no vein or nerve be cut.
And let the scalpel not be very sharp, so that it does not add to your hand with the incision, or trouble the sick person, and cut what is not necessary.
If, however, you cut a vein or artery, or it hinders you from the operation, then put crushed zegi into the wound, or certain powders that cut off blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the abscess subsides and the wound softens, or it begins and wants to putrefy.
For the blood is cut off then. Return therefore to your operation, until you complete it.
Then investigate with your index finger if anything remains of the other small strophes; therefore cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the strophe, then it is necessary not to cut that strophe from its root; rather, it is necessary to tie it with a strong doubled thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old cloth, which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then care for it.
If the entire strophe is cut, then it is necessary to bring together the two edges of the wound, and suture it immediately, after you see that there is no more excess.
If, however, you see that excess remains because of the size of the strophe, then it is necessary to aim to cut its root, and to remove it; and administer the suture that we have described.
And whatever humours the strophes contain, pierce it with a superficial puncture, so that the place of its maturity appears to you; and place the puncture from that which follows below the body, as we said.
Then, after the puncture, apply strips with ointment, and things like them, to corrode what remains of the corruption, until when you see the wound already cleansed; then care for it with a generating flesh ointment, until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
Concerning the incision of the epiglottis from an abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients said that this incision into the epiglottis should be made, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has performed it; and this is the writing of their saying.
However, in those suffering from synocha, the incision of the epiglottis should be avoided; because no benefit is gained by it; for all the veins and lungs are weak.
However, in those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no illness in the passage, it is necessary to use an incision of the epiglottis, to avoid the harm that results from suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do this, we must cut the epiglottis beneath three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision across the width, at the point between two circles, so that the incision is on the membrane, not on the cartilage.  This place is indeed suitable for incision, because it is free of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it.
If the surgeon is timid, then it is necessary to stretch the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he should split the skin until he reaches the passage; he should avoid blood vessels, if he sees any of them.
Then he should cut the membrane that we described; and it is signified by the incision of the passage by the phlegm that emerges from it, together with the rupture and the removal of the voice.
And leave the wound open for a time.
When therefore the hour in which suffocation is feared is removed, bring together the two lips of the wound of the skin, and sew it itself alone without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow, until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. The summary of this account we have given is not an exposition unless they see that some of these abscesses have already closed the throat of the sick person: and the sick person is raised above death, and his breathing wants to be cut off: they go to cut the epiglottis, so that the sick person may breathe over the place of the wound with a certain breath, and be saved from death: and for this reason they prescribed to leave the wound open until the malice of the illness is consumed, and its malice is three days, and like itself: then therefore they prescribed to sew up the wound, and care for it, until it is healed.
And that which I saw by myself is that a certain maid took a knife, and thrust it into her throat, and cut a portion of the lung passage with it.
And I was called to treat her, and I found her groaning, like the groan of a dead person.
Therefore I examined the wound, and found little blood that had come out of the wound. Therefore I was confident that she had not cut a vein, nor a ligament; and wind was escaping from the wound.
I therefore hastened, and treated her wound, and cared for it until it was healthy; and nothing happened to the maid, except hoarseness in her voice; and she returned after days to her better condition.
Therefore I say: that there is no fear in the section of the epiglottis.
Concerning the section of the hernia of the throat.
Chapter XLIV.
This abscess, called hernia of the throat, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body; and it is very common in women.
And it is in two ways: either it is natural, or it is accidental.
In the natural one, certainly, there is no talent.
The accidental one, however, is in two ways: one of which is similar to fatty tumors. And the other way is similar to an abscess that arises from the knotting of an artery. And in it there is fear, and it is not appropriate to presume to use iron upon it unless it is small; and you should probe it and investigate it with an instrument. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty tumor, and it is not connected to any blood vessels, then cut it, as tumors are cut; and extract it with whatever it contains from the cyst, if it is in a cyst; and if not, then thoroughly explore its entirety. Then treat the area with whatever is appropriate for healing until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
Concerning the cutting of various types of tumors.
There are many kinds of tumors, and I have already described all their types in the divisions; and it is necessary for me to describe here the difference between a tumor and a boil, since it is a source of doubt.
Therefore, I say that when a boil is present, there is heat, fever, and intense pain until the ebullition of the excess fluid subsides and the putrefaction is complete; then the fever and heat cease.
However, with a tumor there is no heat, fever, or pain, and it contains a sack-like cyst, which is its own pouch, and is similar in color to the body; and its beginning is like a chickpea and it becomes like a batecha, and larger or smaller.
And they are of two types: either fatty, or containing moisture. And the colors of moisture are many, according to what I said in the division.
Therefore, when you arrive at the treatment of a tumor, you should examine it, and inquire into its form first, with the instrument called the *Intromissum*, in the way its form will be shown in the chapter after this one.
And the method of investigating abscesses and all tumors is that you should take this instrument, and insert it into the moist place you find in the abscess; and you should turn your fingers with it gradually, until you know that the instrument has now penetrated the skin. Then place your hand at the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the *Intromissum* and consider that which emerges in its wake.
If, therefore, running moisture, of whatever color, emerges, then cut it with a superficial incision, as I have said in the other abscesses.
And if no moisture emerges in the wake of the *Intromissum*, then know that it is fatty; therefore, cut over it with a cross-shaped incision, according to this shape, as I taught you; and suspend it with hooks, and peel the skin from all sides with ease; and if you can, keep a *kistin* over it to extract the healthy one with the tumor.
If, however, the *kistin* breaks during the operation, and it is not possible to extract the healthy one—for this often happens—then extract it piece by piece until nothing remains of it.
For if anything small or large remains of it, the tumor returns most often.
If it defeats you, and a small amount remains, then fill the wound at your completion with certain sharp corrosive powders; and constrict the wound, and place over it what sits on a hot abscess, and treat it with the remaining treatment, until it heals.
If, therefore, the tumor is large, then sew its two lips together, and treat it with what causes healing.
If, however, there is a pulsating or non-pulsating vein there, then hurry, and fill the place with ground zegi, and leave it constricted for two or three days, until the wound becomes putrid, and the boiling of the blood ceases; then return to cutting what remained of the tumor.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning the instruments for incision and perforation.
Among them are the forms of instruments of insertion, and they are of three kinds.
For among them is a large one, and among them is a medium one, and among them is a small one.
Let them be made of tempered iron, with square ends, suitably, so that they may quickly enter the abscess.
And these are the forms of instruments, and no, that is, piercing instruments: and one of them is called a *beris*, that is, a piercer: and they are of three kinds.
For among them is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating abscesses, and exits, and fistulas, and buboes, from that which is between them from the bones.
And others are made round, smooth, and gentle, like large needles with which saddles are sewn, namely of citron, or of bronze, or of iron, or of silver.
And the best of them are those made of citron.
And experiments are also made with black lead, which are suitable for experiencing fistulas, whose concavity has reflections, so that they may be reflected with their gentleness with those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. Because some of them are long, some of them are medium, and some of them are short, according to the quantity of the fistula's breadth and its constriction.
The shape of hooks, and they are of many kinds.
Because some of them are simple, namely those which have one curvature: and they are of three kinds as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And of them are blind hooks: and they are of three kinds.
And of them are hooks having two curvatures, and they are of three kinds: and of them are hooks having three curvatures, and they are of three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the shapes of scalpels, with which nodes and abscesses are cut and scraped: and they are of three kinds: because of them there is a large, medium, and small one.
Their cutting edges are sharp, other edges are not sharp: and they are not placed thus, unless that with them help be given in scraping nodes, for fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and so that the sick person may be healed by them and find a little peace from the burning which he finds in the scraping of an abscess.
They are made of air, and are similar to the radio with which alcohol is made: and at the broad end is hidden the point of a spatula: and on it runs to the interior, and to the exterior when you wish, as you see.
The shapes of spatulas that are hidden between the fingers when puncturing abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
The forms of cupping glasses with which the flow of blood is cut off: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
They are made of air, or of amber, round, a little in length, as you see: and be of a fine delicacy.
And it is necessary that these kinds of cupping glasses be large and small with you, so that you may cut off blood with speed in case of need, and then when there is no medicine present for you.
However, do not use them for the cutting of blood in every place of the body, and use them only on fleshy parts: such as the calves of the leg, and the thighs, and the arms, and the breasts, and the abdomen, and the groins, and similar fleshy, moist parts.
And sometimes small instruments like those made of hazelnut shells, in their shape, are made from them; with which blood is cut off when it flows from a place of phlebotomy, or at the incision of a vein or artery; and sometimes they are made round, in this form.
Chapter XLVII.
Of the care of the breasts of men which resemble the breasts of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men become swollen at the time of their sexual arousal in sleep (that is, when they begin to pollute themselves in sleep) until they resemble the breasts of women: and they remain foul-looking abscesses.
Therefore, whoever abhors that, should cut above the breasts with a crescent-shaped incision, according to this form, from line b to line g. Then scrape off all the fat. Thereafter, fill the wound with a flesh-forming medicine. Then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures, and care for it until it heals.
However, if the breast inclines downwards and softens due to its size, as happens with women, then it is necessary to cut it open on its upper two sides with two incisions resembling a crescent shape, each of which is held together with the other at the edges of both, until a larger line contains a smaller one, according to this form, from line b to line g. Afterwards, scrape off the skin that is between the two edges, and remove the fat, and apply what we said about sutures and powders, which is necessary for the treatment, until it heals.
However, if you do not complete the incision that you should make, due to the patient's distress, or due to the flow of blood, then you should fill the wound with lint soaked in a corrosive ointment, and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then care for it until it heals.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess, which occurs beneath the armpit.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses which occur beneath the armpit, are of the species of hard Strophulae, which contain cysts, and of those that contain moisture.
Therefore, those of them which contain moisture, must be incised with a lunate incision, according to this shape, from line b to line g.  When thus the entire pus from the abscess is evacuated, then fill it with old lint, and leave it until another day.
Then remove the lint, and treat it with a suitable ointment for it, until it is healed.
If the abscess is of the species of knot-like Strophulae, then make an incision over it, as we have said in the section concerning Strophulae, equally.
If however the wound extends in such a way as to emit pus, then apply a cautery to it, according to what we have said in what has gone before.
Chapter XLIX.
Concerning the incision of an abscess that occurs due to artery and vein.
When an artery bursts, and the skin above it consolidates, then an abscess frequently results from this. And similarly, if a vein bursts, an abscess and swelling occur.
And the signs by which one knows that the abscess and swelling are due to an artery and vein are these: because, when the abscess is due to an artery, it is long, clustered in the deep part of the body, and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a creaking sound.
And that which is due to a vein is round on the surface of the body.
And incision upon this abscess is an error, and especially that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and neck, and in many places of the body: and it is very large. Therefore, its treatment with iron should be avoided, and that which is also from them in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
Therefore, upon that which is from them due to the inflation of the artery's opening, make an incision into the skin lengthwise. Then, open the incision with hooks, afterward the artery: and free it from its coverings, until it is exposed.
Then insert a needle beneath it, and cause it to penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread in two places, according to how I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries that are in the temples.
Then pierce the place, which is between the two ligatures with a lancet, until all the blood within it issues forth, and the abscess is resolved.
Then administer a treatment that produces pus, until the ligatures fall off; afterward, treat it with suitable ointments for it, until it heals.
If there should be an abscess because of the incision of a vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand what you can of the abscess with the skin. Then insert a needle beneath the place you held with your hand, and cause it to penetrate; and let a double thread be on it, until you pull it out from the other side. Then tie the abscess itself with a good ligature; according to what I told you in the ligature of the grape, from two sides with two ends of the thread.
If however you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire abscess at the incision of the needle; press, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle, until what is within it emerges; then cut away the excess skin, and let that which is tied be from it.
Then place on it a poultice which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then administer treatment, which is done with lint and ointments, until it heals.
Chapter L.
Concerning the abscess that arises from the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, so too does an abscess occur in the nerves: this occurs in them through blows, or arises from excessive labor, and similar things; and it most often occurs in the aidor regions, or the heel, and in every place where the joints move: and it is a serious abscess.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is in many dispositions without pain; and when it is squeezed with force, the afflicted person feels a similar sensation to numbness in it.
And it is not a deep-seated, aggregated abscess, but rather is beneath the skin, and it moves in every direction, and does not go forward or backward.
Therefore, those that are in the joints should not be lanced with an iron instrument. For perhaps contraction will result.
In that which is on the head, or forehead, cut the skin with a scalpel. If it is a small abscess, then grasp it with forceps and cut it away from its root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and scrape it: then remove it, and stitch the wound, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the incision of an abscess that occurs in the abdomen.
Some people frequently get growths in their bellies and other parts of their bodies, which are called Fungalia, by reason of their resemblance to fungi.
Their root is slender, and their head is thick, and their lips are turned inwards. And some of them are small; and some of them grow very large.
And I myself have seen a man who had two growths in his belly which were more like fungi than other things: there was no difference between them, they were white, with slender roots, and their lips turned inward and split, and moisture flowed from them constantly.
Therefore I cut them, and found one to weigh about ten and eight ounces, and the other about six ounces.  And the procedure for cutting them is, if the patient is moist, and the color of the growth is white, moist, with a slender root, then cut it with a broad scalpel, and have cauteries present in fire.
For often much blood is expelled at its cutting; therefore, hurry if blood overwhelms you, then cauterize it.
If you see the patient fearful and timid of cutting with iron, then take a thread of lead properly made, and bind the tumor with it: by the method we described, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the lead bandage. Therefore do not cease binding the lead, whenever it presses on the tumor, until it is severed, and falls off by itself without effort.
If however the tumor is of large root, then in its incision is something of fear and terror, especially that which is in the belly.
However, it is possible to cut half of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to cut a tumor that is dark-colored, with little feeling, and of foul appearance: for it is indeed a cancerous abscess; and I shall come with a recollection of Cancer after this.
Chapter LII.
Concerning the treatment of the navel prominence.
The navel prominence arises from many causes. Or from fissures in the skin over the abdomen; and from it protrudes wind, or an intestine, just as happens in other ruptures. Or from blood that flows from a vein or an artery, as previously stated. Or from confined air in it.
If however it is due to a fissure in the skin, and protrusion of a foul thing, then the colour is like the colour of the body, and it is smooth, without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the exit of an intestine, then its location, with the diversity we have described, is more severe. When therefore you press it with your fingers, it disappears; then it returns, and perhaps with a sound; and it greatly enlarges at the entrance of the bath, and with considerable exertion.
If however it is due to moisture, it is smooth, then it does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor is it tightened, nor does it increase.
If however it is due to blood, then with these signs the abscess appears with a blackness from the bottom.
If however it is due to generated flesh, then the abscess is severe, large, hard, and fixed in a single quantity.
If however it is due to windiness, then its touch is smooth.
And the operation in that is, that you should consider then, if the prominence of the navel is due to blood of the artery, or vein, or windiness: then it is necessary that you abstain from its treatment. For in that there is fear and dread, as I taught you in the chapter, in which I said abscesses due to artery and vein.
If the navel's prominence is due to the intestine or to a hernia, then it is necessary to instruct the sick person to hold their breath and stand extended. Then mark with ink around the entire navel. Afterwards, instruct him to lie on his back between your hands. Then encircle with a wide spatula around the navel over the place you marked with ink. Afterwards, extend the middle of the abscess upward with a large hook. Then tie it firmly around with thread, or with a silk cord or firm bandage; and let the knot of the bandage be tied tightly.
Then open the middle of the abscess extended above the bandage. If therefore you find it already caught by the bandage, then extract the tightly bound, and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine towards the interior of the belly; and if you find a hernia, then take it with a hook, and cut off its excess.
If however an artery or vein is contrary to you, then surround it much. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert in them two strong threads; and insert the two needles in the circumference which you have made around the abscess in the form of a cross, which you make penetrate.
Then tighten the abscess in four places over the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place, until the extended flesh rots: and it falls away by itself; or cut it when it is softened and rots.
Then treat it with what is necessary from ointments, until it is healed.
If the eminence of the navel is from flesh there in it, or from moisture, then it is necessary to hollow out the abscess, as we said. And draw out the blood, or moisture, which you find in it.
Then treat it with that which solidifies the place.
Chapter LIII.
Concerning the treatment of CANCER.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of Cancer, and how there is a way to cure it with medicines; and we have warned against using iron in its treatment, so as not to ulcerate it; and we have spoken of Cancer generated in the womb, and the caution in treating it.
And the ancients said that sometimes Cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as Cancer in the breasts or in the thigh, and similar locations on the extremities, by removing the whole of it, especially when it is small and beginning.
However, when it is old and large, you should not approach it.
For I myself could not cure anyone from it, nor did I see anyone before me who achieved it.
And the treatment for it, when it is located as we have said, is to proceed, and to relieve the afflicted from the black cholera on occasion. Then phlebotomize it if you find it with clear fullness.
Then prepare the patient with the preparation, in which it will be placed with the operation.
Then apply hooks suitable for the Cancer. Afterwards, hollow it out from every side with the skin, according to the examination, until nothing remains of its roots: and let the blood flow, and do not sever it quickly, rather press the areas, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand, or with what is possible there from the instruments.
If however the flow of thick blood from an incision of an artery, or vein, gives you trouble, then cauterize the vein until the blood stops. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of dropsy.
Chapter LIII. We have already described in the division of the species of dropsy, and how the gathering of water occurs; and the signs of each species, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron is only the species of ascites alone: and it is not approached with iron for the species of tympanitis: nor for the species of hyposarca completely; for that is mortal.
When you have already treated this species of tympanitic dropsy with medicines, and your treatment has not availed, then consider. For if weakness has already overtaken the patient, or there is another infirmity with him besides the dropsy, such as if there is a cough with him, or looseness of the belly, and things similar to those: then beware lest you treat him with iron, for there is fear of it.
If however you see the patient of exuberant strength, and there is no illness with him besides dropsy alone: and he is not a child, nor an old man: then the method of operation in him is that you raise the patient standing between your hands, and a servant behind him presses his belly with his hand, driving the water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a pointed spear, according to this form. Let it be of two sharp parts, long, and sharp at the end like a spear. However, let it have something of shortness gradually, so that you do not proceed with it in the operation all the way to the intestines, and harm him.
Then consider. For if there is a generation of dropsy from the intestines, then you must extend the incision from the navel by the quantity of three fingers, downwards in a straight line above the pubic bone.
If, however, the generation of dropsy is due to a disease of the liver, then let your incision be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side; and it is also not necessary for the incision to be on the side on which the sick person wishes to lie, so that the fluids do not flow to that weak spot.
Then pierce the entire skin with the instrument. Then insert it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the skin, and do so as if you were scraping it; and let the quantity that is scraped be the quantity of a fingernail, or approximately that.
Then pierce, doing so until the spatula reaches the empty space, and that is the place of water. Then extract it, and insert into the hole the instrument of which this is the shape. And it is similar to a cannula; it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth and polished; in whose lower part is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side, and one on the other side; and the end of it is cut, according to the manner of cutting a reed, as you see; on its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it itself descends immediately upon the instrument, thus evacuating a medium quantity from the water.
Because if you evacuate more from it than is necessary, perhaps the sick person dies in an hour through the resolution of his animal spirit; or a fainting spell occurs, which approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the quantity of its virtue, and according to what the disposition of the sick person signifies to you from the strength of his pulse and from his well-being.
Then withdraw the instrument, and retain the water.
This is because it is retained immediately due to the skin that retains the opening which is on the sifac: about which I have recounted, that you should perforate it according to that manner.
Then again use the instrument on the following day, if you see the sick person can tolerate it, and also extract a small quantity from the water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error, until only a small amount of water remains.
However, if you fear for the sick person and you remove a large amount of water, then treat him with burial in warm sand, and with much sweat in a bath and in the sun, and let him endure thirst; and treat him with drying medicines until he is healed; and cauterize over the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after the extraction of water, according to what I have recounted in that which preceded.
On the treatment of infants born in places where their urine passages are not perforated.
Chapter LV.
When any infant emerges from its mother's womb, and the opening of its urethra is not perforated, or is a tight opening, or is in the wrong place.
Therefore, it is necessary to assist with its perforation immediately when it is born, with a very fine Spatumilus, according to this form.
Then place a thin leaden nail in the opening, and tie it; and hold it for three days, or four.
When, therefore, it wishes to pass urine, it should be removed, and it will urinate. Then return it.
And if you do not place lead, it is now possible that the urine which flows over the area will not allow it to close.
However, he whose opening is constricted is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days, until it is enlarged.
However, those in whom the opening is in an improper place are harmed. For this reason, in those born with the opening at the end of the penis, they cannot urinate forward until they raise their penis with their hand to the top. Nor is there procreation for him, therefore, because the semen cannot reach the womb in a straight line; and it is a very foul sickness.
And the method of the operation in him is that the sick man be laid on his back. Then, extend his penis with your left hand forcefully, and cut off the head of the penis from the glans area, with a sharp scalpel or a sharp spatula.  Just as one cuts a reed, or just as you shave something so that its middle is similar to a penis head, and that the opening is in the middle as is appropriate.
And beware of blood flow during your operation. For this often happens. Therefore, apply to it whatever stops the bleeding, and treat the wound until it heals.
Concerning Albucasis's matters that occur in the foreskin and the glans penis, and concerning corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter LVI.
These things of Albucasis happen often in the penis, and there is a prominence of foul flesh.
And from it is another that is malignant, and another that is not malignant.
Therefore, the non-malignant one, you should suspend with a fine hook, and cut it off, until you cleanse the whole of it. Then place over it a poultice steeped in Egyptian unguent. Then treat it afterward with the unguent dyafinicon, until it is healed.
If it is malignant, of foul colour, then you should administer a cautery to it after its incision and scraping.
If however the Albather is on the foreskin of the uncircumcised, and part of the Albather is inside the foreskin and part of it outside, then you should remove the Albather that is inside first, until when it consolidates, then treat it externally. For when you treat it simultaneously, the foreskin will not be safe from being pierced.
And they also occur in the testicles, and the foreskin, a blackness and corruption. Therefore, it is necessary that you cauterize all that is already blackened, and that which is about to corrupt, or that which is already corrupted.
Then apply a line over it afterwards with honey that has already been mixed with the flour of pomegranate bark, and crushed, sifted herbs.
Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs, then apply a cautery with a lunar cautery, according to this form.
For cautery is helpful for two dispositions at once, namely for the flow of blood, and for a wound when it corrupts.
If the head of the rod is corroded, and the whole is destroyed in an hour, then it is necessary to insert a lead cannula into the urethra, so that the patient may urinate upon it.
However, in the adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the rod, and this adhesion does not happen except in one whose foreskin is healthy, and circumcision is not required upon him: and sometimes adhesion occurs because of a wound, or an abscess: it is therefore necessary to scrape it with a blunt Spatumili, until the ligament is loosened, and the head of the rod is freed from every part.
If it is difficult to distinguish them precisely, then it is necessary to scrape some of the head of the rod, and from the foreskin. This is because the foreskin, when it is thin, is perhaps perforated quickly because of its thinness.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the rod with a thin linen cloth, which has been soaked in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat it with astringent wine, until it is consolidated.
Chapter LVII.
Concerning the cleansing of infants and the treatment of errors that befall them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds; however, since it is an operation of our own making, done by our own will, and its use is specifically in children, it is necessary that we mark it with an operation more beneficial and a path leading to health more easily.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, since they do not use it in their laws, and it is only what we have acquired through experience.
Because I have found most of the surgeons and phlebotomists using cleansing, circumcising with a razor and scissors; and they use cauterization, and tying with thread, and incision with a knife.
And I myself have already tested all these methods, and have not found a better cleansing than with scissors, and tying with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often slips away. Because the skin of the foreskin has two layers; therefore, perhaps the upper layer of skin is cut, and the lower layer remains; therefore it is forced to cut again to cut the other, and there will be pain. And with cleansing that is done by cauterization, that is, digital, which the surgeons use, the incision of the extremity of the male member is not secured; because perhaps it enters its opening.
However, in cleansing with a nail, perhaps the iron slips out and spoils your operation; and the boy's skin is of a short nature, and many times they are born in such a way that they do not need cleansing; and I have seen that.
However, cleansing with scissors, and ligature with thread, has shown me a good experiment; because scissors are proportionate to the incision; because the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below. Therefore, when you press your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportioned, and they cut according to one operation at one time; and a compression is made in a similar shape, containing the skin of the penis on all sides, with which no error falls at all.
And the proper method of operation is, that you cause the boy, especially if he is one of those who understand a little, to be aware that you do not do anything except tie a thread on his penis; and you leave him until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be joyful in every way possible to you, from that which he receives with his understanding. Then make him stand between your prepared hands, and let him not be seated; and hide the scissors in your sleeve, or under your foot, so that the boy sees them not at all, nor anything of the instruments.
Then introduce your hand to his penis, and blow into the skin, and pull it upwards until the head of the penis emerges. Then cleanse it from the filth that collects on it.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards tie a second ligature a little below that. Then hold the lower ligature's place with your index finger and thumb, with a good grasp, and cut between the two ligatures. Then quickly lift the skin upwards and extract the head of the rod. Then allow the blood to run slowly (for this is better) and place powder on top; and over the powder a cloth on which is spread the yolk of a hard-boiled egg in rosewater, mixed with good fresh rose oil; and leave it on top until the next day; and treat it with the remaining treatment until healed.
The shape of the scissors for cleaning is this.
Let the two ends be blunt and meet; so that the nail branch does not protrude from them; and let the length of the two sharpened parts be equal to the length of the two handles.
However, an error in the cleaning process is: that perhaps the inner skin is entirely or partly lost at the incision.
Therefore, you should extend it from your time with your fingernail before the location becomes an abscess, and cut it according to equality.
If you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then extract it with a hook, and cut it.
However, if three days have passed and what is under the rod remains swollen, abscessed: then leave it until the abscess is warm and ripe, and peel it off easily, and cut it according to what is prepared for you, and beware of the head of the rod. For it will not harm it.
Therefore, treat it with what consolidates wounds from the powders we have described in the treatise on powders.  And if it is cut from the skin beyond measure, and extends upwards, that will not harm it either.
Therefore, treat it with what we have said, until it is healed.
Chapter LVIII.
Concerning the extraction of retained urine in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from an obstruction caused by a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or a growth of flesh, and similar things to those we have mentioned in the methods of treatment in the division.
And if the urine is not discharged, and you see that its retention is due to a stone, which is already in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that he be weak and bent over his knees. Then let his feet be placed upon his back, and let him extend his abdomen from the upper part of his back, and let the weak man strain his breath as much as possible. Thus, it is forced with the neck of the bladder to expel the stone backwards: wherefore the urine is released.
If it is not released with what we have said, and the matter is severe on the patient, then it is necessary to administer its extraction with an instrument called a syringe, whose form is this: Let it be of silver, and let it be slender, smooth, concave like a bird's quill cannula in thinness, one and a half palms in length, to which let there be a slender cannula at its head.
And the method of the urine's exit through it is that you take a doubled thread, and tie to its end wool, or tow, with a good ligature; and insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the syringe; and cut with shears if any excess of wool remains, but insert it into the cannula with force, like a lever.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and egg white; and make the patient sit on a seat, and embrocate the bladder, and its penis with moist oils, and olive oil, and tepid water.
Then insert the syringe into the penis with ease, until it reaches the root of the penis.
Then double it upwards from the navel.
Then drive the syringe inside it, until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels it has already reached the empty space; and this is not done in this order, unless because the passage, in which it travels, is one in which there is tortuosity.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For urine will follow the wool.
Then pull it out, and make the urine run through the urinary passage; and repeat the syringe, and do not cease to do that until the bladder is emptied, and the patient finds relief.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a clepsydra.
Chapter LIX.
When a sore occurs in the bladder, or blood congeals in it, or pus is collected in it, and force is applied to pour water and medicines into it: then it is done with the instrument called a Clepsydra, and this is its form.
It is, in fact, deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other side, as you see.
And the hollow space, in which that which impels it is located, should be according to the amount that fills it without addition, until, when something is pulled with it, moisture is drawn out; and when it is impelled, it is expelled along with it to a distance; according to how a clepsydra works, by which naphtha, that is, Greek fire, is projected in naval battles.
Therefore, when force is to project the moisture into the bladder, introduce the end of the clepsydra into the moisture, and pull with that which impels it upwards, for the moisture is drawn into the concavity of the clepsydra.
Then introduce its end into the tube, as we have described in the syringe. Then propel the moisture with that which impels it, for that moisture reaches the bladder immediately, so that the sick person feels it.
A fine form of clyster with which the bladder is clysterized.
Let it be made of silver, or brass; its head should be higher, similar to a small alca, and below it a sinus into which a ligament falls.
Then take the bladder of a ram, and place in it the moisture with which you wish to clyster the bladder. Then tie it between two sinuses with a strong ligature made of doubled thread; and warm that moisture a little over the fire.
Then introduce the end of the clyster into the tube, afterwards grasp your hand over the bladder containing the moisture with a fitting constriction, until the weak person feels that the moisture has now reached the bladder.
If, however, you do not have a bladder present, then take a piece of parchment, and make a circle from it, and pierce it all around its circumference. Then introduce a strong thread into those openings, as they revolve. Then assemble the circle, as a flask is assembled; after you place in it that which you wish from moistures, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument, and do with it as you did with the bladder by pressing with your hand, until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter LX.
Concerning the extraction of the Stone.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of Stone, and its treatment with medicines; and I have described the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and the bladder, and its signs in their entirety; and I have described the stone in which operation by hand and incision is required; and this is the stone generated in the bladder properly; and that which is fixed in the urethra; and I am recounting it with clear explanation and brevity.
Therefore, I say that a stone generated in the bladder most often occurs in children.
And among its signs is that the urine passes from the bladder like water in its subtlety; and white sand appears in it, and the afflicted person rubs their urethra, and struggles with it, and frequently has to lean over. Then it rises and the anus discharges with it in many of them.
And the cure is easy for children, as long as they do not reach fourteen years of age. And it is difficult in the elderly. However, in young people, it is moderate, in that it is between those two things: and if the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, the cure is the opposite of that.
Therefore, when we come to the treatment, it is first necessary to cleanse the sick person with an enema, which will extract all the faeces that are in their intestines; for it sometimes prevents the finding of the stone during the investigation.
Then let the patient be taken with their feet, and shaken and moved downwards: so that the stone may descend to the bottom of the bladder, or leap from a high place sometimes.
Then have him sit between your hands prepared, and let his hands be under his thighs, so that the entire bladder will be sloping downwards.
Then examine him, and touch him from the outside.
If therefore you feel a stone in the space, then immediately proceed with a section upon it.
If, however, it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary that you wipe your index finger with oil from your left hand, if the patient is a child; or your middle finger if he is a full grown youth; and that you insert it into his anus, and search for the stone, until it is under your finger; and turn it gradually towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and drive it outwards against the place, whose section you desire; and command a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and command another servant to extend the testicles with his right hand upwards; and with his other hand, to remove the skin, which is beneath the testicles, in the part from the place where the section is.
Then insert the Spatumile, which is of this form. And find it in that which is between the anus and the testicles, and not in the middle, to the side of the left buttock; and let the incision be upon the stone, and your finger be in the anus; and let the incision be transverse, so that the incision be wide on the outside, and narrow on the inside, according to the amount that it is possible for the stone to exit from it, not being larger.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the stone at the incision, and it exits without difficulty.
And know that the stone has angles and edges, wherefore its exit is difficult because of it.
And if it is smooth, like a gland, and round, its exit is easy.
Therefore, to the one that has angles and edges, add in the fissure.
If it does not exit thus, then it is necessary that you devise above it: either that you string above it with a properly shaped instrument, the extremity of which is like the Moon, which strings above the stone, and does not slip from it; or that you introduce a thin instrument with a curved extremity beneath it.
If however you cannot above it, then enlarge the opening a little. If anything from the blood overcomes you, cut it with a zegi.
If however the stones are more than one, then drive the largest first to the opening of the bladder. Then cut above it. Afterwards drive the small one after that; and similarly do if there are more than two.
If it is very large, then it is ignorance to cut above it with a large incision. For if one of two things happens to the sick person, either to die or to suffer constant dripping of urine, because the place is not entirely consolidated.
But administer its expulsion until it emerges; or fashion instruments in its fracture with forceps, until you extract it bit by bit.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, and aloe, and dragon's blood; and bind it, and place over it a cloth infused with oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be seated.
Then lay the sick person on their back, and do not loosen the bandage, until the third day. When therefore you have loosened it, embrocate the place with water and much oil.
Then treat it with dyafinicon ointment, and basilicon ointment, until it is healed.
If however in the wound a hot abscess is added, and corrosive, and similar things: such as if blood is congealed in the bladder, and the urine is prevented from flowing out: and this is recognized from the excretion of blood with the urine. Therefore, insert your finger into the wound, and extract the blood. For if it remains, it provokes corruption of the bladder, and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and apply whatever is appropriate from the treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary in the hours of the entire treatment, that you bind the two thighs, and the buttocks, on account of the medicines which are placed on the place.
If a small stone is in the urethra, and is stuck in it, and prevents the exit of urine: then treat it with what I have mentioned before you get to the incision. For often you will be spared the incision by this treatment, for I have already proven it.
And it is, that you take a perforator of iron, whose form is this: triangular tip, pointed, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread, and tie the urethra below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the piercing iron into the urethra with ease, until the perforator reaches the stone itself: and revolve the perforator with your hand on the stone gradually: and try to pierce it, until you cause it to penetrate on the other side.
For the urine is immediately discharged.
Then press your hand on that which remains of the stone from the outside of the urethra. For it breaks itself and comes out with the urine, and the sick person is healed.
If however this treatment is not prepared for you, because of something that prevents it: then tie a thread under the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone on the urethra itself between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards, loosen the ligature, and cleanse the congealed blood that forms in the wound.
And a ligature of thread under the stone is not necessary unless to prevent its return to the bladder; and another ligature above, unless that when the thread is loosened after the stone's removal, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound; and for that reason it is necessary for you to elevate the skin upward when you tie the upper thread, so that it returns to its proper position and covers the wound, as we said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the extraction of a stone in women.
Little stone is produced in women.
However, if a stone happens to any of them, then the cure is difficult, and it is prohibited due to many methods.
One of them is: that a woman is perhaps a virgin.
And the second is, because you do not find a woman who reveals herself to a physician, when she is chaste, or from those having husbands.
The third is, because you do not find a woman well knowing this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is the distance from the location of the stone: wherefore it needs a deep incision, and in that is fear.
If necessity provokes to that, then it is necessary that you take a woman physician, well knowing: and little is found.
If truly you isolate her, then seek a chaste, skillful physician, and present a skilled female midwife knowledgeable in women's matters, or a woman who indicates a part of the art in this skill:  make her present, and especially for her to do whatever you command her from the examination concerning the stone.
First of all, that she examine if the woman is a virgin: then it is necessary that she insert a finger into her anus and seek the stone.
If therefore she finds it, she should grasp it beneath her finger, and then command the midwife to insert her finger into the vagina of the sick woman, and inquire about the stone, after she places her left hand upon the bladder and compresses it with a good compression.
If therefore she finds the stone, then it is necessary that she gradually move it from the opening of the bladder downwards with all her skill, until she reaches it with the root of the thigh.
Then she should cut upon it at the point opposite the middle of the vulva at the root of the thigh, from whichever side it is prepared for her, and she feels the stone in that part: and her finger should not be removed from the stone, grasping it underneath; and let the incision be small at first. Then insert a rod over that incision.
Then insert a rod over that incision. If therefore she feels the stone, then she should add to the incision according to the proportionate amount that the stone emerges from it.
And know that the types of stone are many. For from these are the small, and the large, the smooth and the rough, the long, and the round having branches.
Know therefore its kinds, so that this may be made clear to you regarding that which you wish.
If therefore blood overcomes you, then add in the place of crushed zegi, and hold it for one hour, until the blood is severed.
Then return to your operation, until the stone emerges; and do so, preparing for yourself from the instruments, which I have said in the extraction of stone in men, so that you may assist in your operation by means of them.
If, however, a flow of blood overcomes you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from an artery that has been cut, then place powder over the place, and bind it with linen bandages in a fitting constriction; and do not change it; and leave the stone, and do not extract it. Perhaps, indeed, the weak woman will die. Then treat the wound.
When, therefore, the sharpness of the blood subsides after days, and the place becomes putrid, then return to your operation, until the stone emerges.
Chapter Sixty-two.
Concerning the incision of a watery hernia.
A watery hernia is not, unless it is an aggregation of moisture in a white sifac, which is beneath the skin containing the testicle, and is called sifac.
And sometimes it is in its own membrane, which nature prepared for it in the part of the testicle, until it is putrid: which is another testicle, and is the skin of the testicle between the white sifac, which we said: and this does not happen except rarely.
And the generation of this hernia, indeed, is from the weakness that happens to the testicles: therefore this matter is poured out to them. And sometimes it happens from a blow on the testicles.
And this moisture has many colours. For either its color is inclined to yellowness, or it is blood-red, or foul, black, or watery white, and that is what is more.
And the signs by which it is known where the collection of water is, are these. For if it is in the white sifac that we mentioned, then the abscess is round, somewhat elongated like the shape of an egg: and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture contains it on every side.
And if the moisture is in the proper tissue to itself, then the abscess is round like a part of an egg, and therefore a man estimates that it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under the senses.
Therefore, when you desire knowledge of the color of the moisture, then experience the color of the moisture in the abscess with the introduced square, whose form preceded. Therefore, according to that which emerges in the track of the Intromission, judge about that which is between it.
Therefore, when we come to treatment with iron, then it is necessary that you order the sick person to be bled if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then let him lie on his back upon a slightly raised surface, and place many cloths beneath him.
Then sit you on his left side, and order the attendant to sit on his right, who shall extend his rod towards one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles, and towards the part of the navel of the belly.
Then take a broad spatula, and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle lengthwise, until near the pubic bone; and let the incision be according to the straightness of an equidistant line which divides the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the white sifac that encloses it: and scrape it and observe it, or beware of this, that you separate it: and let your scraping with which you scrape it, be from the part that hangs with the more egg; and subtly scrutinize it with your scraping according to the amount that is possible for you.
Then pierce the sifac full of water with a large perforation, and draw out all the water.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the sifac upwards, and do not touch the skin of the testicles that encompasses it: and cut the sifac however it is possible for you to cut it, either with its entirety, or in pieces, especially its thin side. For if you do not meticulously investigate, security is not achieved in its incision, lest the water return.
If, however, the egg protrudes outward from its skin during your operation: then, when you have completed the incision of the sifac, return it. Then bring together the two lips of the scrotal skin with sutures.
Then treat it like the other wounds until it heals.
If you find a corrupted egg with a different blackness, then you must tie the vessels that are the suspensory ligaments, fearing the flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with the suspensory ligament, and remove the egg; afterward treat it with what we have said.
If, however, water is collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches: therefore, cut the other part in the same way you did to the other one, equally.
And if it is made equal for you, so that it is one operation, then do it; afterward wipe away the blood. And introduce into the sections wool soaked in oil, or in rose oil; and place outside wool that you soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles, and the belly's wound; and place over it doubled cloths, and they are pillows; and tie them above with a ligament, which has six according to this form.
Then take two cloths, and sew them together, and fill them with dyed wool, according to this likeness; and let the edges with which it is pressed be made of cotton or damp wool; and let them be gathered with a constriction at the lumbar region of the breeches; and one edge shall take hold above the anus as far as the back, and the second edge shall take hold at the side of the pubic region; and two edges above the two thighs below, and two other edges above the two groins, and let the entire constriction be gathered in the lumbar region of the breeches.
And sometimes this branch is artificially treated with a cautery in the place of the incision with iron: and it is that you should take a small, knife-like cautery, and with it cut the skin of the testicles; and it is ignited, as we have described; until, when a white, water-containing substance is revealed, then take the cautery, according to this form.
It resembles a Greek letter.
Then pierce it with it, and let it be ignited, until all the moisture comes out.
Then spread the sifac with hooks, and scrape it with a heated fine cautery, and cut it as much as possible, until you eradicate the whole of it.
And be careful, from the egg, lest the fire touch it.
And know that this operation is safer, and farther from blood flow, and better than the operation with incision.
However, if it presents opposition to you at the time of incision, or at the time of cautery a hot abscess, or blood flow, or anything else: then you must treat whatever causes opposition to you from it with the treatment that suits it, according to what has been stated.
However, if the patient is timid and does not await this operation, due to its horror: then administer the perforation as I describe it; and it is so.
When it is certified to you by what we have said concerning signs that the ramex is watery, then you must have the patient sit on an elevated seat.
Then perforate the abscess in its lower part with a broad spatule, and make the perforation along the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water runs out.
Then place a poultice on the location, and bandage it, and allow it to run out the remaining water; then treat it until the wound is healed.
If, however, there is an obstruction to the outflow of water at the hour when you perforate it; and this is only because a white pannus obstructs the wound's opening and hinders the exit of the water: then it is necessary to insert a pen into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of hydrops is extracted, the form of which has preceded; or add a little to the wound's operation.
