And know that the water returns and collects after six months, or about that. When it therefore collects, then let it be perforated in the same manner, and the sick person will pass their days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy Ramicis.
Know that in the section above this Ramic, there is fear leading to harm in many dispositions. Therefore it seems to me that its dismissal is to be saved from it.
And I am recalling the operation in it, and the appearance of the abscess.
I say, therefore, that many abscesses occur in the bodies from which the testicles are composed; and that is produced from many causes, namely.
Or from acute or acrid superfluity, which is poured out to the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it; and it is a serious abscess.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it arises from the knotting of arteries and their inflation, as its recollection has already preceded; or from the inflation of veins.
Therefore, that which arises from the inflation of arteries is recognized by the disposition of the abscess, when you press it with your fingers. And you should not touch it completely.
However, that which arises from the inflation of veins, nothing is dispersed from it upon compression with the fingers.
And it is now possible that a section be made upon this species and upon the rest of the flesh; and it is as if you were to separate the skin of the testicles. Then raise the egg upwards and draw it out with a white sifach; and free the suspensorium from the vessels, and tie the vessels; and cut the suspensorium after you have freed it from all parts of the egg.
If therefore the egg is consolidated with those nascent flesh parts, then it is necessary to draw out the egg, and to cut it.
And if there is an adhesion between something of the siphaket, or in that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary to free all that adhesion, and to cut it with a round incision.
If however there is a nativity of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary to cut all of it and draw out the egg, as we said.
When therefore your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter Sixty-four.
Concerning the treatment of Ramicis, which is with Vite.
Vitis is an abscess enveloped in a certain wrapping, similar to a bunch of grapes, with a softening of the testicles; and it makes difficult for the sick person movement and exercise, and walking.
And in the treatment of this illness there is fear, because of that which preceded, as remembered.
But it is necessary that we should say the operation in it according to what the first ones did.
I say therefore, that it is necessary that you make the sick person sit on a raised seat. Then push down the suspensory ligament of the testicles. Then grasp the skin of the testicles with your fingers, along with the vessels that are close to the penis; and let another attendant hold them, and stretch them with a forceful extension.
Then, with a broad, sharp scalpel, make a transverse incision directly over the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then scrape away from every side, as we told you in the extraction of the arteries that are in the temples.
Then insert a needle with a double thread into them, and tie it in the first of the places where the veins occur; and tie it again in the last of them. Then cut it in the middle, in a straight section along the length of the body, and extract what is collected from the putrid, foul humors.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds, in which the force generates pus, so that the vessels nourishing one testicle may fall. For there is no evil through it.
However, if all the vessels are affected, then it is necessary that the testicles, with the vessels, be removed; so that the egg is not deprived of nourishment, because of the incision, and it withers or dries up, and there is no benefit with them.
Chapter LXV.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal colic.
The outcome of this colic is from a section, which occurs in a sifach extended over the abdomen in the groin area from a mirach of the belly.
Why do the intestines descend from that rupture to one testicle? And this rupture is either from a sifach, or its extension.
And these two types occur from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or the lifting of a heavy object, and similar things.
And a sign, when it is from the extension of a sifach. It is that which happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly: and it is an abscess equal towards the depth; because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And a sign of it, when it is from a section of the sifach, because it makes a severe pain occur suddenly at its beginning: and the abscess is diverse, appearing under the skin nearby; and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course outwards from the sifach.
And sometimes it emerges with the intestine in a circle; and this is called a ramified intestine, and zirbia; and sometimes it happens with flatulence.
And sometimes faeces run into the intestine and are retained there: and this is a harm to the sick person, because it causes a dull pain, and especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of these types of illnesses, there is fear with iron; therefore, a fall must be avoided in it.
And the method of operation is that you should instruct the sick person to draw back the intestine with their hand into the interior cavity of their body, if it yields to the withdrawal. Then he should be laid back on his neck between your hands, and elevate his legs.
Then extend the skin that follows the skin upward, and cut the skin of the testicles entirely lengthwise. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them; and let the incision be according to the amount, that it may be possible for the egg to emerge from it.
Then scrape the siphak, and what is under the skin of the testicles, until when the white, hard siphak is exposed on every side; then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is under the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and detach with it the adhesion that is on the posterior part of the egg.
Then move with your right hand the skin of the testicles inward; and with this, extend the white siphak upward with your left hand; and elevate the egg with the siphak towards the incision; and order the servant to extend the egg upward; and you sever the adhesion, which is behind the complete separation.
And examine with your fingers that there is nothing there of the intestine wrapped in the hard white siphak. If however you find something of it: then push it towards the lower abdomen.
Then take a needle, in which is a thick thread, made of linen from ten threads; and insert it at the last siphak, that is, the tunic, which is under the skin of the testicles which follows the incision; then cut the ends that protrude from the thread, until there are four threads. Then place some of them over some with a figure that is in the form of a cross; and bind with them the alsifach, of which we spoke, because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligament, until no vessels, which nourish them, can send anything to them; so that a hot abscess does not result from it. And place another ligament outside the first ligament, distant from it by no more than two fingers. And after these two ligaments, leave from the siphak, which is under the skin of the testicles, a quantity the size of a finger; and cut the rest entirely around, and remove the egg from it.
Then cut into the lower part of the skin of the testicles, from which blood and pus runs, as we described in what has gone before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it on the wound; and apply the bandage that we have described. And sometimes the white siphak that we mentioned is cauterized after its incision, fearing the flow of blood. For this often happens. And leave the bandage until it falls off by itself.
If however its falling is delayed, then it should be soaked with hot water; and place upon it what we said, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the rest of the wound treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVI.
Concerning the windy ram.
In this ram, I have not seen anyone who dared to use iron for its treatment.
And the first ones said that it happens as we have described in the ram with the vine. That is, tie the vessels after the incision, then cut in the middle, and finally treat with whatever opens it until the vessels fall out; and treat the wound as we described until it is healed.
Chapter LXVII.
Concerning the rupture that occurs in the groin.
When rupture occurs in the groin, as we have said, and the area bulges, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles; and if it does descend, it is little and returns every hour; but if the time with it is prolonged, a fissure is added in the scrotum, until the intestines descend into the scrotum.
And that happens from the stretching of the scrotum, which is in the groin, as we said. That is because the scrotum is stretched; then the area softens and becomes an abscess, and bulges.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as its preceding description.
And sometimes it is cured with an iron, according to this method: and it is that the sick person lies on their back between your hands. Then cut the place of the ulcerated and bulging rupture, cutting across its width, according to the size of three fingers.
Then press the scrotum and what is under the skin, until when the white scrotum is exposed, which is under the skin that follows it.
Then take a rod, and place it upon the bulging place of the scrotum, and press it to the depth of the belly.
Then sew the two bulging places on the end of the rod from the scrotum, and connect one of them to the other with a suture; then pull out the end of the rod.
And do not cut the scrotum at all, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, just as I have made you know in the treatment of the intestinal hernia.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds. When therefore you cut the threads, cleanse it, and restore the wound, until it is consolidated.
For the scrotum contracts, and no bulge of the rupture is added.
And the cautery is better in this place, since it is closer to health.
Chapter LXVIII.
On the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles is softened in some men, because of which the flesh that is between them is softened, and its appearance is disfigured.
Therefore it is necessary for whoever is asked to treat it, to have the sick man lie on his back, and to cut all the softened skin over the natural skin.
Then he should bring together the two lips with sutures: and if you wish, first sew up the excess of softened skin with three or four sutures, and secure the suture.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat the remaining wounds with care until they heal and the threads fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
Concerning Castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law; and because of that, I might not have mentioned it in this book of mine; and its mention is not necessary unless for two reasons.
One of which is that it should be in the knowledge of a physician when questioned about it, and so that he may know the treatment of whoever has it.
And the other reason is that we often require the castration of certain animals, for our benefit through it, such as rams, goats, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore, I say that castration is done according to two methods. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and incision.
In that which is done by attrition, the method of operation is that we make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles soften and loosen and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer visible to the touch.
In castration by fissure and incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut above each testicle with one incision, until two testicles are exposed; then cut them after you have peeled back each, and do not leave any sifak on them apart from the thin sifak that is above the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by attrition, because attrition perhaps leaves some part of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires intercourse.
Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter LXX.
Concerning the treatment of a hermaphrodite, there are two methods.
A hermaphrodite is from men in two ways. One of these is that, as it appears, there is a space following, or in the skin of the testicles, in the space between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs; and sometimes urine runs from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one kind, and it is a woman's vulva upon the pubis like very small male testicles protruding outwardly; of which one is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of three kinds, two from men, and one kind from women, is: because it is necessary to cut away the added flesh, until their impression disappears. Then treat them with the treatment of the other wounds.
In the second kind of men, however, from which urine issues, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of the labia, and the protruding flesh in women's vulvas.
The labia may perhaps be added to the natural condition until its appearance becomes ugly and foul; and sometimes in some women it becomes so large that it spreads out like in men and reaches the point of sexual intercourse.
Therefore, you should hold back the excess of the labia with your hand, or with a hook, and make an incision; and do not cut too deeply, especially into the root; so that a flow of blood does not occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals.
However, the flesh that grows is flesh that arises in the opening of the womb until it fills it; and perhaps it emerges outwardly in the likeness of a tail; and for that reason, some earlier writers call the illness the "tailed illness. Therefore, it is necessary to cut it as we cut the labia, and treat it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of the Alratica, which is the unpierced vulva of a woman.
Chapter LXXII.
Alratica is when a woman's vulva is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is from an illness that preceded it; indeed, it is made either from added flesh growth, or by a subtle or thick sifach; and it is made either deep within the womb, or in its sides, or in its upper or lower part; and it prevents intercourse and impregnation, and childbirth, and perhaps it prevents menstruation.
And its recognition is made through the obstetrician's observation, if an apparent obstruction is nearby.
If however it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a probe.
If however the obstruction is from a thin sifach, and is close to the two lips: then hurry, and break it apart.
And it is, that you place pads on the two lips.
Then place your index fingers of both hands together, and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread. Then extend the two lips with strength, until the thin sifach is broken, and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool, and soak it in oil, and place it on the location: and let the woman do this every day, so that the location does not solidify again.
If however the sifach is thick, dense: then it is necessary to cut it with a spatula wide similar to a myrtle leaf.
If however the obstruction is born of flesh, then suspend it with hooks, and cut it: and let there be with you medicines that assuage the flow of blood without mordication, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense mixed with the white of an egg.
Then administer a wide lead cannula, so that the wound does not heal quickly, keep it for a few days: and administer a linen bandage, then proceed with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
And sometimes other flesh births occur in the womb: therefore, it is necessary that they be cut in this way: while it is not a cancerous abscess; for a cancerous abscess in the womb, there should be no opposition with iron at all.
Chapter LXXIII.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and red Bothor in women's vulvas.
Hemorrhoids indeed are the opening of the veins' orifices, until a lot of blood flows from them constantly.
When hemorrhoids become chronic, they become thelil; and I have already stated their species and signs in the division; and I will state here which receive treatment from them and which do not.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the senses, then there is no treatment with iron for them.
However, those of them which are in the orifice of the womb upon which the senses fall, are those which are cured.
Therefore, it is necessary that you introduce the woman into a cool house. Then extend the thelil with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then powder over them, at the flow of blood from one of the incisions, without chewing: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar to them.
Then let him propel her feet against the wall for one hour. Afterwards, place her in cold water.
If the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and quince bark, and gallnuts, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and make it into a paste with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it on her back.
If therefore the blood is cut off with it, it is good; and if not, make cupping vessels adhere to her back and under her arms, without scarification.
Then take wool, and immerse it in the juice of tarathit, or the juice of arnoglossa, or the juice of the alaulat branches, that is, aglanter, Pontic wine, and apply it to the wound.
When therefore the abscess subsides, then treat the area with ointments until it heals.
Albarth is indeed red, similar to the heads of alhaasse, and is of harsh appearance.
Therefore, you should cut away what appears from it, according to what I said in the thelil equally, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter LXXIV.
Concerning the perforation of the exit that occurs in the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and abrasions, and alekilati, that is, herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and ragadiae, and thelil, and a hot abscess.
And I have already discussed all these ailments and their types, and their signs, and their cures in the division: it is necessary to describe in this treatise the hot abscess that occurs in the womb, when it is from the abscesses which collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is made with an iron.
Therefore, it is necessary to consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is sharp with its percussion, and inflammation, and fever: and you see it red: if the sensation falls upon it, then do not hasten with its perforation: and treat it with that which helps in its maturation, until when the sharpness of the occurrences subsides.
Then it is necessary to have the woman sit on a seat that slopes two seats, and to lean, or lie down on her back, and to draw her feet to the lower part of her abdomen: and let her thighs be spread out, and let her place her arms under her knees, and bind each with a suitable bandage to it.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and administer the instrument with which the opening of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary, when you wish to administer this instrument, to fix it with the rod deep within the woman's womb: so that that which enters from the instrument is no more than the depth of the womb, and does not harm the weak one.
However, if the instrument is more than the depth, then it is necessary that you place cushions upon the tissue of the womb's depth: so that the entire instrument's entry into the womb's depth may be prevented: and it is necessary that he place the rotation, which runs on the instrument, from the upper side; and that he hold the instrument, and that the minister rotate the rotation, until the womb's depth is opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and is touched by the hand, and is smooth and soft, then it is necessary to separate it at its head with a wide spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place on the wound a linen cloth soaked in rose oil, or green oil, in which there is some astringency; and place the cloth, and extract the section into the depth of the womb.
And place externally over the womb, and over the pubis, clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
Then treat it after two days with the ointment that suits it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed, so that it is flushed with water and honey; then with water in which the root of lily or long aristolochia has been boiled; then return to the ointment.
If the wound is hidden inside the womb, then it is necessary that you refrain from treating it with iron, rather treat it with what we said in the division.
Chapter LXX.
Teachings on Midwifery, how living embryos are treated, having emerged in a non-natural form.
It is necessary for the midwife to first know the form of natural childbirth.
Therefore, among its signs is this: when you see a woman pressing downwards, desiring to smell the air, and relief is upon her regarding the birth, and the embryo's exit is hurried: then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and its exit is upon her head, and the afterbirth with it: either hanging or suspended from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her abdomen be compressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon her head, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluities by good cleansing.
However, any birth besides this one is unnatural, blameworthy.
For sometimes the fetus emerges upon its feet, and its body emerges before its head, and its feet, and its hands together, or one foot: or its head and hand emerge: or the belly emerges: and perhaps it turns upon its neck, and similar things are among the blameworthy figures.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be prudent, subtle in all these matters, and to beware of error and deception: and I am showing every form among them, and how the skill is in it, and how it is signified by it, and how to stand upon it.
When the fetus emerges upon its head in its natural emergence, and it becomes violent upon the woman, and her labor becomes difficult, and you see her strength already weakened: then have her sit on a seat, or let women hold her; and apply vapors to her vulva with decocted fenugreek and with moist oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and split the afterbirth with it, and cut it with her nail until that which is within it runs out from the moisture; and press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If, however, it does not descend, then it is necessary for the woman to be cleansed with a clyster of fenugreek mucilage, with sesame oil.
Then prescribe to her after the clyster that she compress herself; and make her sneeze with condiments, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will emerge immediately.
If, however, two hands of the fetus emerge first, it is necessary that you reduce them with ease gradually. If, however, it does not yield during entry, then place the woman on a seat, and raise her feet upwards; then shake the seat on the ground; and let the woman be held so that she does not fall during the shaking.
If, therefore, the hands do not emerge, and the fetus dies: then cut them, and remove the remaining fetus; and tie its hand with cloth, and extract it with equality; for it will emerge.
The exit of the embryo is upon its feet. When the fetus emerges upon its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards; then turn the fetus gradually; afterwards, equalize it.
Then take one of its feet and extend it gradually.
When, therefore, they emerge up to the hips, then command it to be compressed, and make it sneeze with condiments; for it will emerge, if God wills.
And if it does not emerge with what we have described, then return the fetus gradually until you place it in its natural form. For it will then emerge with easy exit.
If it is prevented by all that we have described, then take from marshmallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum, and pound all in a mortar with a good pounding.  Then line with it the vulva of the woman and the lower part of her belly. Then make her sit in warm water until she experiences hypocondria.
When therefore you see that her lower parts are already heated, then make her a poultice of myrrh, and apply it. When therefore the poultice has held for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze, and pinch her nose and mouth, and press the lower part of her belly with gentle pressure. For the pus will come out immediately.
The exit of the fetus is upon the knees and hands. When it emerges in this manner, then gently insert her hands into it. Then the woman shall lie on her back with her feet hanging down, lying on the bed, and her hands folded. Then press gently on her abdomen. Then pull out what is under her feet from the pillows until it is prepared at the lower part. If the fetus does not emerge, then take her feet and move them with a forceful motion. Then press gently on what is above the hypochondria until the fetus ascends to the upper parts. Then let her insert her hands, and gently adjust the fetus; and instruct the woman to compress until the fetus emerges.
The exit of the fetus hangs sideways from the hands. Her hand should be skillfully employed in the reduction. If however she cannot reduce it, then lift the woman, and make her walk.
If, however, she cannot walk, then have her lie on a bed and shake her legs with a forceful shaking.
Then administer the liniment which you prepared from mucilaginous substances.
If childbirth takes it (the pain), then have her sit on a seat. Then strive in the reduction of the hand, and arrange the fetus according to its natural form; and make the woman sneeze, and command her to compress herself, until it emerges.
The exit of the fetus is upon her neck, with outstretched hands, and its face is towards its mother's back. The midwife should hold the hand of the fetus, then gradually turn it and arrange it with ease.
When it is thus arranged, then place upon its vulva what we have described, of oil and mucilages.
Then command her to move gradually. When you have done that, then have her sit on a seat, and command her to lean to her right side. Then command her to be compressed; and make her sneeze with condiments.
The exit of the prepared fetus on the side. When it descends to the opening of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then cut the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture.
If therefore it hangs over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel: so that it is not retained, and dies.
Then push the fetus inward, until you place it as it ought to be: and command the woman to compress herself several times: for it will exit with an easy exit.
The exit of twins, or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three, and four are born. However, that is unusual and rarely happens.
However, five is a thing beyond nature, and they do not entirely live: and a single afterbirth contains them, although they multiply, and they are separated by a partition, distinguishing them, tied to the umbilical cord of each of them.
And sometimes they emerge just as a single fetus emerges by a natural, easy exit, and they emerge by that praiseworthy exit, as we have narrated.
If some of them are difficult at birth, and reach that which we have said of unnatural shapes, then treat them as we have narrated; and let the midwife be subtle, and do what she does with ease and slowness, and devise according to every shape with that which is prepared for her from ingenuity leading to health.
A reminder of what is formed in the womb from embryos, and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb; and it has already been verified by me that a woman miscarried seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
That which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise man.
Therefore, all this must be in the knowledge of the midwife. For perhaps a similar case will come to her: therefore, let her oppose it with that which is necessary.
Chapter LXXVI.
Concerning the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I have said in the division of medicine, and it does not emerge, and you are forced to use an operation with your hand, then it is necessary that you consider.
If then the woman is of sound constitution, and is not afflicted by any of the accidents from which harm is feared for her: then it is necessary that the woman be laid supine on a bed, on her back, and her head lowered, and her legs raised; and let the attendant hold her by two sides, or let her be bound to the bed, so that her body is not moved during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with humid oils, with the mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and flaxseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages; then let her introduce it into the womb with ease. Then let her seek with it the place where she can fix the hooks on the fetus, and suitable places for this.
Therefore consider then, if the fetus is prepared to descend upon its head: then fix the hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its fork, or in places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
If however the descent is upon its feet, then in its pubic bone and its middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand, and place its curve in that which is between the fingers of her left hand, and introduce it with her hand with ease, and fix the hook in any of those places which we have mentioned, until she reaches the empty space.
Then she should fix another, or a third hook, if necessary, opposite it, so that there is equal contraction, and it does not deviate to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let the extension not only be in a straight line, but rather be moved in every direction, so that its exit is eased; just as is done with a tooth during its extraction.
And it is necessary for that which is between them to be softened by the extension. If a part of it remains, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with oil, and insert them from the side, so that she may turn with them that which remains.
If therefore a part of the fetus emerges, then the hooks should be moved to another place that is higher; do this until the entire fetus emerges.
If therefore a hand emerges before the other, and it is not possible to return it, because of its constriction, then it is necessary to wrap a cloth around it. Then pull it out until when the entire one emerges, cut it either at the ankle joint or at the elbow; and thus it is necessary that you do to the other hand, and to the two feet.
If however the fetus's head is large, or constriction occurs in its exit, or water has accumulated in its head: then it is necessary to insert a thorny Spatumile between the fingers, and to split the head with it, and to extract the water; or to break it with the instrument called Almisdac: whose shape comes with the shapes of the other instruments in the chapter that is after this.
Similarly, he should do so if the fetus is of a large-headed nature, then it is necessary to sever it with algengematu, or to extract it with misdach, as we said. Then pull the bones with forceps.
If the head emerges, and is constricted at the fork, then it is to be split, until the moisture that is in the chest is discharged.
If, however, it is not entangled, nor yields to exit: then cut the head into pieces, as much as possible for you.
If, however, the lower belly has an abscess, or has dropsy in it: then it is necessary to pierce it, until moisture runs from it.
If, however, the fetus is above its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction to the mouth of the womb is slight.
If, however, it is constricted at the belly or chest, then it is necessary to extract it with a cloth which you have wrapped around your hand: and to split the belly or chest until that which is in them flows out.
If, however, the rest of the limbs are removed, and the head returns: then the midwife should insert her left hand. If therefore the mouth of the womb is open, then she should insert her hand deep into the womb, and search for the head with it, and pull it with her fingers to the mouth of the womb: then insert one or two hooks into it and pull it with them.
If, however, the mouth of the womb is constricted, because of a hot abscess that has occurred to it: then it is not necessary to work on it, but rather it is necessary then to administer the effusion of humid, fatty things; and to administer plasters; and to sit in waters that soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, then it is possible to equalize it: then let what we said about a living fetus be administered.
If that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you not leave anything of the afterbirth inside at all.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then administer what we said about hot abscesses which occur in the wombs.
If a flow of blood occurs, then let it descend into astringent waters, and administer the rest of the treatment.
And indeed I have already seen a woman, in whose womb a dead fetus was: then she became pregnant over it again, afterwards another fetus died as well. Therefore, a long time after, an abscess occurred in her navel, and it swelled until it opened: and it began to emit pus.
Therefore, I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time: and the wound did not heal.
Therefore, I placed over it some plasters of strong attraction: and a bone came out from the place. Then a few days passed, and another bone came out. I was therefore surprised by this, since there is no bone in the belly.
I therefore thought that they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
I then searched for the wound, and extracted many bones, the woman being in her best condition; and she thus lived a long time, and emitted a little pus from the place.
And I have not established this extraneous thing here, unless because there is knowledge and aid in it for the work that a skillful physician manages with his healing hand.
Chapter LXXVII.
Concerning the shapes of the necessary instruments in the extraction of a fetus.
Shape of the pivot by which the womb is opened.
Here below is the shape of the Forceps, by which books are equalized, to which are equally two pivots at the two ends of two pieces of wood. However, this pivot ought to be finer than the forceps; and so that it is made, either of ebony or of boxwood.
And the width of each piece of wood is about two fingers; and the edge of each of them is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are now fixed to both, whose length is half a palm and a little more; and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are introduced into the opening of the womb, so that it may be opened with them when you turn the two pivots.
Another form, similar to that one, finer, and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony wood, or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity let there be two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each of these additions is about a handspan: and the width of each is two fingers.
When therefore you wish to open the womb with it, then make the woman sit on a bed, with her feet hanging down, and the space between her legs open. Then insert these two joined additions into the womb: and you are holding the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with shears, equally according to the amount you wish of the opening of the mouth of the womb, until the midwife does what she wants.
Another form of Vertigo, which the ancients called the Impelling Form, by which the fetus is impelled.
The Almisdac form, by which the large head is crushed; and it is likened to a pair of tongs, which have teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it becomes elongated, like tongs according to this form, as you see, which have teeth like saw teeth, by which it is cut and crushed.
The form of a hook having one spine.
The form of a hook having two spines.
The form of a spatula with two ends for severing the head.
The form of two spatulas, also broad.
All these instruments are used, and the more their types are multiplied, and they are prepared by the craftsman, the swifter is his operation and the higher he is esteemed among men because of their quantity.
Therefore, do not despise any instrument from among them without having it prepared. For their necessity is inexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Concerning the extraction of the Secundine.
When the Secundine is retained at birth, then it is necessary to instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then cause her to sneeze with spices: and press your hand upon her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it emerges by this means, it is good.
If not, then take a pot, and pierce a hole in the lid: and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as calamint, rue, dill, chamomile, asafoetida, cinnamon wood, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them in water, and place them over the fire.
Then place a reed tube over the hole of the pot's lid, and the other end in the mouth of the womb: and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then cause her to sneeze, as we said. For the afterbirth will emerge with ease.
If however it remains after this treatment, and does not emerge: then instruct the midwife to immerse her hand in sesame oil, or marshmallow mucilage. Then let her insert it into the vagina, and search for the Afterbirth with it. When therefore she finds it, then let her take it, and extend it gradually.
If however it be attached deep within the womb, then let her insert her hand, according to what we have described, until when she finds the Afterbirth, and pull it out gradually in a straight line, so that the womb does not fall during the violent pull; rather, it must be shifted easily to the right and left sides; then let the amount of pull be increased. For it then yields, and is preserved, and escapes from adhesion.
If the mouth of the womb is constricted, as we have already described: treat it with sneezing and by medicine of a pot with herbs.
If, however, it does not emerge with all that we have described, then be careful not to tire yourself out in repeated extraction.
But it is necessary that what emerges from it to the mouth of the womb be tied to the woman's hip. Then administer an enema to her with the four-drug ointment. For it will putrefy it after days, and it will dissolve, and it will emerge.
However, when it putrefies, a bad odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and harms the weak one. Therefore, suitable fumigations should be administered to it.
Some of the ancients have already experienced fumigation with nasturtium and dried figs.
Form of the instrument with which to fumigate a woman in retention of the Secundinae and menses.
Let it be made of glass similar to alabaster, or of earthenware: and let the wide end be placed over the fire: and let the fumigation be placed over the prunus: let it hold it, until that fumigation is consumed: and again another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the treatment of an unperforated anus.
Many children are born with their anuses unperforated, which a fine sifac has already obstructed. Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to pierce that sifac with her finger; and if not, to pierce it with a sharp spatula; and let her be careful not to touch the flesh.
Then place upon it wool soaked in wine and oil. Then care for it with ointments until it is healed.
If, however, you fear that it will become obstructed, then place a lead cannula in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wishes to evacuate.
And now, obstruction of the anus also happens from the consolidation of a wound, or an abscess. Therefore, it is necessary that that consolidation be cut, then treated with what we said concerning treatment and lead.
Chapter Eighty.
Concerning the treatment of Fistulas that occur in the lower part.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part are swellings and enlargements that occur near the anus externally, or in the space of one of the parts: and it is one fistula, or several.
When that swelling has become old, it opens, and watery white moisture, or thin pus, runs from it.
And sometimes there are penetrating fistulas from these. Therefore, penetrating ones are recognized by that which emerges from excretion and windiness, when the sick person uses excretion: and perhaps worms emerge from them.
And some of them are fistulas when, being penetrating, they are in the space leading to the bladder, and to the urethra. And some of them are penetrating as far as the hip joint, and as far as the tailbone.
By this, however, a penetrating fistula to the anus is recognized, even from a non-penetrating one, that you insert your index finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe into the fistula, from air or iron; when there is no winding.
If there is winding in it, then insert into it a thin probe of lead, or a hair from a horse's mane, until you feel the probe or hair in your finger.
If you do not feel it completely, and nothing emerges from the opening in terms of expulsion, nor wind, nor worm as I said, then know that it is not penetrating; therefore, hasten to treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrating to the bladder, or to the urinary meatus, then its indication is the discharge of urine from it, and its prevention from this that the place be consolidated with medicines.
If however it is penetrating to the hip joint, or to the hip bone, then its sign is that the probe reaches that place, if there is no winding to the bone; and the discovery of pain in the surrounding area, and that pus emerges from it always; and that no medication works on it, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas, there is no cure at all; and their treatment is labor and vanity for him, from foolish physicians, upon whom it is formed.
However, in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing is hoped for with a saw, according to what I tell you, and I have already proven; and it is that the sick person lies on their back in your hands; and raise their legs above their hips, and their hips are inclined toward their stomach. Then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is a winding in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
If, however, the sick person feels it around the anus, then you should introduce your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel the probe has already penetrated itself, detected, beyond what you feel between your finger and the fistula, or flesh: then you know for certain that it is penetrating; therefore do not labor on it, for there is no cure for it, as we said.
However, they say that in some people the flesh heals, and from the medication from which they hope for help, it is that you heat a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as before, and introduce it heated into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat it twice or three times, until you know that all those added filtered flesh that are like the hollow quill of a bird are already burned.
Then treat it with lint wrapped in butter, until those burned flesh come out; then treat it with ointments that generate flesh and consolidate it.
If therefore it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it will never heal with any other treatment.
And if the probe emerges, and does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh between it and the probe, or if such a thing happens, and you see a fistula in what follows the skin surface, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you proceed cutting along with the probe that is in the fistula, until the cut reaches where the probe's end reaches, and the probe is released and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered tissues, which resemble the hollow of a bird's feather, so that nothing remains of them.
However, if blood overcomes you, and separates you from your operation as you cut those tissues, then it is better, and more quickly helpful, to cauterize it with fire, or with a sharp medicine.
Because cautery with fire gathers good qualities, to burn those added tissues, and to cut off blood, and to dry out the moistnesses.
Then treat it with bandages soaked in butter, or with crushed herbs, until the place discharges pus, and those burned tissues are discharged in the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments, which produce hard flesh: and there are medicines in which are astringency and drying.
For when it is restored, then it is already healed: and you do not fear a recurrence.
If however the fistula has already reached the depth of the anus, and extends from the body's superfluity: then insert your finger into the anus, and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel a tension, and between you and it is a veil of sinew or flesh, and it is close to penetration: then there is no operation on it, except according to the path of trust and hope.
That is, because one of three ways is administered in it: either to cauterize it itself, as we said: or to dissect it, until you reach its concavity, so that it is possible for you to insert ligatures, and a cure from close by. And do not proceed far with the section, so that you do not cut the muscle containing the anus, and cause a sudden exit of excrement from the sick without their will. Then treat it with what we have said.
Perhaps it will be healed. Or you will extend the veil and cleanse it after you have cut it close to the anus, as we said, either with a probe or with another instrument with a sharp end. Then cleanse those filtered tissues that are in the section, until you can reach it with exploration.
Then attempt the restoration of the entire wound with the incision and the upper opening where it consolidates; and a fistula will remain open in the interior part of the anus, and it will be less severe on the sick person.
And sometimes the fistula is sutured in this way: and it is, when you insert a probe into the fistula; and it is on the side of the anus around the surface of the body with the skin and the margin of the anus; then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like an alef needle, that is, for preparing skins, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wrapped with five threads, and so on. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its concavity.
If, therefore, it penetrates the inner margin of the anus nearby, then pull the thread out of that opening; so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and bring the two ends together; and tighten each, and leave it for one or two days; and the more the thread cuts into the flesh, the more tightly you should tighten it, until those parts of flesh between the two ends of the thread are cut and fall off; then treat the wound until it consolidates and heals.
If however the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For it is not in your power to do that for fear of injuring it.
Then do with it as we have said, until it heals.
Shape the pointed spatula with which the fistula is cut, whose curvature is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision: and the treatment of fissures.
There are hemorrhoids in two ways. For they are either inside the anus, like the bladders of carbon, or of prunes: or as if they were themselves grapes. And from them are small, and large: and blood flows from them always, and there is one, and there are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those which are outside the anus, are for the most part few in moisture, from which flows yellow water, or little blood with continuous flow: and they are according to the colour of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you command the sick person to sit; and it is squeezed, until it emerges from the anus, and the athelil appear to you. Therefore, also suspend them with hooks: or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut them at their roots.
If, however, the hooks are not retained in them, because of their moisture and their softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with a cloth from double-layered wool, and extract them with your fingers. Then cut them and, after the incision, sprinkle some hot powders over them, so that they will stand in place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what has preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
If the anus does not yield to expulsion, then inject the sick person with an enema containing a small amount of mordication, so that it washes away what is in the anus, and yields to expulsion quickly, when the sick person expels it.
If, however, the piles are external to the anus, then their removal is easy; and it is to grasp them with your fingernail, or grasp them with a hook, and cut them. Then treat them with what we have said, until they are healed.
And whoever shuns incision with iron, then it is necessary to treat their sutures in this manner. Take a wrapped thread, and insert it into the needle. Then pull the pile upward, and cause it to penetrate with the needle at its root on the other side; and wrap the two ends of the thread, at the lower part of the needle, and let them be lengthwise; and bind the pile with a strong constriction. Then tie the thread, and remove the needle.
Do this with all the piles, and leave one of them; nor tie it, so that superfluity of blood may run from it.
Then place a cloth soaked in rose or cedar oil over the anus, and place it over the anus; and command the sick person to rest. Then leave it until it falls.
When therefore the piles fall, treat them with ointments and the other treatment, until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of Fissures. Indeed, they often occur from the drying of the stool and constriction of the nature.
When therefore they become old and medicine is of no avail in them, then it is necessary that you scrape them with a pointed spatula, or with your fingernail, until they become moist, and the upper crust that prevents their consolidation is removed. Then treat them until they are consolidated as is necessary.
If however they do not consolidate, then scrape them again with a more forceful instrument than the first, until they bleed much, and are excoriated, then treat them until they heal.
Concerning the treatment of inverted and non-inverted almirmars, and of dry athelils, and of ants.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that an Almirmar is nothing other than a round swelling the colour of the body, similar to the head of a nail, and it occurs throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes, and pain arises from them when walking.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut what is around the nail, and to hold it with scissors, or with a hook, and to uproot it from its root.
Then treat it, and if you wish, cauterize it according to what was stated in its chapter.
From dry athelil, one is a small, rough, filtered, round eminence above the surface of the body. And they frequently occur on the hands of children.
And its treatment is to extend the athelil and cut it; or to bind it with silk thread; or with a hair, until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been previously stated with fire, or with a sharp medicine; and now inverted athelil are eradicated, and not inverted with the instrument that I shall name.
A formica, however, is also a small, filtered, thick eminence above the body's surface, penetrating deeply; and when cold finds it, a sensation similar to an ant bite is felt in it, and it spreads throughout the body; and most of it occurs on the hands.
And its treatment is to take a cannula from a goose's feather, or a strong vulture's feather; and to place that cannula over the Formica until the cannula's circle contains its parts. Then turn your hand with the cannula until you sever what is around the Formica; and place it into the depths of the flesh, and eradicate the Formica from its root.
And if you wish, do it with a cannula of air, or iron, according to this form.
Let the cannula's top be at a height that pulls the enclosed part upward, so that the grasping and turning of it and itself might be lightened.
And if you wish, cauterize the Formica according to what was previously stated in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter Eighty-three.
Concerning the forms of instruments administered for enemas in diseases of the anus, and for bowel evacuation, and colic.
Sometimes an enema is made of silver, or of bronze, or of cast brass, or of hammered brass.
And sometimes it is made from these instruments, small and large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore, that which is administered in the treatment of young children should be small; and likewise their enemas, for those whose anuses are weak or painful.
Large enema.
Its tube, that is, the upper end where the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and it should have a distinction so that the bag is tied as you see, and its lower end, which is inserted into the anus, should be closed, that is, not open, smooth, very much so, drawn to a thinness; and on one side of it should be two openings, and on the other side one opening.
And the size of the opening should be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker; and the bag, in which the medicines are placed, should be of an animal bladder, or of parchment, which should be according to the shape of a small asofrat, the root of which is one palm.
And it should be pierced with many holes as it is rolled, and the space between each hole should be the thickness of a finger. Then insert a strong thread, made of ten threads or so, through those holes, and attach the bag to it like an asofrat. When the bag is thus placed in it, the top of the enema should be tightened between two distinguishing constrictions with a strong constriction. Then irrigate with it.
And this is the shape of the bag depicted here, and of the holes.
The method of irrigation with it is: fill the bag, or bladder, with medicine that has been heated according to the patient's tolerance; and beware that it is not overly hot or overly cold.
Then the patient should lie on their back, and raise their legs upwards; and place a cushion beneath them, that is, a hide upon which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the tip of the enema with oil, or with egg white, or with the mucilage of fenugreek, or similar things. Then insert it into the anus with ease. Then let the attendant grasp the bag with both hands simultaneously, and express it with force, until everything in the enema is evacuated into the intestines.
Then remove the enema, and let the patient raise their legs towards the wall.
If the medicine compels him to an outcome, then let him hold it as long as possible. And if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
If, however, you administer an enema for ailments of loose bowels, and its exit is hasty, then administer an enema again, until he is healed.
If there are hemorrhoids or damaging abscesses in the anus, then let the enema be very thin, gentle, from enemas with which children are enematized.
And this should be the form of a thin enema, above which the skin should be tight.
Chapter LXXXIV.
I have already said in the division of the treatment of wounds that it was suitable for the book, and I will now describe their treatment with the hand, and with medicines, according to its completion.
Therefore, I say that wounds are diversified according to the means by which they are made, and according to the place on which the wound falls.
The things by which wounds are often made are, like the throwing of a stone; or the incision of a sword, or a knife; or the impact with a spear, or wood, or an arrow; and by similar things from many such.
And the wounds that are made according to the places of the body are, like the wound that falls upon the head, or the neck, or the chest, or the belly, or the liver; and similar ones from those members.
And I am narrating the treatments of certain wounds, that I may set forth the reasoning and rule concerning the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple wounds of the head, because the recounting of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs on the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: consider then, if it is from a stone's throw or similar things, and has only broken the skin: and is a large wound, and you fear a bad outcome of a hot abscess: then hurry, and perform a cephalic phlebotomy immediately; and do not delay it; and draw from his blood according to the amount of his strength: if nothing forbids it from the phlebotomy, especially if the blood that emerges from the wound is little. And let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the sick person beware of repletion, and wine, and heavy foods.
If however something prevents diminution, then let the food be lessened, and let him not approach meats, nor wines.
And if a hot abscess happens to him, apply a poultice soaked in rose oil alone, or with wine that contains astringency, over the wound.
If however you are certain of the absence of a hot abscess, then apply, if it is recent with its own blood, and the air has not changed it, the powder I will describe shortly; and if the air has already changed it, then apply certain ointments until it is healed.
If however the wound is large, or is from a sword cut, and similar things; and its lips do not come together by means of pledgets, then bring them together with stitches, according to what I will describe regarding the sutures of belly wounds.
If however the wound is already exposed to the bone, and hangs, and its retention is only in a small suspensory, then cut it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore the hard flesh in place of the skin, and instruct the sick person to eat foods with bulk, such as heads of grain, and cooked wheat, until it is like a pulp; and similar things.
If you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all the putrefied part, and cast it out; then treat it.
If however in these wounds there is an artery, or vein from which blood flows; and it is not stopped by remedies, then investigate the artery. If therefore you find it not severed, then cut it with a Spatumili, or tie it. If necessity compels when that which we have said does not help, then cauterize it until the blood is stopped.
If however these wounds are small, simple, then their treatment is easy, light; so that you powder over them this powder, because it consolidates them before air changes them.
Method of the flesh-producing powder. Take one part of frankincense, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of quicklime: thoroughly grind the whole, and sift it: and fill the wound with it, and bind it tightly with a good bandage, until it adheres well to it, and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it from above with bandages, and leave it, and do not unbind it while it remains, so that it does not fester. For the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes lime alone does it, when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood present, and the latter also performs the same operation in many large wounds, when it is appropriate to put it on them and bind them, and are fresh wounds with their own blood, which air has not altered.
If it has already been altered by some alteration of the air, especially when it is summer, then the application of this powder is not effective. Therefore, treat it with what we have said, by applying some digestive ointments to it, or place poultices of barley flour, made with water and honey, until it discharges pus. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
However, if a fracture occurs with a bone wound, and it is small, then extract it with forceps; and I will now describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Concerning wounds of the Neck.
However, if a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in treatment between it and a simple head wound.
However, if the nerve of the neck, or artery, has already been cut: then if it is a nerve, there is no skill in it, except that its treatment should be avoided, along with anything that creates strong astringency, such as ziniar, and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm the nerves and contract them.
And do not put anything cold on the wound at all: because the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is higher than the limbs: and let that by which it is treated be gentle, such as lime washed with sweet water, infused with oil sometimes, or with rose oil, and tuttia, when washed also with sweet water; and the remaining mineral stones in this manner, and moist ointments.
If however the wound is large, then administer a suture: or narrow its edges with pledgets. Then treat it until it heals.
If however the wound has depth, or there occurs a sinus in its lower part, in which pus is already collected, then pierce it in the more depressed place in it.
If indeed an artery has been cut in the wound, and its blood flow has resulted, then separate or cut it, and tie it: or cauterize it, if necessity prompts it.
If however the windpipe has been cut, or the entire one, and the other parts have been saved, then join the skin edges with a suture over the windpipe, and do not touch the windpipe, rather straighten it, and bring it back to its natural form. Then bind it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for a few days. Then treat it with ointments containing astringency and desiccation, until it is healed.
If however the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then investigate it: then if there are bone fragments within it, extract all or as many as you can of them: and what cannot be removed, leave it, until the wound rots, and its discharge is separated and relieved.
If the wound is recent, then powder over it the powder I have described. If however bone is seen in the wound, and it becomes a fistula, then treat it with what is recounted in its chapter.
Concerning wounds in the shoulder blades.
If there is a blow from a lance or a knife, and you see its depth, then consider. If therefore wind issues from the wound, when the sick person gasps from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
But if it is not deep, and is fresh with its own blood, then do not put powder on it from the first hour, nor bind it, so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the sick person; but put attracting ointment on it and loosen it twice a day.
If however the ointment is not present for you, then put an old cloth in the wound opening, so that it will suck that which emerges from it of the humors; and make the sick person sleep over the wound, so that that which is gathered in it may run.
However, if three days or more have already passed since the wound, and no spasm has occurred in the sick person, nor bad heart tremor, nor constriction in breathing, and you see the rest of their condition good, then know that the wound is safe. Therefore treat it with bandages and the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
If however its healing is excused, and it continually opens, then know that it has now become a fistula. Therefore treat it from its own chapter.
And if the wound is from the incision of a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what preceded regarding sutures, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
If however it has already made an impression on the bone, and chipped pieces from it: then investigate the wound, and hasten to extract those pieces, if they are detached. If however they are not detached, then leave them until the wound putrefies, for the extraction of them will be alleviated.
However, the treatment of other accidental wounds in other limbs is judged by the judgment that we have said.
Concerning wounds of the belly, and the exit of the intestines, and their expulsion.
The rupture which occurs in the belly is great, medium, and small.
From the great one, therefore, sometimes the intestine, or a number of intestines, emerges.
Therefore, their introduction, and their more vehement and difficult suturing, occurs.
And a small rupture also becomes difficult in another way. Therefore it follows, that their reduction is more difficult if you do not hasten their introduction to their place immediately, since it is inflated, opened, and thickened: therefore its entrance becomes difficult.
For this reason, therefore, the middle rupture of ruptures is better, because with it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in these two types.
Know that the suturing of the belly is according to four ways.
Two sutures common to the suturing of the belly and the suturing of the other wounds of the body, and two other sutures properly belong to other wounds. However, they are more properly associated with the suturing of the belly, and I will describe each with explanation and clarification.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small, and something from the intestine protrudes from it, and it becomes difficult to reduce it, then that is due to one of two ways. Either because of the smallness of the rupture, as we said, or because there is inflation in the intestine due to the cold air.
Therefore, when this is so, then it is necessary to warm it, just as you would immerse a sponge or a damp cloth in lukewarm water alone. Or boil it in water with squinancy, cyperus, and spike, and apply it to the intestine with it until the inflation resolves.
Sometimes wine with astringency does this, and it is better than plain water in resolving the inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow or mallow has been boiled. For by that, its entrance is eased with a small thing.
If however its return is excused after this treatment, then make a small incision in the rupture with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when it is prepared, than the incision; and no coercion is made to cut after necessity and instrument failure.
Let one part of it be twisted, sharp, and the other part not sharp, and let the slender extremity not be with the slenderness of a spatula, but rather somewhat blunter: and it is that which is similar to a cabbage leaf, as you see.
Therefore, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its proper place, if possible over it; because it is better.
When, however, the rupture is large in the lower part of the belly, then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your attention and your consideration always on this, that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you administer it in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
In small wounds, however, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then have a pious attendant present between your hands, who should hold the entire rupture with his hand, and draw together its lips. Then uncover from it for the one who follows the suture something after something: and this is the common method of a single suture.
And it is that you should take a needle, or a number of needles according to the quantity of the wound's breadth. Then release from the extremity of the rupture a quantity of the ring finger, that is, the physician's: and fix one needle past that into which you insert thread at both ends of the skin, with the two ends of it (the thread) being what is under the skin from the insides, until you cause it to penetrate from the other side, and you have already brought together the two extremities of the skin, and the two ends of it (the thread): and four rows are made.
Then tighten with a double thread in the circumference of the needle several times from both sides simultaneously, until the two lips of the wound are brought together in a decent aggregation.
Then release the thickness of a finger also, and fix another needle in them; afterward, thread it with the thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease to do that with the needles you need until you complete the constriction of the entire wound.
And let the needles be intermediate in thickness and thinness; because very thin needles pierce flesh very quickly, and thick ones are difficult to enter the skin; for that reason, they ought to be intermediate in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed in the two close extremities of the skin very much, so that the flesh is not pierced quickly and the wound opened before it is consolidated, and also not to be extended too far with the suture, so that the wound is not prevented from consolidating.
Then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not harm the sick person in their sleep; and place cushions of cloth on every side, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is now consolidated.
And this type of suture with needles is indeed more suitable in small wounds. Because one, or two, or approximately that number of needles is sufficient for their suture.
However, the second common suture is to gather together with the suture four extremities: two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the stuff, at once, with a needle in which is wrapped a thread equal in thinness and thickness.
Then, when you have pierced with the needle these four extremities, withdraw the needle from the side from which you began, in the same way, so that the thread drawn back falls from the upper part of the wound; so that there is a suture, like a suture of cloth with which cloths are tied together, and place between each suture an amount of thickness less than a little finger.
However, men know this suture.
And with this suture I sewed up a wound that had occurred to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the wound rupture was greater than one palm: and approximately two palms of the middle intestine had come out, and there was a rupture in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I returned it after his intestine had stood outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
Therefore the wound was consolidated in a space of approximately fifteen days: and I cured him, and he was healed; and he lived after that many years, engaged in his affairs.
And the physicians had judged that it would not be healed.
And it was miraculous that I did not cure it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment, nor anything of the medicaments, was to be found. Therefore I placed an old linen cloth over the wound twice a day, and I diligently washed it with honey water, until it was healed.
I will now describe two specific types of sutures, according to the account of the text.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp suture: and introduce the needle from the exterior to the interior part.
When therefore the needle penetrates the skin, and going into the muscle, along the length and breadth of the entire body: leave the extremity thus on the side in which you inserted the needle, and cause it to penetrate on the other extremity from the interior part to the exterior on the other extremity mirach.
When you have caused it to penetrate, then cause it to penetrate according to this on the same extremity mirach from the exterior part to the interior: and leave the extremity sifac, which is on this side, and cause the needle to penetrate the other extremity from the interior part to the exterior: and cause it to penetrate with your penetration, in which you cause it to penetrate on the sifac extremity mirach, which is on its part, until it penetrates the whole thing.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the extremity, which is from sifac on the other side, and draw the needle out of the skin which it has penetrated.
Then reduce it into that skin, and sew the extremity sifac, which is on the other side, with this extremity mirach: and draw it out of the skin which is from its part, and do it one time, and another: and do it turn by turn, until you have sewn the whole exterior according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that it should be like the suture of leather workers when they sew hides: so that you should sew sifac at one time from one part of one skin's extremity: and that you should leave it at another time from another part, until you complete it.
Second species of proper suture, which Galen said.
His discourse recounts that one should sew it according to the likeness in which certain physicians sew them, in such a way that they gather every part to its suitable counterpart by nature: and they apply the extremity sifac to its other extremity, and the extremity mirach to its other extremity. And that is done according to what I tell you.
One must place the needle in the extremity of mirach nearest to you from the outer side, and cause it to penetrate to the inner part in that alone; and leave the two extremities of sifac. Then turn the needle, and cause it to penetrate from the outer to the inner side in the two extremities of sifac, namely on both sides.
Then turn both, also causing both to penetrate from the inside to the outside, into the two other extremities of mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and is a suture whose remembrance precedes.
This is therefore the speech of Galen also.
And some said. The suture of the belly is rectified with two needles. That is, introduce one thread into each of them: and begin with the introduction of the needle, from where it is with you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew skins equally.
And know that when there is a rupture in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than the other places of the belly.
The judgment of the treatment of a wound, is the judgment of the other wounds.
That is, when you obtain a fresh wound with its blood, before the air alters it, and you replace the intestines, and adjust them, and adapt them, then place it on the incarnative powder.
If the air has already altered the wound, then place upon it certain ointments, which are present to you, until they produce pus; and the threads fall away, and the wound is consolidated by means of sifac, and mirach. Then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed.
However, if the medicines are not present to you, then immediately place upon it, from the moment it begins to emit pus, old lint; and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not require any other medication in many dispositions, when the wounds are simple.
If, however, you fear that the wound may communicate with the principal members in the injury, then it is necessary that you immerse soft wool in oil of moderate warmth, or in rose oil, and place it around the areas that are between the groin and under the armpits.
If, however, he feels pain, or prickings, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens), then administer an enema to him with astringent, black, lukewarm wine; especially if the putrefaction has already reached the intestine, and has made a penetrating exit to its concavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing; and that which is from the small intestine is of more difficult healing.
However, the intestine called the Jejunum does not accept healing from a wound that occurs in it at all; and this is because of its multitude of veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
If, however, that which proceeds from the wound is a zirbum, and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce the intestines equally.
If, however, a space has passed it, and it has already become green, or black, then it is necessary to bind it with thread above the place which has been blackened by it, so that a flow of blood does not occur. For in a zirbum there are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligature, and place the two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it, so that the traction of it may be alleviated upon you, and the extraction of it at the occurrence of a zirbum, and the emission of the pus of the wound.
A recollection of the wound that occurred in the Intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it is possible that it might be restored in some people; for this reason, because I saw one who was wounded in his belly with a spear thrust, and there was a wound on the right side of his stomach, and it was an old wound: and it had become a fistula, and excrement and wind came out of it.
Therefore I applied treatment, until I was not confident in his healing; nor did I cease to refine it, until he was healed, and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the sick person, lest an evil accident occur in his belly; and no such evil accident occurred to him from that. And he remained in better dispositions, eating and drinking, and engaging in sexual activity, and entered a bath, and exercised in his daily activities.
