And now some of the experimenters have said that when a wound occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it ought to be bound in this manner. And it is as if you take ants of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place one ant of those which have an open mouth upon the two lips of the wound. When therefore it takes hold of them, and closes its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not detach.
Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease to do this with ant after ant according to the amount of the whole wound.
Then reduce it, and suture the wound. For those heads remain attached in the intestine until the intestine is nourished and healed, and no harm comes to the sick person at all.
And now it is possible that the intestine be tied even with a fine thread: which has been scraped from the animal's intestine, attached to it after it is inserted into the needle.
And it is that you take the end of this thread from the intestine, and it is extensively scraped. Then tie a fine linen thread wrapped around its end. Then insert that thread into the needle, and sew the intestine with it. Then reduce it to the disruption.
And this method of sewing with ants and intestine is only according to the way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound which is large, extensive, especially if it is in one of the small intestines: then there is no remedy but refinement, nor is its healing complete.
Concerning the treatment of ulcers and fistulas.
Know that any wound, or abscess, when it grows old and becomes an ulcer, and does not heal, and constantly discharges pus, and is not cut off: then it is universally called a fistula, wherever in the body it is, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula, in truth, is a filtered, hard, white knot, with no pain, having the hollow cavity of a bird's feather; and because of that, some call it Risati, that is, feather.
And it is in some hours moist, which discharges pus. And perhaps the moisture is cut off in some hours. And sometimes this moisture is abundant, and sometimes it is scant; and sometimes it is thick, and sometimes it is thin.
And I have already mentioned in the division, when I said about wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal, nor flesh grows in it, is not so except for one of nine causes.
Of which one is, either because of the scarcity of blood in the body; or because of its bad quality; or because between it and the edges of it is hard flesh, preventing the growth of flesh; or because there is much filth and dirt; or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter that is applied to it is of bad quality; or because the medicine is inappropriate for its treatment; or because the corruption descending on the region is a kind of pestilence, or by a property in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta, in which there is a difficulty in digesting illnesses, and the healing of abscesses by nature is delayed; or because there is one bone, or a number of bones, in it.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their cures with medicines in the division: and it is necessary here to make you know the cure of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by means of surgery with the hand.
Know therefore that Fistula occurs in all members of the body. For of them are fistulas which reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinews; or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to any of the ribs; or to any of the vertebrae of the back, or to any of the joints of compound compositions, such as the hand and foot; and of them are those which go to the vicinity of the main member, and similar ones.
Therefore, whatever of these fistulas is of this kind, is of illnesses difficult to heal, and does not receive a cure, and dealing with it is labor and ignorance.
If there is any fistula whose depth does not reach these places which I have said: and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to attain knowledge of it, then take a probe of air or iron, if the fistula tends in a straight line, and search for it with it.
If however there is twisting in the fistula, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead. Because lead, with the lightness of its body, is alleviated and is bent around the twisting.
If the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to mark it with a probe. Therefore, irrigate it from its openings.
For the moisture you irrigate it with, goes towards the other openings, and runs out of them.
Then, inquire carefully in what way it is possible for you to know whether there is a bone or nerve, and the fistula cavity is distant or near; or if it is a single fistula with many openings, and stand over the entire area with all your strength; with your questioning, in which you question the sick person, and the discovery of pain upon compression with your hand on the area, and with similar signs and causes of accidents relating to abscesses.
Therefore, once you have stood over all those areas correctly, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  And consider whether the fistula is a near, apparent fistula, or in a safe place far from a joint, or a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the locations I have mentioned to you; then cut the fistula as previously described in my explanation, and remove what is within it by filtration, and the corrupted flesh, that grows in its opening from flesh, or boils, and similar things; and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the fistula is of distant, deep location, and is in a straight line, then you must cut it as deeply as possible. Then cleanse it from all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply bandages soaked in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula that you have grasped with the iron.  Do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine has consumed all that remains in the fistula cavity from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If, however, it does not heal by that, then cauterize it according to what we have described.
If the cause of the fistula is bone, and this is ascertained by you, then investigate it and cut it as I have told you, if nothing from a vein, nerve, or main limb prevents you, as we have said.
If bone is discovered by you, and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until all its corruption is removed. Then treat it with that which causes it to flesh over, until it is healed.
If it does not heal, and discharges pus as it did, then know that you have not achieved the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore, expose it again, and meticulously perform its scraping and cleansing with all your strength.
Then restore it. If it heals, well and good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in a distant deep part of the body, which you have not reached. Therefore, you have no skill in it, except to leave it to nature.
If the small bone is decayed, and it is possible for you, extract it with fine forceps suitable for it.
If, however, there are many bones, then carefully extract all of them, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If, however, any moisture offers resistance to you, and there is no fear or error in cutting them: then cut those moistures and flesh, if there is any, and free the bones.
If, however, you are not prepared to remove the bones from your time, then place over the wound something that will putrefy it, and leave it until that which is around those bones from the flesh and moistures is putrefied.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And constrict the incision that you made in your operation. Indeed, place over it, if you fear it, a cloth soaked in ground sulfur mixed with oil, or a cloth soaked in Egyptian unguent, or any green unguents.
For when those humidities, namely, putrefy, and the bones are separated from them, the removal and extraction of them is eased.
If however there is one large bone, such as the bone of the leg or the bone of the hip, and similar to them, and that which is corrupt from it is a large part: then scrape it with the utmost scraping until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
If however that which is corrupt within it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to saw it, and cut it completely up to the place where the corruption has reached: then treat it until it is consolidated.
And I narrate to you a case, which happened to a man in his leg: that you may set it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man about thirty years of age, who suffered pain in his leg, from a cause which originated within his body, that is, from the interior, until the matter reached his leg, and it became a large abscess: and there was no external cause.
Therefore, time passed with the physicians' error, until the abscess was opened, and many matters ran together with it. And his treatment was delayed, until the leg became fistulous, and many openings were made in it: which all emitted pus and bodily humours.
Therefore, a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no one skilled in the art of medicine among them, until I was called, and saw his leg, and the matters that were running from those openings with great force: even the entire man's body was already wasted, and his colour was yellowish.
Therefore, I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I probed all the openings, and found them to reach each other from all parts of the leg.
I therefore hastened and cut over one of those openings, until I exposed a part of the bone: and I found it corrupted: which was already gnawed, and blackened, and rotten, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
I therefore cut what was revealed to me, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I judged that there was in the bone only that corruption which I had cut away and sawn. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I placed the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it was not consolidated.
Then I tried, and I discovered a second bone above the first discovery: and I found continuous corruption with the bone: and I sawn what appeared to me to be also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore, I again uncovered it, and did not cease to cut the bone piece by piece, and to attempt its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut approximately one palm's breadth from the bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was quickly consolidated and healed: and this repetition in its operation and section was not necessary, except because of the disposition of the weakened patient, and his/her lack of tolerance, and my fear for him/her of death: since it happened to him/her at all hours from an excess of evacuative syncopes, which were bad.
Therefore, he/she was healed with complete healing: and hard flesh grew in the place of the bone, and a good disposition was established in his/her body, and his/her strength returned; and he/she was restored to his/her previous disposition; and no harm was done to him/her in walking at all.
If, however, a prominent bone in the body has already been fractured, then it is necessary to saw it, according to this method: and it is that you should take a ligature, and tighten it at the extremity of the prominent bone; and instruct someone to raise it upward; and place another thicker wool ligature over the first ligature. Then tie it over the flesh which is under the bone; and extend its extremity, so as to draw the flesh downward; and you uncover the flesh over the place you wish to saw, so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place wood, or a plank, underneath the bone below properly, because when you do this, it does not prevent the sawing of the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupted place a little, so that there is no corruption in the concavity of the bone; and it does not appear in its appearance to the senses, and compel sawing of another place.
If the bone is corrupted, and not prominent, but rather its part continues with another part; and the corruption is in its middle, or in a part of it: then uncover the flesh from all sides entirely. Then place wood below.
Afterwards saw it from the first side, where the corruption is, until the sawing continues on the other side; and let the sawing be along the length from the corruption a little, as we have said.
If the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it continues on the other side.
If, however, the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no remedy in it except abrasion.
If the corruption is in the comb of the hand, or in the comb of the foot, then the matter is very difficult in it. But you must investigate how the corruption appears to you. And abrade it, and cleanse it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever remedy is rectified for you: when it does not cause you opposition from the vein or nerve.
And know that there are many incisions and saws for cutting these bones, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness and thinness, and their size and smallness, and their hardness and their rarity. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare an instrument suitable for that operation for every type of operation.
And know that these operations show you the type of instrument you need when you have prolonged study with you. And knowledge of the methods of this art is a witness to the methods of illnesses: for then you will already find for yourself that which is appropriate for it from instruments for every ailment.
And I am establishing for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments, which you will set as examples upon which you will make incisions, and the test by which you will experiment upon others.

And it does not agree, unless the heads of the joints are rubbed with it, when they are corrupted, or the bone is large and great.

This is similar to a saw, and its end is like a saw; it is shown in many places from the reason of bones.
Shape of the razor suitable for the scraping of that which is perforated from the bones.
Its end is triangular, with sharp edges.
It is made of Indian (metal). And similarly, it is necessary that all that we have said be made of razors, and knives, and saws.
Concerning the incision of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
Extremities putrefy, either from an external cause, or an internal one.
Therefore, when you have treated that corruption with medicinal remedies, and the treatment does not help; and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, to which nothing responds; then it is necessary to cut that limb as far as the corruption extends, so that the afflicted person escapes death, or a greater harm which is from the lack of the limb.
And a sign that this occurs is that the limb darkens until it stinks; that fire has burned it, or it rots after darkening; until that rot is cut away down to the healthy part of the limb, and spreads to the whole body.
Therefore, act quickly with its incision, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of some venomous creatures, such as a sea scorpion, a viper, a rattlesnake, and similar ones.
If therefore the corruption, or the injury, is in the extremity of the finger, then cut off the finger, and do not give space to the corruption, so that it advances to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut it off from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption, until it takes hold in the forearm.
And if it invades it, then cut off the arm at the elbow in its joint.
If however the corruption passes, and you see it approximately at the elbow, then in that is the death of the weak one. And to administer beyond that from the cure, is a measure of virtue.
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption invades the toe, then cut it off at one of the joints; and if it takes hold in the arch of the foot, then cut off the entire foot. If however it ascends to the knee, then cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If however the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no skill in it, except dismissal, and leaving the sick person to death.
And the method of cutting or sawing off a limb is such that you should tighten the ligament below which you want to cut, and tighten another ligament above the place. And let the attendant extend one ligament downward, and let the attendant extend the other ligament upward. And you should separate the flesh which is between the two ligaments, with a broad spatula, until the entire flesh is exposed. Then it should be cut or sawed.
And it is necessary that you place linen cloths on all sides, and that the saw does not touch a healthy part; and that the infirm person suffers additional injury, and a hot abscess.
If, however, internal bleeding occurs during your work, then cauterize the place quickly, or place some powders that stop the bleeding upon it. Then return to your medication, until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I tell you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident that I am telling you.
Because it happened in his foot a blackness, with a burning similar to the combustion of fire: and that corruption first happened in his toe, until it began the entire foot.
Therefore the man hastened himself, after seeing the corruption fall upon the limb with its vehemence, which he found from its pain and burning: and it struck him at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time passed, that same corruption happened to him in the index finger of his hand.
Therefore he came to me, and I tried to remove that excess with what I put on his hand from medicines after the cleansing of his body: and the excess was not repelled, and it began to proceed to another finger, until the corruption began in his hand.
Therefore he called me to cut his hand: and I did not want that, hoping from me about the repulsion of that excess: and I also feared for him at the cutting of his hand death. Because the man's strength was over the case.
After he therefore despaired of me, he returned to his land: and it came to me about him, that he hastened, and cut his whole hand: and he was healed.
And I have not brought this account, unless that it may be a help concerning that which falls from the genus of this sickness: and that it may be a sign by which it may be signified, and according to which it may be done.
Concerning the treatment of Sinuses, and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members, and the space of the abscess prolongs itself until it collects pus: then it breaks open, or is pierced, and that which is in it of pus emerges, and a hollow space remains, as if it were a vessel; and the skin which is above it is like a cloth which has already been thinned out; and it does not reach the ultimate stage in its corruption, nor does it impinge upon the mouth, nor the nerve, nor the ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus; and it is not called a Fistula: however, if its space is prolonged, until the corruption impinges upon any of these members, then it is called a Fistula, or a zuken.
And its treatment is to cut away that entire skin, as it is rolled up; and especially when it has been thinned out, and made like a cloth; and you are confident that it does not adhere to the place, because of its corruption.
However, if you hope that the skin adheres, and that corruption does not result from that ultimate consequence; and if there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with an enema; and it is, that you should consider the Sinus.
If it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it has a foul odor, then enema it with Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, and combining them in a vessel; and they should be boiled over the fire until the medicine turns red; and it comes to the consistency of honey.
Then take from it what you need; and dissolve it with water and honey, and enema the Sinus with it; and constrict its orifice, and leave the medicine in it for a quantity of two hours.
Then draw it out with pressure; do that for so many days, until the Sinus is cleansed, and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is enemated with ashes water, when this medicine is not present to you; and it is, that you should take ashes of vine wood, or ashes from holly wood, and pour water over it. Then strain it, and enema with it, until you are confident that the Sinus is now cleansed.
If the sick person does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor the water of ashes, then enema him with water and honey, to which some crushed ziniar has already been mixed. Or enema him with mixed honey and wine, since the property of honey is to cleanse and wash away; and wine causes the Sinus to adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this several times, and are confident that the corruption of the Sinus is now removed, then enema him with that which causes flesh to grow in it.
And it is that you take from the diafinicon plaster, and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or enema him with some medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written in the treatise on ointments.
If the opening of the Sinus is narrow, into which the enema cannula does not fit, then widen it a little with iron, or place in it a linen cloth wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in Basil ointment, until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is also wide, then bring together its lips with sutures, and leave in it a quantity into which the enema will enter without addition.
And similarly, if the opening from which pus runs is elevated upwards, then cut it in the lower place within it, so that pus runs from it downwards; because pus, when constricted in the depth of the Sinus, prevents flesh from growing there.
If it is not possible for you to cut the sinus roughly below it as you wish, then try to prepare the limb in such a way that pus runs from it easily according to how it is prepared for you. Try to prevent any pus from being retained there at all, and take consolidating medicines that are placed on a mark, appropriate to what you wish. And spread them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such a size that it covers the entire sinus. Then, with scissors, cut a hole directly over the opening of the sinus, slightly larger than the sinus opening. Then spread some mild ointments onto another cloth, according to the size of the hole, and place it over that in a position slightly distant from the sinus opening, so that the pus from it may be alleviated; and do not remove the large cloth, unless after a few days, at one time.
However, the one over the sinus opening is the one you remove every hour, so that what accumulates in it of pus may come out; and so that you may know whether the matter flowing from it, the virus, is much or little; or digested or undigested.
And with this, again consider the location of the sinus itself, whether its master feels pain there, or if there is no pain there, and whether there is an abscess there, or if there is not an abscess there.
Therefore, when your treatment proceeds thus, and you see the matter that emerges from the sinus opening, then know that the sinus is now adhering, or approaching adherence; then, therefore, loosen it in the morning, or on the third day; and when you loosen the wound, then change the cloth and renew the ointment.
However, if a thin virus emerges from the sinus after a long period, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is approaching, since among people are those who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and among them are those who act contrary to that.
If, however, undigested pus emerges from the sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If, therefore, the adherence of the sinus is delayed and its matter prolonged, then add in the drying of the medicines, but let the physicians arrange that the medicines are humid in their substance and dry in their virtue, like the unguentum dyafinicon, when superfluous addition of zegi is added to it.
And from the most ultimate of its cures is that you take the unguentum dyafinicon, and let it be dissolved with rose oil, and let ancient wine be sprinkled over it equal in its substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And it does a similar action to this, when the unguentum dyafinicon is not present for you, honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloes, and frankincense: grind the whole, and powder it over honey while it is over the fire. Then line a cloth over it, and bind it over the sinus; and line it with the honey which you boiled until it thickened; and powder spices over it, and place it over the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes long aristolochia, and the roots of the celestial lily, and the lily, and herb flour, and centaury are administered in it. Use them, simple or combined, grind them, and sieve them: and powder them over the honey which I have described, and use it.
If the sinus condition is prolonged and not cured by this treatment, then know that there is corruption deep within it: or it is already making an impression on the bones, or on the other hard members or ligaments; then treat it by the treatment for fistulas, as previously described.
Concerning the treatment of whitlow, and of a worn-out nail, and of a superimposed finger, and of a section.
A whitlow is an abscess that arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or of the foot; and perhaps it arises on the other fingers.
When therefore its condition is prolonged, and its treatment is neglected, and it becomes an abscess with a hot abscess: or it becomes corrupted and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail; and perhaps it corrupts the whole of it, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul odor; and the end of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
When therefore you treat it, with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not help, then it is necessary that you cut with iron the entire excess that remains of the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cautery is very helpful.
If however the bone is healthy, and the nail also is healthy, and the superfluous parts of the nail are now pressing the flesh inwards, and begin to prick it and injure it, then it is necessary that you place a thin reed under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and drive it upwards; and cut the flesh with ease, and place on that which remains of the flesh burning, corrosive medicines, until the whole of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If however the corruption has already penetrated the bone, then it is necessary to cut the bone and extract it; for it will never be healed while the corrupt bone remains in it.
If you see corrosion and corruption proceeding into the finger, then cut it at one of its joints, according to its previous description. Then treat it until it is healed.
If, however, there is a rubbing or striking of the nail, and a severe pain occurs in it, then it is necessary to first bleed the sick person. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatulum in a transverse section from above.
And beware that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause severe pain to the sick person by that, and it will be a cause for the birth of additional flesh in that place.
Then treat the place with what soothes the pain.
However, the extra finger that arises in some people's hands is perhaps entirely fleshy; and perhaps some have bone in them, and perhaps there is a nail on it; and some arise at the root of the joint, or its origin is in some nodes of the finger. Therefore, the one that arises in some nodes of the finger does not move; and the one that arises near the joint of the finger perhaps does move.
Therefore, the one that is fleshy among them, its incision is easy. That is, cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
However, the treatment of the one whose origin is at the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore, abstain from its incision.
However, in that which arises in the finger at the last knot, then you must first cut its flesh in a round incision, all the way to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for it. Then treat it until it heals.
And the consolidation that occurs between fingers often happens either from the man's birth, that is, it is born with him, or from a wound's consolidation, or from burning by fire, and similar things.
Therefore, you must separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them strips, or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not become quickly consolidated, and separate them; or place a thin sheet of lead between them, until the place has been consolidated as it ought to be.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs between certain fingers and the palm, then cut that consolidation, according to what is prepared and suitable for the member's shape.
About the incision of a defect.
Ulcers are veins wrapped, thick, full of melancholic superfluities that occur in many body members; and their effects are most frequent in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel, work, and carry burdens.
Therefore, it is necessary to administer a shaking of the body from black cholera sometimes with strong shaking.
Then phlebotomize the patient's basilica vein.
However, their treatment with iron, is according to two methods. One of them is that they be cut, and the black blood be drawn out. And the other method is that the vein be lifted, and the whole thing be drawn out.
However, the incision of it is according to this method. First, soak the leg in a great deal of hot water until the thick, foul blood is dissolved. Then, bind the sick person's leg from the upper part of their thigh down to the lower part of their knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then raise, that is, draw the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg up to the top, and the physician must first do this on the sick person. Then cut the nail down to the bottom, until an amount of blood sufficient and which the sick person's strength can tolerate is released.
Afterwards, tie it [the wound]; and order him to abstain from foods that generate black bile; and repeat the evacuation and bloodletting, when the veins are full and it harms the sick person.
Its raising, however, is in this way. Shave the leg of the sick person if it has many hairs on it. Then immerse him in a bath; or apply a warm water embrocation to his leg, until it reddens and the veins swell; or, if a bath is not available, have him exercise vigorously, until the limb is heated.
Then cut the skin in a straight line along the vein: either at its end near the knee, or at its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and scrape the vein from all sides until it is apparent to the senses: and its appearance is that you see it, inclining from red to black. When it therefore emerges from the skin, you see it white, as if it were a string.
Then introduce a probe beneath it, until, when it is lifted and emerges from the skin, suspend it with a gentle, blunt hook.
Then cut another section near that section by three fingers' breadth. Then scrape the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then lift it with the probes, as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then cut another section, or several sections, if you need them. Then lift, and cut it at the very last section near the heel.
Then pull it out to the section that is above it.
And do that until you pull it out from the section that is above, or higher than all the sections, until when it emerges whole: then cut it.
If however it does not obey you in pulling and lifting it: then insert an awl with strong thread doubled, and tie it, and pull it out; or insert a rod beneath it; and revolve your hand in every direction until it emerges. And be careful not to cut it. For if it is cut, it becomes difficult for you to lift it greatly, and harm enters upon the weak from it.
When therefore you have pulled it out whole: then place upon the wound site wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil; and care for it until it is healed.
If the vine is contracted, having a twisting to which there is wrapping to the sides, and it is not as we have said in terms of straightness, then it is necessary to cut along it in every direction of its twist and its points of wrapping.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you have lifted it all: and be careful at your incision upon it not to cut a vein, or wound it: for it will become difficult for you to lift or extract it. Therefore preserve your prudence.
Shape of the incident by which the vine is healed.
Shape of the blind hook.
It does not have a curvature like the other hooks, nor is it sharp at the end so as not to injure a vein: and it is of a thick, gentle flexure, because if it were thin, it would cut a vein with its thinness: indeed, let its thickness be superfluous, as we said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Al-Jazirah and the land of the Arabs, and in arid, hot lands, few in tenderness, that is, in which neither grass nor tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And indeed its generation is from the putrefaction which occurs beneath the skin, just as serpents and ascarid worms and worms generated between the skin and flesh occur.
And a sign of the beginning of this vein's occurrence is that a violent inflammation occurs in the leg. Then the place becomes blistered. Then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant or an animal.
Therefore, when its extremity appears from it, then it is necessary to wrap a piece of lead, weighing from one drachma keil to two drachmae, around it and tie it, and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in lead and tie it. If it extends a long way, then cut part of it and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root before the whole of it emerges. For if you cut it, it retracts and enters the flesh, and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it must be gently handled and pulled out gradually until it emerges from its outermost part and nothing remains of it in the body; and from this vein indeed, in some men, there emerges what is five palms and ten in its length. And it has now come to my attention that it emerged from a man at twenty palms.
However, if it is incised in the time of your medication, then insert a probe into its opening, and pierce it with a long probe, along with the body, until all that is in it of matter is evacuated; and treat the putrefaction of the place with medicines for several days. Then treat it with the treatment for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears in the joint of the foot, or in the foot itself; and many openings occur to it, and a branch emerges from each opening.
Therefore treat it, as we said in the division, and with what has been stated before.
Concerning the incision on worms generated under the skin, and it is called the Cattle Disease.
This ailment is called in a certain land among us the Cattle Disease; because it often happens to oxen, and is a small single worm that is born between the skin and flesh.
And it goes throughout the body, ascending and descending; and it is shown to the senses by its movement from limb to limb, until it breaks wherever it breaks through the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humours. As happen worms and snakes, that is, roundworms, and threadworms in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice. Because when it moves within the body, and rises to the head, and reaches a place, then perhaps it bursts there, and emerges, and destroys the eye; and that happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure it, and extract it, then it is not done except at its movement and its appearance to the senses.
Therefore, it is necessary to bind that which is above it and that which is below it with a strong binding ligature. Then cut above it, and extract it.
If it has sunk into the flesh, and you do not find it, then place a cautery with fire over the place, until it dies.
And the greater fear is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If however you see it already having reached the head near the eye, then bind beneath it the eyebrow with a good ligature. Then cut above it, and extract it.
And it is necessary for the sick person to use cleansing of their body, with medicines that dissolve the bad putrid humours.
And let him beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
Concerning the incision above the illness of Nakir.
This illness is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, then changes from limb to limb.
And I myself have indeed seen it, as I tell you. I was summoned to a woman ill in certain villages. And she exposed her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I remained an hour, I saw one swelling move with the pulse, like a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than it can be: so that it is like quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
Therefore, the pain receded from that place, and it settled in the shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour, and it ran to the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it circulated throughout her whole body, according to what I saw.
Therefore, I marvelled at the speed of its change from limb to limb. And indeed, I had not previously seen this illness with my own eyes in such a way. However, I have seen many who find pain shifting from limb to limb; and I have not seen it in this way with my own eyes, as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it is anything but because the woman was one of those whose dry bodies have detected veins; hence, that windy movement appears to the senses.
And it is necessary that they not appear in this way in those who are delicate, and with moist bodies, and hidden veins.
When therefore you wish to treat it, and the patient feels the pain there: then if it is apparent to your eye, as we said, then tighten above it, and below it quickly; and cut above it, until that constricted windiness emerges; and cauterize the place.
If, however, you do not see it with your own eyes, then treat it with shaking of the body, and with that which expels winds, and calms them with foul pills, and pills of serpent, and similar things.
On the extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their kinds, and according to the places in which they strike the body.
Their diversity is indeed, because among them are those which have large points, and small: and concave, and blunt. And among them are those which have three angles. And among them are those with tongues. And among them are those with stars.
Those, however, which are according to the members on which they fall, are according to two ways. For either the members are from the principal concave members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lungs, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and things like them. Therefore, when it falls into any of these members, and signs of death appear to you, which I tell you later, then you must avoid the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows it in whomever it is, in many dispositions. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it, and pierces the bone, and emerges through the covering above the brain, are: because there happen from it violent headache, and scotoma, and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of the intellect, and vomiting of cholera. And perhaps blood emerges from the nostrils, or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And white moisture like gruel emerges from the place of the wound. And from it emerges something like the watery part of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and remove the arrow if it does not emerge.
The signs of an arrow striking the heart are: that it is near the left breast; and that one feels as if it were fixed in a hard thing, not in an empty thing. And perhaps the arrow's movement is similar to the pulse's movement: and black blood runs from the wound. And that is followed by coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore, that death descends undoubtedly.
And a sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound: and the vessels which follow the neck fester, and the sick person's color changes. And he breathes out a large, deep breath: and seeks the attraction of cold air.
If an arrow strikes the membrane, which is in the chest: then it is near the small ribs: and there is a large gasping with intense pain, with a sigh: and all the arm members move.
And if an arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps something undigested food exits from the wound.
And if an arrow strikes the belly, and is fixed in it: something of excrement, or zirb, or intestine that is already ruptured, exits from the wound. Therefore, there is no hope in its treatment, nor in extracting the arrow.
If an arrow strikes the bladder, and urine exits: and something proceeds from it to the exterior, and the pain is stronger than the weakness: then know that it is fatal.
However, the remaining members, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and the arm, and the vertebrae of the back, and the collarbone, and the hip, and the leg, and similar members are saved according to various conditions, when the arrow does not strike an artery, or a nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned.
And I tell you what I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may signify through it upon your treatment.
Because an arrow struck a man in the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. Therefore, I extracted it from the other side, under the fat of his ear; and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which struck him in the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid; and the arrow was already hidden, and I obtained from it only a small tip, with which it adheres to its shaft; and it was a large arrow from the bows of the Turks, with soft iron, in which there are no ears.
Therefore, the Jew was healed, and no bad accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is the one which has ears. Therefore, I cut above it between the two guides: and it was already submerged in his throat. Therefore, I worked with it, until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from the man which had fallen into his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
Therefore, after thirty days had passed, and nothing of his conditions had changed upon him, I cut above the arrow: and I worked upon it, and I extracted it: and he was healed, and no bad accident happened to him.
And I saw a man on whom an arrow fell on his back, and the wound on it was healed. Therefore, after seven years, an arrow exited at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman on whom an arrow fell into her belly, and the wound on it was healed, and the arrow remained inside her: and no change was made in any of her conditions, and the woman did not find any harm to her in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man, on whose face an arrow fell: and the wound was healed, and it remained, causing him not much pain: and many similar to these.
And I extracted an arrow from the king's chief men, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and had already deviated slightly to the right side: and the entire arrow was hidden.
Therefore, I was summoned for his treatment after the arrow's fall up to three days. Therefore, I found the arrow wound to be very tight.
Therefore, I searched for it with a fine probe, and did not feel it: and indeed he found a prick and pain under his right ear. Therefore, I hoped that that prick was from the tip of the arrow.
Therefore, I plastered the area with a plaster in which there was attracting and digestive virtue, trusting in myself that the area would become an abscess, and that I would see a sign of the arrow, so that I could cut over it: and there was no sign in the place indicating that the arrow had reached the place.
Therefore, I applied the plaster to him for many days: and no sign appeared, and the wound healed meanwhile: and he remained weak from the extraction of it for many days, until he felt the arrow one day inside his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. Therefore, I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to that wound for many days, until it opened: and when I probed it, I felt a thin arrowhead adhering to the wood.
Then I continued to apply the sharp medicine to the wound opening, until the arrowhead became visibly apparent: and approximately four months passed with him during this time.
Then, after the wound had enlarged, and I was able to insert forceps into it and move it, and it did not yield to extraction, I did not cease to refine and devise methods concerning it, until one day I used forceps properly made, as will be shown in the form of them at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed, physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore I restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and the infirm was healed with complete health; and nothing at all harmed him.
And I narrate to you the quality of the extraction of certain arrows, so that you may posit it as a rule and significance over that which I have not said.
Because the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with the narration; nor does the book comprehend it, and a skillful craftsman compares little things to great things; and with that which is present, to that which is absent; and finds a new operation and a new instrument in the descent of foreign things of this art when they occur.
I say, therefore, that arrows do not emerge from the members on which they are fixed, except in two ways. Or by traction from the place from which it entered. Or by the contrary of another part.
And that which emerges from where it entered is either an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place, and is drawn out, and emerges.
If it does not yield to exit at the hour in which it falls, then it is necessary to leave it for some days, until the flesh that is around it rots, and its traction is eased, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in the bone, and does not yield to you for exit, then leave it also for some days, and repeatedly apply traction and motion every day, until it emerges.
If it does not yield to you for exit after days, then it is necessary to pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine drill, until the arrow's surrounding material is enlarged. Then pull it, and extract it.
If, however, the arrow is already hidden in a place of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sight, then search for it with probes. If you feel it, then pull it out with certain instruments that are suitable for its removal.
If, however, you cannot remove it because of the constriction of the wound, and because of the arrow's length deep inside, and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there, then cut around it until the wound is widened, and it is possible to remove the arrow.
If it has ears by which it is held, then free the flesh fixed to them from every side, by every method you can; and if that method is not sufficient to free the flesh from the breaking or twisting of the ears, continue until it escapes.
And when you perform the removal of the arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps on all sides, until you make it escape; and with the utmost delicacy, so that the arrow is not broken, and its extraction becomes difficult for you.
If you cannot remove it within one hour, then leave it for several days until those surrounding it rot. Then try again: for it will then be easier.  But if a flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we have said about its treatment in its chapter; and guard your skill from incision of a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And employ every method possible for the arrow's escape; and let it be with ease and tranquility, or slowness and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary, when you pull out the arrow, that it be weak in accordance with the shape in which the arrow was lodged. Because it is more helpful. If however it is not possible for you, then administer what is possible to you from the figures.
However, if an arrow exits from the opposite side, either something is already protruding from it outwards, or the arrowhead causes a swelling on the skin near the upper part, and you see it protruding; therefore cut over it, and let the incision be according to the amount that the forceps can grasp.
Then pull it out; for it becomes easy to remove.
If however it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to the roundness, until the arrow strikes the bone, and enlarges itself; then pull it.
And if not, leave it for a few days; then try it again, until it comes out.
If the arrow's shaft is in it, then push it out with it.
If however the shaft has fallen away, and you wish to administer a push, then insert a concave instrument into it, so that its concavity enters the arrow's tail; then push it out with it.
If however the arrow is concave, then push it out with an instrument entering it. For the arrow is relieved by it.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cauterize all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you. Then treat it with whatever is appropriate for it.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side; and it is close enough to what you touch with probes, and it is possible for you to cut above it, then cut, and be careful not to cut a vein or a nerve, and pull it out.
Then stitch the wound, if you see fit, and treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the extremities be similar to the beak of a bird, made of iron: when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And many forms of them are made, large, small, and medium, according to the quantity of the arrow's size, and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and its constriction.
The shape of the pushing concave one.
And the shape of the pushing deaf one.
This is the deaf extremity, like a ray, so that its entrance into the concave arrow may be eased, and it may be propelled with it.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins.
The veins to be bled, whose use runs through the body, are thirty.
Of these, sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbs; and two arteries, which are visible on the two temples; and two veins that are at the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called the Looking ones; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein that is at the end of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins, however, which are opened in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalica, and it is from the wild side: and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the Fusca. And it is the Middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilica, and a branch of the Cephalica: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilica, and it is situated on the domestic side: and it is also called the Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the Cord of the arm, and it is situated above the fossa: and it is that in which bloodletting is performed, and it is that which appears above the thumb by clear appearance.
And the Saluatella: and it is the vein that is between the ring finger and the little finger: and it has two branches.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curve of the big toe on the wild side. And the second is the Saphena: and its place is at the heel on the domestic side. And the vein Nesse: and its place is at the heel on the wild side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
Concerning the phlebotomy of two veins behind the ears.
Phlebotomies of the two veins behind the ears are beneficial for old colds, and hemiplegia, and headaches, and old, bad head ulcers.
And the quality of the phlebotomy is as I describe. And it is done by shaving the patient's head, and rubbing the back of his head in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, with a good friction. Then let the patient tighten his neck with a bandage until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in two depressed areas of the head. Therefore, seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse beneath your finger, mark those places with ink.
Then take a phlebotomous knife, and that is called the Incidens. Then insert it beneath the vein in the skin, until the phlebotomous reaches the opening. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upward, and cut the vein with the skin with a sloping incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two joined fingers, or about that: and cause as much blood to issue as you wish.
Then bind it with bandages, and leave it until it heals.
And already previously in the beginning of the book was the incision of them, and the cauterization of them.
Phlebotomy of the two arteries located in the two temples is helpful in mitigating old hemiplegia, and in addressing crookedness, and persistent ophthalmia, and the excessive flow of acute, shed tears to the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is according to what I tell you. Let the infirm person strain their neck much, until two veins become clearly visible to the senses, and their pulse is demonstrably felt under your finger. Then, mark the locations with ink.
Then, lift the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the incision-making phlebotom, and lift the vein upwards. And cut it, as you did in the other two veins: and make blood flow according to your need.
Then loosen the infirm person's constriction, and place your finger upon the vein for one hour. Afterwards, place a bandage and pads upon it, and bind it tightly above the constriction: and release it, until it is healed.
And already the remembrance of them, and their incision, and their extraction have preceded at the beginning of the book.
Indeed, the benefit of phlebotomy of the frontal vein is after phlebotomy of the cephalic vein, for old facial illnesses: and for hemicrania, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is, according to what I tell you. The sick person tightens their neck with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take the instrument called Fossorium.
Place the prominent point that is on the head of the Fossorium over the vein itself, and strike upon it with a comb, or with something else in its manner: and allow the blood to run according to the quantity that you wish. Then loosen the wound of the sick person, and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide phlebotome. However, it should not be a phlebotome with a sharp end, like the other phlebotomes, rather it should have a broad end. And phlebotomize it along the transverse direction, because the bone is close by: and perhaps the phlebotome will break in it when it is slender.
However, the relief of the two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, is for an eye afflicted with scabies and redness; and for a scab on the eye, and ailments of the face.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is such that it tightens the weak neck with a bandage. Then phlebotomize them: and you be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made transverse to the length a little, with a small phlebotom, somewhat broad, for there is no flesh in the place. For if the phlebotom is of a delicate extremity, perhaps it is broken.
Then make the blood run out as much as is necessary for you. And place a bandage on them, and tie them for one night, and untie them.
Phlebotomy of the veins of the Nose is useful for acute fevers, and violent headaches, and diseases of the face, such as hemicrania, and redness that occurs in the face, especially when it is of long standing.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the weak man tightens his own neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a slender, long lancet, and place it in the middle of his nostril, between that which divides the nose according to its straightness. Since the vein is not apparent to the senses there, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you hold your hand with the lancet for a little while, and cause blood to flow according to your need. Then tie it with a single bandage. For it is quickly restored.
The benefit of the two phlebotomies of Guidegi is for constriction of breathing, and the onset of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and bad ulcers, and those that corrode, that is, shingles.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is that the weak person is held by the two ligatures on his neck, and the craftsman stands over the head of the weak person, and the weak person sits on a seat.
Then the phlebotomist should open the vein to a width suitable for the phlebotomy for a short while. Then extract from the blood a moderate quantity, or according to that which you see from necessity for that. Then do likewise with the other vein.
Afterwards, loosen the ligature, and tie the two veins with a middle constriction, so that the weak person is not overly choked. And leave it until morning. For the wound heals.
On the phlebotomy of veins, Algeberich. The veins, according to Algeberich, are a help in phlebotomy: because they contribute to sores in the mouth, and gum corruption; and bad ulcers; and lip fissures, and bad ulcers, even those that are on the nose and its surroundings.
And the quality of phlebotomy of them is such that you make the infirm sit before you, and bind his neck with a strip. Then roll back his lip and look at the two veins: one of which is on the right of the lip and the second on its left; and the demonstration of both is by the blackness of both. And that is true, because around them are small, black veins. Therefore, cut each with a single incision.
If, however, there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is, then direct yourself to the incision of the larger of them, and the more evident of them.
And similarly do with the two veins that are on the upper lip; and indeed, most custom runs with it; and cut the two veins which are on the lower lip.
Concerning phlebotomy of the veins under the tongue after phlebotomy of the cephalic veins. However, the benefit of phlebotomy of the two veins which are under the tongue is that phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is for the suffocation which is in the throat, and the illness of the uvula, and the illnesses of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is that the sick person should sit between your hands in the presence of the sun; and lift his tongue, and look under his tongue on one side of it for a vein, and on the other side of it for a vein; and the color of each is sloping towards blackness.
Therefore, phlebotomize each one; and guard that you do not put your hand to the utmost in phlebotomizing them. For under it are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood will occur from those arteries.
However, the three veins that are phlebotomized in the elbow are those whose phlebotomy has run in custom among all men.
And their phlebotomy is done according to two methods. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle phlebotome, or an olive-shaped one, to a degree of steepness, or by cutting with a knife phlebotome, and it is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And of them are broad and narrow types, according to the width and constriction of the veins.  The shape of the myrtle phlebotome should be broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, proceeding, apparent, large veins, and those containing thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotome is of lesser width, and of a finer extremity.
And it is suitable for the phlebotomy of slender veins, which contain subtle, yellowish blood.
The Basilica vein, which is one of these three veins, is a help in phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from ailments of the throat and neck, from thence following the chest and belly; and it befits the phlebotomist, when phlebotomizing it, to be cautious and to be wary of it, for there is an artery beneath it. If therefore he errs, and adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will strike that artery, and there will be an occurrence of blood flow. Therefore it is necessary that he not phlebotomize it with a phlebotomizing instrument; rather, he should cut with an Alnesil.
If, however, the Basilica vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to abandon it and to switch to another, or to seek a branch of it; or to phlebotomize the Brachial vein instead. For it is indeed manifest, and cut it with a phlebotomizing Anesil instrument, as we said.
If however you wish to phlebotomize the same vein, then it is necessary, before the constriction of the arm, that you touch the place, until you recognize the place of the pulse. Then mark the place with ink, afterward the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using the Alnesil instrument, as we said. And beware that the blow does not fall in the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, when you constrict the ligament, an inflation in the place which you marked with ink, then that inflation is the inflation of the artery: therefore avoid it.
If you see blood at the phlebotomy leap, like the urine of a child, and it is thin blood, red: then know that it is from the blood of the artery. Then quickly, place your finger over it for a long space, then remove your finger. If therefore blood is cut off (for it is often cut off), then constrict the arm, and release it; and let the patient beware of its movement; and let it be upon his fear.
And let him not move it for several days, until it is healed.
If however the blood is not cut off, and overcomes you; and there is no medicine present for you in your moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities contract, and blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of the fig tree, and split it; take one half, and tie it over the place of the vein with a proper constriction, with a bandage and lint until another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not, then treat it with that whose recollection preceded from the place of powders cutting off the flow of blood; and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in many dispositions, because of the small place of the wound, and the possibility of a bandage from the arm.
Phlebotomy of the Fusca vein is beneficial, because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is composed of a branch of the Basilica vein and a branch of the Cephalica vein, as we have said.
And it befits the phlebotomist that it be above fear from its phlebotomy, for a nerve is beneath it. Therefore, if the phlebotomist adds in the puncture, and finds a nerve, numbness occurs in it, and its healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not healed completely.
And this nerve often appears to the senses. However, if it is hidden in some people, and is subtle and not shown, then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy, so that you phlebotomize it, incision with a lancet, and avoid the nerve with your skill.
If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise.
Concerning the cephalic vein. Phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is beneficial, because it draws blood from the head, and contributes to eye ailments.
And it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish, to phlebotomize it by pricking with an olive-wood lancet, or with a myrtle-wood lancet: because it is the safest of all veins, because there is no artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, it is fitting for you in phlebotomy to avoid with the lancet only the head of the muscle, and to seek a smoother place: and it does not harm it if it is not found with the first blow, so that it may be repeated over it with phlebotomy several times.
However, perhaps it becomes inflamed in some people when it is not phlebotomized on the first strike, but that inflammation does not harm them in any way.
Concerning the usefulness of Phlebotomy, and the precautions to be observed in it. However, the quality of Phlebotomy, and its consequent accidents, which you must consider in its correction.
First, you should know that phlebotomy is not administered except in the preservation of health, and its endurance, and caution against the onset of illnesses.
And so that phlebotomy is performed in one of the three veins that are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Basilic, and the Median cubital. And so that phlebotomy is performed in the beginning of Spring on an empty stomach, in Spring and Summer; and in Autumn and Winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And let phlebotomy be performed on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed of the day.
However, phlebotomy administered in illnesses does not have a set hour; but when convenience and necessity call for it, at night, or day, and at all hours, and at all times.
And children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should the elderly be bled, who have already passed sixty years.
Therefore, when someone wishes to be bled for whatever reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their bowels beforehand with a mild clyster, if there is much retained stool in them, so that the veins during the phlebotomy do not draw out putrid, harmful superfluities from the intestines to the principal members.
Nor should a patient be bled who is suffering from a surfeit, nausea, or drunkenness, until that has been removed from them. Nor should a sick person with dropsy.
And phlebotomy should be avoided again after colic, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive intercourse, labor, exercise, vigils, fasting, and anything that weakens the body's or animal's strength.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors before that, if the blood is thick with foods, drinks, and medicines, if it is possible to do so.
Then he should enter a bath, if nothing prevents him; or he should exercise with some exercise, so that the blood may be made thinner.
And place his phlebotomy on his chest, around the third day, as we said: and strive that his chest be emptied that day of all evil animal accidents: such as anguish, and anger, and fear; and of all bodily accidents, such as labor, and pain, and excess of intercourse, and similar things.
And let him have present in his seat things with whose use custom has run among men from aromatics, and scents, and dancing, and similar things in the greatest possible quantity.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion which is higher than the phlebotomist's cushion; afterwards let him pull his arm out and let the phlebotomist rub it with his hand twice or three times. Then tie the bandage with a strap, and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, for when the constriction is unequal, or through excess in the constriction, then it hinders the course of blood; or in relaxation, then it also hinders the course of blood.
Then after the constriction, it is necessary that the phlebotomist rub his hands together until the veins swell and are made apparent to the senses.
Then let the phlebotomist wipe the phlebotomized area with a little old, proper oil.
Then let him place his left index finger slightly over the vein itself, beneath the place where he intends to make the phlebotomy, so that the vein does not move and the blow is not missed. Because there are veins that are like a fleeing string during phlebotomy; and there are those that are full of airiness; and when you place the phlebotom on it, it contracts under the phlebotom, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and the phlebotom does not open the vein; and if it does open it, it is constricted.  Therefore, it is necessary for the phlebotomist to be firm and careful in all these things.
Then he should lay down the phlebotom. If therefore the vein is opened at the first attempt, then it is good. And if not, then repeat it another time under that place slightly, or above it with haste, if the place is not inflamed.
If, however, it becomes inflamed, or the patient becomes weak, then leave him for a day or two, and do not tighten the ligature. For perhaps it will draw a hot abscess. Nor let him enter a bath.
Then repeat the bloodletting, if he wishes.
If the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly, and you fear that not the quantity of blood you desire will exit, then repeat the bloodletting in the same opening according to the straightness, and add a little in the operation.
And do it with haste, before the place is suppurated, for a suppurated place is in many people in a small operation.
If therefore you see it already suppurated, then do not repeat on it at all, for it does not excuse anything to you. And place something of the dregs of oil upon it, because it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than the other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your phlebotomies, where phlebotomies open veins against the prohibition of blood flow: and the Tyriac alfarach, and sagzenea do this, when one of them is placed upon the place.
For blood is made thinner and resolved when it is thick.
If a large abscess occurs at the phlebotomy site, for it often happens, especially to one who is not a phlebotomist except on that occasion, or if the opening of the vein is small, then hurry and place a sponge soaked in water and salt, warmed a little, upon the place: and bind it for one hour: for it dissolves.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has exited the vein itself, when it is full, or from another vein.
If some blackness or greenness remains in the place after a few days, it does not harm: and if you wish, put some aloe, or dissolved myrrh, or some of the juice of mint, that is, of balm or similar to it, upon it.
And very often indeed abscesses and prominences occur at phlebotomy in the Basilica.
Therefore, place your hand upon it; then if you find softness upon compression with your hand upon it, then that prominence is bad. Therefore, beware that you put something upon it of that which we have said. For perhaps blood will flow from it of the artery.
But make a plaster on it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place may be hardened. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract from every man's blood according to the quantity of its virtue. And that which appears from the color prevailing over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it be allowed to exit until it turns red; and similarly, if you see it thick, then let it be allowed to exit until it becomes thin; and similarly, if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting at once, that the vein's phlebotomy be widened and the phlebotomist be broad; and in the one who is weak, be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary that he extract it in portions, and that the opening be narrow; and better what is administered in vein-letting is that it be a transverse incision, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is safe from a flow of blood, and from the incision of a nerve; and it is more praiseworthy, and safer than bloodletting by width and length.
And to whom it is accustomed to come to fainting in bloodletting, it is necessary that he eat before bloodletting a little bread soaked in pomegranate-water; or secaniabin, if it is heated; and extract from his blood in three or four portions.
And if he is of a cold temperament, then let him take before bloodletting bread soaked in myrrh syrup, or in syrup of spiced honey, or in fragrant aromatic syrup.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting, and its cause is the abundant outflow of blood, then it is necessary that he take in a drink water of flesh, and a subtle fragrant syrup; and let him use aromatics with almug; and let his chest be lined with it; and let him use the rest that we have stated in the division in the chapter on fainting from evacuation.
Whoever wishes to evacuate his arm and release his blood a second time, it is necessary for him whose bloodletting is for a great evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that he release blood gradually by the amount of strength over consecutive days.
However, whoever wants to evacuate his arm and release his blood a second time, and his body is strong, then let him do it after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting.
Whoever wishes to draw blood from his body to the opposite part to which it inclines, it is necessary that he be evacuated on the second or third day.
In whose body, however, is much blood already heated, and sharpened, and causes fever to occur: it is necessary that blood depart from it in one instance, and a large quantity be drawn from it; and the opening be enlarged until fainting occurs; after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting; and that you place your hand upon his pulse at the flow of blood, so that death does not occur in place of fainting. For often that happens, when the bloodletter does not know, and negligence falls.
And it is not necessary, when you wish to release the arm and release blood a second time, when the opening of the vein is already closed and the exit of blood is difficult, that you press upon it with violence, and rapidly bend or extend it. For that is very harmful; indeed, leave it, until it is bled again.
Or remove with the sharpness of the bloodletter that which has clotted from the blood in the opening of the vein, and place upon it something of dissolved salt in water. And place upon it something of al-thiryac al-faruq or sagzenea, and place it with gentle compression, until the blood issues forth.
If however the vein is already ulcerated, then leave it, and do not touch it, until the ulcer subsides.
If, however, necessity provokes you to draw blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either bleed him above that place or in another arm in another vein.
However, the arm's vein should be lanced at the place of the Cephalic and Basilic veins, when they are not found, and are hidden, because it is composed of them.
And the quality of the bloodletting is such that the infirm person should immerse his hand in warm water until the skin reddens, and the vein becomes clearly visible.
Then tie it above it with a ligature with a moderate constriction. Then lance it transversely, gradually, not laterally, nor longitudinally; and let the opening be wide, and your incision be over the joint of the hand for a short time.
If the flow of blood is impeded, then immerse the hand again in warm water and allow the blood to flow into the water until you achieve your desired result.
If, however, you are in the days of summer, then you are excused from the repetition of immersing the hand in warm water. And indeed, the flow of blood is placed most frequently in warm water in wintertime.
And the incision of this vein is safer than all veins, because there is no pulsating vein nor nerve beneath it.
Phlebotomy of the right hand's Saluatella vein is beneficial for liver ailments.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that you should constrict the mahason of the hand with a bandage, or with your hand, after you immerse it in warm water, until the vein swells and becomes very apparent to the senses. Then, incise it transversely. And if you cut it completely, it will not harm him in any way.
And be careful not to finish your hand with the lancet. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers exposed from the flesh.
Then, return the hand to warm water, and let the blood run in it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood coagulates in the opening of the vein, and is prevented from flowing.
When therefore you have drawn out the necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt upon the vein: so that it does not quickly consolidate.
And similarly it is necessary that you do so with every tight branch.
The benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand, however, is: because it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen: and similarly do in its phlebotomy as you did in the second equally.
The benefit of saphenous phlebotomy is for illnesses that are in the lower part of the body, such as ailments of the womb, and the retention of menses, and illnesses of the kidneys, and old sores of the hips and legs, and similar illnesses.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that the sick person should immerse their foot in warm water: and do friction upon it until the veins swell: and bind above the joint of the foot with a strap. And indeed the vein's location is at the heel, appearing toward the big toe: and from it branches out many branches on the face of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or at the heel in its aggregation, because it is better and safer.
If you phlebotomize it on the foot's surface, then beware of the nerves that are under it on the surface of the foot.
And place your phlebotomy instrument so that you phlebotomize it across, as if you wish to cut it: and let the phlebotomist be Nesil.
If however the outflow of blood is prevented, then repeatedly place his foot in warm water, and allow the blood to flow into it, until it is evacuated.
If the phlebotomist errs in the first instance, then he should repeat the phlebotomy a little higher up, for the place is safe, and you do not fear harm from it, when you beware of the nerves, as we said.
And similarly with the saphena of the other foot equally.
The Sciatica vein's location, as we said, is near the heel on the wild side.
And the relief of phlebotomy is for it, for the pain of the knee, when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the patient should enter a warm bath, and hasten: and he should bind his leg from the side of the knee up to the heel by four fingers, with a long, thin bandage: for it does not appear except on account of that.
