When it therefore appears, then the phlebotomist should cut it according to whatever skill is possible to you, or according to a transverse method; and it is better either to cut it with an incision, or to sever it by a section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people. If, however, you do not find it, and it is not at all apparent to the senses, then the phlebotomist should cut some branch of it, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot, and towards the ear; and beware of the nerves, and cause as much blood to flow as you wish.
Then loosen the bandage, and place leather over the phlebotomy site, and bind the area. For it quickly heals.
Concerning Cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping instruments are made from horns, and from brass, and from glass.
And cupping indeed is done according to two methods. With scarification, and the extraction of blood; and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or indeed it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And cuppings that are administered with scarification and the extraction of blood are fourteen places of the body. Of which one is the cupping Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head; and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck; and the cuppings Alhagdahim, and they are two flaps of the neck at both sides together; and the cuppings of the chin, and it is below the jaw in the lower part of the mouth; and the cuppings of the two shoulder blades; and the cuppings Alachosos above the end of the tail; and the cuppings of the two armpits, and they are in the middle of the two arms; and the cuppings of the two thighs; and the cuppings of the two calves.
And cupping indeed does not extract blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that, virtue does not fall from them, as is the case with phlebotomy. Nor should cupping, whether with or without fire, be administered in any illness arising from fullness until the whole body has been evacuated.
If necessity prompts cupping for illness or because of custom, we administer it at any hour at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, in the middle of it, and whenever it may be.
This is because there are people in whom when blood multiplies within, until it needs extraction by cupping, they find heaviness and soreness in their head.
And some of them find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And some of them find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And some of them scratch the site of their cupping. And some of them have increased laughter. And some of them find the taste of blood in their mouth; or the tongue becomes suppurated, and they spit blood. And some of them have increased sleep. And some of them see in their sleep blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to them.
Therefore, when we see any of these things, and especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order a letting of wind, after two or three hours have passed of the day.
The benefit of letting wind from the nape of the neck is: because it contributes to the heaviness in the head, and what is poured out to the eyes. However, it is necessary that it be after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in place of a head-vein incision.
And it is not permitted to use it on one of cold temperament, or in whom there is a cold. For it brings him great harm.
And therefore it is not appropriate that the aged, and those with cold ailments on their heads, use it.
But if one is continuously on top of it, forgetfulness is generated in him.
And therefore it is necessary to order the wind-dresser to lay his hand for a little while lower with the wind, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Wind-dressing, or Alkehel, is in place of the phlebotomy of the Fusca and the Basilica phlebotomy, and therefore it is beneficial for asthma, and constriction of breathing, and fissure of the breathing instrument, and coughing, and fullness.
And it is necessary that the Alkehel cupping glass be impelled for a little while, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Wind-dressing, however, of the alhagdahim, is beneficial for accidental pains in the head, and ophthalmia, and hemicrania, and perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is in place of the phlebotomy of the Basilica.
And it is necessary to instruct the wind-dresser not to press his hand with the scarification, so that he does not strike an artery and cause a flow of blood.
Wind-dressing, however, under the chin, is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar to them of diseases which are in the mouth: and it stands in the place of the phlebotomy of alieherich, which are on the two lips.
Wind-dressing of the spatulas, however, is beneficial for alchafacham, which is produced by repletion and heat.
Wind-dressing, however, of the inner parts of the two foci, is beneficial as a substitute for the phlebotomy of the three veins, Basilica, and Fusca, and Cephalica: because it draws blood from all those small veins that are in the flesh; and those small veins draw from other larger veins than themselves, until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary to instruct the wind-dresser not to proceed too far in the scarification, because the place is denuded of flesh, and under it are nerves and arteries.
A cupping vessel, however, that is cupped above the alchausos, is beneficial for hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary to instruct the wind-dresser that the cupping vessel be large and made of air, because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps the thin cupping vessels will break; and it should be scarified with a large scarification.
Cupping of the legs diminishes noticeably: because they draw blood from the whole body, and they relieve old pains in the kidneys, and the womb, and the bladder; they provoke menstruation, and are beneficial for botor and ademenul; and they stand in the place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause syncope in many people.
And the benefit of alhorcobeni cupping is close to the benefit of cupping the two legs.
The quality of placing the cupping vessels is that you should first place an empty cupping vessel and apply moderate suction. And do not prolong the placement of the cupping vessels, but place them quickly and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place with equal arrival. And do not cease repeating this and continuing it, until you see the place already reddened and inflamed, and the redness of blood appears: then, therefore, scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider the disposition of the bodies.
For those who are of delicate flesh, with rare pores, it is necessary to scarify them once, no more, so that the place is not ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification and deepen it slightly, and to moderate the suction with ease, speed, and smooth movement.
If there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify it twice. In the first instance, a path is opened for the thinning of the blood and its becoming watery; and in the second, for a careful examination of the extraction of thick blood.
If, however, the blood is very foul, then let the scarification be performed a third time, so that it may achieve its final result.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or for a short time, a single scarification suffices for us. If we wish for a large extraction of blood, we scarify many times.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep scarification; and the equal limit in the scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is that which ought to be administered from oils at the placement of Cupping, and what should the cupper beware of?
In that one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary that the places of cupping be anointed with oils that are opening, soothing, and resolving: if it is in summer time, such as the oil of alkeiri, or the oil of violet, or the oil of sweet almonds, or the oil of pumpkin seed.
And if it is in winter time, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or camomile oil, or sarbantine oil: and similar to these.
If however the superfluity is thick, cold: then the oil should be amaracin oil, or balsam oil, or alben oil, or anise oil: and similar to these.
If however the cupper is of large pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary that he abstain from oil, and it is necessary that those ones wash the places of cupping after cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with soothing water, or with pumpkin water, or with purslane water: and similar to them.
However, he whose blood is of much moisture, should wash the cuppings with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac: and similar to them.
However, whose superfluities are thick, should wash their cuppings with old wine, or with sorrel water, or with anise decoction, or camomile: and similar to them.
And it is necessary that cupping be avoided in a bath, or in the vicinity of a bath, indeed it is necessary that it be administered after exiting from the bath by an hour, or by two hours.
And it is not necessary that anyone sleep after cupping.
What is it by which it is necessary that the Ventosandus, or Phlebotomist, be governed before cupping?
And after it, it is necessary to consider first: if the Ventosus or Phlebotomist is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation overcome his blood, then it is necessary that he take refrigerants, such as pomegranate juice, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and iuleb, and similar to them.
And place his food chickens, and mutton sekebiget, and foods seasoned with gooseberries, and similar to them.
And whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in his drink syrup from honey, or from almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds: and let him take medium scented wine, which is between old and new: and let him be instructed to use little food. And place his food chickens, and canebir, and sparrows, that is, small birds, and young doves issiadebet.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of the ventosation and phlebotomy be more than the food; and it is necessary that, in some men, in the hours of tyriaca alfaroch, or a medicine of moss, that is, musk, or alesselithe be given in the drink before the ventosation, and before the phlebotomy, or after, so that the principal members may be strengthened, and the blood may be refined. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Concerning ventosations without scarification. However, ventosations that are without scarification are ventosations that are placed upon the liver, and the spleen, and the gout, and upon the belly, the navel, and the places of the kidneys, and the chest of the loins, because these members do not tolerate scarification upon themselves.
And they are not sought out by them, unless either a connection of blood from a member to a member; just as we place a ventosa upon the nipples in an illness of nosebleed. And we use them to free a member from a cold, viscous windiness in the member; just as we place a ventosa upon the belly and the navel. For it indeed rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes the pain because it dissolved that windiness.
It is also placed upon the kidneys when obstruction or a stone occurs in them. For by its power of attraction, perhaps it opens the obstruction or uproots the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed upon the liver and spleen for a troublesome windiness in both of them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with only a cut. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in cases of pleurisy; and that is so that the cupping glass be filled and be large, with only warm water, or water in which herbs suitable for it have been boiled.
Form of cupping glass administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two open fingers, according to what we have formed: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be a small hole on its side, in a place approximately in the middle of it, according to the size for a needle to enter it: let it be made of bronze, or brass, with thick, smooth, even, polished edges, so that it does not injure the limb in its placement upon it: and let a transverse rod of bronze or iron be in its center, where the candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, larger than the one we described, and a little smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
For the cupping glasses of children and the thin are different from the cupping glasses of men and those with large bodies.
The method of placing this cupping glass with fire upon the limb is to light a wick with fire from flax properly, or a small candle of wax, and place it over the middle of the transverse rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass: so that the fire is kindled approximately at the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the body of the sick person is not burned.
Then it is placed upon the limb, and a finger is placed over the hole that I mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glass, as long as we need it, we remove the finger, and vapor will exit through that hole, and the cupping glass will be released immediately.
Then light the wick according to its way, and repeat it, if you need it.
However, in a cupping glass applied in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse rod, nor hole: and it is not applied except because it is filled with water, and placed upon the limb only.
And this is its form.
And the larger this cupping glass is, so that it can hold much water, the better it is.
Concerning the suspension of bloodsuckers.
Bloodsuckers are not applied in multiple places, unless in those limbs where the application of cupping is impossible, due to their small size, such as the lip, and the gums, and similar parts. Or on a limb denuded of flesh, such as a finger, and the nose, and similar parts.
And the quality of using them is: that you should take bloodsuckers which are in fresh, clean waters, free from putrid matter.
Then they should be left in fresh water by day and night, until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body should be evacuated first with bloodletting and with cupping. Then cleanse the limb of the sick person until it turns red. Then place them upon it.
When, therefore, they are filled, and fall, and suction of the place is possible with cupping, then it is most helpful; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and it is rubbed, and squeezed out.
If the flow of blood after the fall of the Sanguisugarum is prolonged, and it is oozing, then pour a linen cloth into cold water, and place it over it, until the oozing is cut off.
If the blood multiplies, then pulverize ground zegi, or gallnuts, or similar astringents upon it, until the blood is cut off.
Or place halves of shelled beans on the area, and let them remain until the beans adhere to the area, for the blood is cut off.
And if a repetition of the Sanguisugarum is necessary, when possible, change them.
If, however, the leeches are prevented from being suspended, then the area is wiped with fresh blood. Or fix a needle in the areas until some blood emerges. Then place them, for when they sense anything of the blood, they immediately adhere.
When therefore you wish them to fall off, pulverize some crushed aloe, or salt, or ashes on them: and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know, O children, that the foolish physicians and the common people boast about this chapter, and those who have never examined an ancient book, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason, this method is private among the sciences in our land. And I have never encountered any good in it at all, and I have not attained that which I have attained from it except because of the length of reading ancient books, and study concerning their understanding, until I extracted the knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge: and upon which my experiment has passed, after I have abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the occupation of elongation, and abbreviated it at the end of the abbreviation, and shown it at the end of the declination; and I have formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments which are administered in it, since it is from the addition of declination, just as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary, as we say in the prologue of this chapter, to summarise the discourse and chapters, the understanding of which the craftsman is first required to attain to stand upon their truth: so that you, and those who are ready, may know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or twisting, or fall happens to someone, then it is necessary, first of all, to hasten to bleed him, and to loosen him, or both simultaneously: if nothing prevents it, such as weakness of strength; or if the one to whom anything of these has happened is a child, or a decrepit old man; or if it is a time of intense heat, or intense cold greatly.
Then let him be content in his food, with cold vegetables, and the flesh of birds, and kids: and forbid wine, and coarse meats, and filling oneself with foods and every food which fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from a hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb: then let the sick man return to his first regimen with which his custom runs.
When therefore the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing foods, with much, coarse, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and coarse wine, and things similar to them. For with this regimen the coagulation of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in the first age of old age, and decrepit, cannot be joined and consolidated according to their own nature always, because of the drying out of their limbs: and those that are in them at the end of their lightness are consolidated and joined, like the bones of small children.
However, nature attaches something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, to the broken bone from every side, by which it adheres to and binds it, until they are glued together and bound together, until it comes to the end of its strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you put the sick person's nourishment in foods in which there is weight, and viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs: for the fracture of the leg is different from the fracture of the skull: and the fracture of the breastbone is different from the fracture of the spine: and similarly all the other limbs are different from each other: and I have come with a recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its chapter, distinct from another. And the types of bone fracture are diversified even within themselves.
Because its fracture is arched, besides which splinters occur in it. And sometimes its fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and its fractures are splintery, that is, splinters, and proceeding additions, as are in a reed when a knife is fitted to it for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a fracture with a small fissure.
And each type has its own nature, or instrument, in its restoration, according to which its remembrance will come, distinct in its places.
And from what the fracture of the bone is known, there is its twisting and its prominence: and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor a sound upon touching it with your hand when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the sick person experience great pain; then there is no fracture there, but rather, it is possible that there is a twisting or a small fissure.  Therefore, you should not move it with your hand and pressure at all, but rather place upon it the medicines, the remembrance of which comes, after the time that is appropriate to the location; then a bandage is tied tightly.
And know that when a place is bent and split in two, besides the efforts that happen in it, nevertheless each part is moved away from its counterpart; then it is necessary for you to begin your hour for its straightening, making it even before a hot abscess occurs on it.
If however a hot abscess occurs on it, then leave it for several days, until the abscess subsides. Then make it even according to any method possible by gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and its making even is easier than a bone, in which efforts occur.
Then bind it according to how its memory comes to you.
If however there are efforts in the bone, then extension of this broken limb, if it is a hand or foot, is necessary from two parts; or with your hand, if the limb is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb over a level spot, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken limb are extended: then try to restore those additions to their places with all your skill, and in any way you can; and be careful according to your strength, that no pain or injury be brought upon the weak by your operation; and try to the best of your ability to fit one of the two bones with its partner according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time that you try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see there anything different, rectify it and make it equal according to the extent of your ability.
And beware of excessive extension and strong compression, as many foolish people do. For often they cause a hot abscess or contraction in the limb by their operation; as we have already seen this from their operations many times.
Then, after the equalization and proper operation, and constriction of that limb, and rest and tranquility, and admonish the sick person about it. And make him beware that he does not move it during the hours of his waking and his sleep, at its turning, and its agitation, and at its excretion, and all its movements with all his strength. And that he beware lest the position of the limb be one that will follow with pain. Because when he feels pain or injury in the disposition of the position of the limb, then it is necessary that he change it to another disposition from which he does not feel pain.
And take care with him that his position be even, straight, so that twisting does not occur in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the constriction of the fractured limb is, according to what I relate.
Know that fractured limbs are diversified in their smallness, and their size, and their shape.
Therefore, those of them that are small, such as an arm, and a finger, and a foot, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, thin cloth. However, those of them that are large, such as the thigh and the leg, it is necessary that there be a wrapping of wide, hard material in them.
Because a broad ligament connects a large limb and binds it from every side with equal constriction, so that a blemish does not enter it.
Then, after your evacuation, begin to apply a suitable liniment to it, on soft lint over the site of the fracture itself. Then begin wrapping the ligament over the fracture site itself with three or four wraps, according to the limb's needs; and tighten your hand slightly on the ligament.
Then proceed with it towards the upper part from the fracture site, and tighten less than was your previous tightening, with which you tightened the fractured site. Then move away from the fracture site with the wrap slightly, and gradually loosen the constriction, until you obtain a large portion from the healthy part.
Then take another bandage, and wrap it again over the fractured site in wraps. Afterwards, move the bandage to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in the tightening of the bandage, and the loosening of it, according to what we said in the first upper bandage.
Then place between the bandages soft wool, or cloth, so that the twisting of the fracture is equalized if there is twisting in it; and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then wrap another bandage over it. Afterwards, place over these bandages evenly-made stars immediately, if there is no swelling in the place, nor a hot abscess.
If however there is swelling or an abscess in it, then place upon it that which will settle that abscess and remove the swelling; and leave it for a few days. Then bind over it the stars.
And let the stars be made from the halves of broad canes, shaved, skilfully shaped; or let them be made of the wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or gerard, that is, palm branches, or calingi; and let the width of every star, according to this form, and this same figure, be [specified].
However, it is necessary that the star placed over the wound itself be somewhat thicker and wider than the other stars; and the length of the star should be according to the size and smallness of the member.
Then bind over the stars with another bandage, according to the first binding, the same. Then tie over it with threads properly, according to what we said about the binding; and it should be that your binding is tighter over the break itself, and the further away from the break it is, the looser the binding.
And it is necessary that the threads be medium in thickness and thinness, and be of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the operation of foolish ones who place their threads from twisted linen cords, it is a great error; because the binding falls out of quality with them.
And again, thin threads do not suit: because you will not achieve through the binding with them that which you desire.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger between splint and splint.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the splints after the binding in healthy places, then place under them soft tow or dyed wool until nothing is injured from it.
However, when there is a fracture with a wound, and a rupture occurs in the skin, I will come with a reminder of it in the second chapter according to its singularity.
And know that not every fractured limb needs to be bound with splints from the first day. Because when a limb is large, you should not put splints on it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your judgment of the safety of preventing a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, by which the ancients restored those applied to fractures and dislocations.
A method of a brief common plaster, restoring fractures, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children, because it is from it that neither heat nor cold prevails.
And it is as follows: take flour dust, which is the flour meal that hangs on the walls of the mill during the movement of the millstone; and make it as it is, except that you sift it with egg white; and make the concoction neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
Another method of plaster, restoring fracture, dislocation, and twisting. Take of almess, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugath, and juice of all, each 10 drachms; of myrrh and aloes, each 5 drachms; and of alethil 20 drachms.  Grind all these together, and sift them; and mix them with alethil water, or with egg white if the patient's complexion is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is a good compound, restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
Another method of plaster, which also benefits fracture and twisting. Take of mugath, mess, and white marshmallow, of all 10 drachms; of myrrh and aloes, each 5 drachms; of acacia 6 drachms; of Armenian bole 20 drachms. Grind all thoroughly, sift them, and make a paste with water, or egg white; and administer.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places; and it relieves the pain occurring to them, and the splitting of bone, and the fracture. Take fresh wool and soak it in vinegar and boiled oil; and place it on the affected area.
And in this plaster there is no virtue of restoration, but it is good for calming a hot abscess; and it drives out pains properly.
A method of plaster that restores a fractured bone. Take the leaves of the fig tree and the leaves of the wild poppy; and grind them all and make a plaster from both.
A method of a shortened plaster, administered in the restoration of a broken bone; and when you desire the resolution of the remaining abscess. Take the root of marshmallow, and chamomile, and violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part: let all be ground, and mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
If however it is inflamed, then prepare it with the water of moist coriander, or with water, and use it.
A method of another stronger plaster in resolution, than this first one, when a hard abscess occurs in the restoration of the limb. Take the root of marshmallow, and flaxseed, and pomegranate seeds, and melilot, and plantain, and violet flowers, and chamomile, of each one part: let all be ground, and mixed with willow water, or with fresh water, or with wine. All that according to the warmth of the limb and the quiet of its heat.
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration except a plaster made from wax and oil; nothing else; and he recounted that it should be of a consistency between thick and thin.
Galen, however, thought that things that dry out heat should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration, such as myrrh, and aloes, and frankincense, and similar ones to those that we have recounted.
Method of the plaster which is beneficial for injury and pain. Take mugwort, and chickpeas, and cut human hair, or bird feathers, and marshmallow root, and salt in equal parts: grind them, and sift them, and mix them: and make a plaster from it.
The amount of time the bandage should remain, then be removed, is that you should consider, if no pain or itching occurs in the patient, and if the fractured bone is not moved from its place: then do not remove it for many days. But if such itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling occurs in the area, then quickly loosen it, and remove the plaster: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it, until the itching subsides, and the pain settles.
But if such itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling occurs in the area, then quickly loosen it, and remove the plaster: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it, until the itching subsides, and the pain settles. Then allow the limb to rest for one hour. Then place upon it cut wool, soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind it upon it for one night, until you are sure of the absence of a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then again apply a gentle bandage and a small plaster; and do not tighten it with your first bandaging, and treat it gently until it heals.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all the accompanying symptoms have completely disappeared, and you no longer need a plaster and bandage, then treat it again as you did at first equally.
If nothing of what we have said happens to the limb, then do not loosen it, except after three, or four, or five, or seven days. And when it is left for twenty days, according to what appears to you from the limb's healing, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and the flesh's coagulation approximates it: then also add more binding than your first complete binding; and also add more strengthening food, according to its previous recollection.
If however you see the fracture site already drying out and thinning more than necessary, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore, bathe it with lukewarm water every time you loosen it, and let it remain for three days, and ease the bandage slightly.
Through this operation, therefore, nourishment reaches the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, that which foolish restorers do to a fractured limb a second time, when it is not restored at first as it should be, and is restored according to twisting, is an error of their operation, and a great fear: and if it were correct, the ancients would have said it in their books, and would have operated with it.
And I have seen no trace of it anywhere in that at all, and it is better that it not be done.
Concerning the treatment of an accidental injury to the head.
The kinds of fractures are diverse, and its shapes are many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture that is caused by a blow with a sword; and the blow removes the bone as far as it reaches the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an axe does in wood; and because of that, this type of fracture is called Axialis.
Or the sword cuts a part of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its utmost limit; and this type of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, a lifting absolutely.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is a crushing or abrasion; and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which now approaches the periosteum, which is under the bone, or is on the surface of the bone; and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone in the subtlety of a hair; and it is a small fissure, and because of that this type of fracture is called Capillaris.
And of these is a fracture, which happens from a fall, or a blow from a stone, and similar things, which cause a layer of bone to enter the interior, and a hollow is formed there; just as it happens with bronze cauldrons, when a blow strikes them, and a part of them enters the interior.
And this happens most often in the heads of moist bones, such as the heads of children.
And for all these types of fractures, separate descriptions are made, and I will come with a recollection of it all in its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures through examination upon it, and investigation of them with probes, and the removal of the corrupted flesh that is upon them.
However, the capillary type is recognized by this, that it is uncovered on the bone and cleaned, and smeared upon it with ink: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, first consider the patient's symptoms: then if you see from the symptoms that it signifies manifest danger, such as vomiting, cholera, extension, loss of intellect, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, protrusion of the eyes, redness of them, and similar symptoms in various conditions, without a doubt it will not be healed; and if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for recovery, then begin its treatment.
That is, if a wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded, and it is in the days of winter, then it is necessary to strive for the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all the dispositions.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary to hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the membrane becomes corrupted, and those accidents happen which we have described.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and is with fragments and pulling, then it is necessary to cut away the broken and ground portion, as I describe to you.
And it is necessary to shave the head of the wounded person and expose the bone in whatever way possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound and what is gentle upon the patient.
If, during the exposure of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it with what is appropriate. And it is necessary to fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and to allow the wound to rest until the abscess subsides and you are certain of the cessation of bleeding. Then begin the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a scalpel of narrow blade; and this is its form.
Then apply another scalpel, slightly wider than the first; and this is its form.
Then apply yet another scalpel, wider still than the second.
And the second method is that you should have a number of different scalpels; some being wider than others, and some shorter than others; and let the edges of their sharpness be the last, and be of iron temper, or steel; and use ease in striking upon the scalpel, so that it does not strike the head and injure it.
However, if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary that you perforate around it, before you apply the scalpels, with drills called drills that do not penetrate deeply; and they are not so called unless because they do not pass the limit of the bony flesh to that which is behind it; because the drill has a round point above that which is under its sharp head, similar to an edge, and a small circle prevents it from sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it befits you to take from these drills a great number, each one fitting the quantity of the bone's thickness, until you have a drill present for every skull; whose sharp point's quantity in length and shortness is according to the quantity of that skull's thickness.
And this is the form of three kinds of drills, or trepands.
The quality of perforating around a fractured bone is: to place the drill on the bone, and turn it with your fingers, until you know that the bone has already been pierced. Then change the drill to another place, and place that which is between every hole the quantity of the thickness of a radius, or approximately that.
Then cut with the knives what is between each hole from the bone: and make it thinner than you can on top of it from the tip of lightness, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with some other of the instruments that you have prepared for it: such as nippers, and fine pincers.
And it is necessary that you beware with all caution, lest the drill or the knife touch anything of the marrow.
When therefore you have surrounded the bone by piercing, and it has been separated from the marrow, if it has been continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you scrape and level the end of it that remains in the bone with another instrument similar to the knife. However, it should be thinner and more delicate than other knives.
If any small pieces of bone remain, and splinters, then take them easily with the instruments you have. Then treat the wound after that with the bandages and ointments that we have mentioned.
And another method of operation is easy, entirely free from fear: which Galen said, and praised greatly: and this is his statement. First, you must begin to expose the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is more severe and more obvious: until, when you have exposed that part and placed the tip of this lenticular knife there, whose form is this.
And the lenticular part, soft, does not cut anything: and its pointed part running along the two sides of it, as you see: so that the lenticular part rests on the bone, its pointed edge meeting the bone.
Then strike the knife on one side with a small mallet, until you cut the whole bone with ease, as it turns; and be sure, by means of the bandage, that no accident, which you greatly fear, occurs to it.
And if the surgeon were not more foolish than other men, and more ignorant of it, nor were sleeping, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the bandage in certain places, he would cut it away from it with the tip of his lenticular knife, and free it from it with ease. It is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the bandage, and that which is cut away from the bone is its surface, and there remain on it roughnesses and subtle irregularities: then it is necessary to scrape away that roughness and remove those irregularities with the small razors from which you have prepared a variety of sizes, so that it is possible for you to administer from them the most appropriate and straight ones in every suitable place, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the broken bone, and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer, in your reasoning, by which you first scrape the bone, more widely than those razors. Then use after it one more subtle than that.
And it is necessary that you administer, in your reasoning, by which you first scrape the bone, more widely than those razors. Then use after it one more subtle than that. And do not cease to do that according to continuity, until you arrive at administering the thinnest and most subtle of them all.
In each of the remaining small fissures, and subtle fractures, it is necessary to administer care, according to that which leads to its straightening.
And it is a matter not hidden from anyone who has any study in this art, and it stands upon that which we have written and shown in great fractures.
If, however, the flap is detected when you remove the bone: then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the wound opening. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth; and do it as easily as possible over it, so that it is not burdened by this.
Then administer a wide bandage over it; and do not tighten it, unless to the extent that it holds the wound only; and leave it with this treatment for one day, or two days, until you are certain of being free from a hot abscess. Then loosen it, and administer afterward medicines with which superfluous dryness is countered; such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and aristolochia, and similar to them: and it is that you make a powder from these medicines, which is powdered over the wound as it is dry.
And universally administer in it any medicine whose property is to cleanse, and not to bite. And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment be clear; neither be there any dirt of ointment, nor any filth.
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment be clear; neither be there any dirt of ointment, nor any filth. Nor allow the virus to collect in it at all: for when virus collects upon the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and makes it putrid, and great evil comes upon the sick person afterward; and this occurs in a suppuration of the head, when the bone is cleansed from it, especially when its treatment is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore consider, if the sick person suffers the accidents that we have mentioned, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine placed upon it, and is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness, then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and linen with them. Then place it upon the suppuration; afterward treat it with the kinds of treatment that are suitable until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the Nose when it is broken.
Know that only two parts of the nose's upper portion can break simultaneously, or one of them; for it is composed of two bones. Because its lower part is cartilaginous, it does not break, and it suffers only from abrasion, twisting, and crookedness.
If one of the two parts breaks, then it is necessary to insert the little finger into the nostril, and to align that fracture with your index finger and thumb on the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape; and do this skillfully and with ease. And be careful that your operation does not cause the patient pain.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger cannot reach it, then it is necessary to align it with the end of a radius, on which a small swelling is present.
If the fracture is in both parts, then do likewise; and hasten its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at the quiet of a hot abscess.
Then introduce into the extremity of the nose a strip of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part; or introduce two strips if the fracture is in two parts; and let the strip have a thickness sufficient to fill the nasal opening.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the strips should be steeped in butter and changed every day. However, that does not seem to me right; rather, the strip should be steeped in egg white, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the strip until the bone is strengthened and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes a goose feather is introduced into the nose in place of a strip, after a soft cloth has been wrapped around it; and it is done so that it holds the fracture of the nose more strongly and so that the weak person is not hindered from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it; and if you wish, do the strips.
If, while operating on a hot abscess, the nose happens again to be affected, plaster the nose with careton, or with cot infused in rose oil, or a little of diaquilon.
However, if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary to plaster it with sesame flour and powdered frankincense mixed with egg white. Then place soft lint over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
If, however, the bones of the nose fracture or are broken into small pieces, then it is necessary to cut around them, and to extract them using instruments appropriate for them. Then stitch the wound, and treat it with that which causes flesh to grow and consolidates from suitable plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nostrils, then it is necessary to treat it with dressings, and administer a lead cannula until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken, and its fracture is not with a wound: then see if its fracture is only from the outside, and is not broken in two, and is bent inward: then its knowledge is easy.
Therefore, if its fracture is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the patient's mouth. Similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand. And push the curvature of the fracture from within towards the outside with your other hand from the inside of the bone properly, and with it straighten it.
If the fracture of the jaw has already separated into two, then it is necessary to apply an external force from both sides, according to the straightness, until its alignment is possible.
If, however, displacement or breakage has already occurred in the teeth, then bind those which you hope will remain with gold wire, or silver, or silk. Then place a poultice on the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and place a large, properly made bandage on top of the cloth, or a piece of leather sole of equal length to the jaw.
Then bind it from above, according to how its ligature is prepared for you and its application is appropriate, until it is not broken; and order the sick person to use tranquility and quiet; and give him soft drinks.
If, however, you estimate that something of its shape has already been altered in any way, then hurry and loosen it on the third day. Then rectify what has been altered from it, and plaster it with mill dust, with the white of an egg, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the poultice, and place soft wool over the plaster.
While that plaster remains attached to it, and the arrangement of the bone does not change, leave it, do not loosen it, until it is healed; or tighten the fracture. For this fracture is often tightened in three weeks.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs in the meantime, then administer what we have said many times in its sedation, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. If a piece is already separated from the bone, or from the surrounding tissue, then be meticulous in this, so that you remove those pieces of surrounding tissue with whatever is suitable to you in removing them from the instruments.
If however the wound opening is constricted, then widen it with a phlebotome according to the amount of your need.
Then, when you have removed that surrounding tissue, and nothing remains of it, then the wound opening, if it is wide: and if not, place on it from the plasters that are suitable to it, and cause it to heal, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the Forcep, when it breaks.
Indeed, the Forcep most often breaks at the anterior part, on the humeral side, and its fracture occurs according to one of three methods.
Either it breaks and separates into two, besides which some swelling may occur in it: and it is easier to restore it.
Or in such a way that swelling occurs in the bone: and it is more difficult to restore it.
Or in such a way that it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation in it, when the fracture is without a wound, is to have two assistants present, so that one of them holds the support that follows the fractured Forcep, and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then adjust the fracture with your fingers, until its shape is as it should be: and there is neither a prominence nor a cavity in it.
If you need more extension, then you must place a sphere made of cloth or wool under the patient's arm; and its size should be according to your need; and extend and lift the Forcep, and contract the sphere with your hand, until you adjust the fracture, as it should be.
If you cannot pull the end of the Forcep outward because it has reached deep inside, then the patient must be placed on their back, and you must place a pillow of medium size under their shoulder, and the assistant must press down on their shoulder until the bone of the Forcep that is deep inside is lifted upwards; and then straighten the fracture and adjust it with your fingers.
If you feel a splinter from the Forcep, and it moves, then you must cut above it and extract the splinter with ease.
If however it is retained in the bone, then make an incision around it with one of the scalpels you have prepared for that purpose, after you have placed under the Forcep an instrument that keeps the sifach: and this is an instrument made of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon that has no cavity.
And its width should be according to the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length, however, should also be according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And it should have two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide, and the other narrow.
If however the wound that you have made when you have extracted the bone fragment is wide, and you fear a hot abscess: then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the wound is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and small cushions, according to the size of the wound's opening.
If a hot abscess should occur, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it over it.
A bone fracture, however, when it is without a wound, and without incision, is treated by placing a plaster made from mill flour with egg white upon the bone. And place soft lint over it. Then place a ball under its (the bone's) raised portion, if you need it. Then take a himatema, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long, and let its width be one palm, or about that. Then place a doubled pad over the lint and plaster. Then take a thin board, whose width is of three fingers, and in length likewise. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the splint in the place of the alhimemati that falls over the fracture and the location. Then place the alhimemati over the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it over its neck, and under its healthy and under its diseased titillico: and reduce it over the entire part, and as you see that the constriction holds the fracture with a decent constriction: and that is what is not hidden from you, and the whole, so that the splint is not removed from above the broken bone. And for this reason it is necessary that you observe the sick person every day. Therefore, whenever the ligament softens, and you see the splint already being removed, then rectify it and tighten the ligament.
Then place the sick person's sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his pillow at night, so that with it his support is raised from his side. The fracture of the clavicle is therefore raised by raising the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck, and do not loosen the bandage if it does not happen in a place that is affected by itching or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face towards it. And add to the constriction, and leave him until it is restored and coagulated.
And indeed the fracture of the clavicle is bound and strengthened most in twenty-eight days, and sometimes it happens in some people in a shorter time.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured scapula.
The scapula is rarely fractured, and in its broad area, and only its margins are fractured from it.
Therefore, when a part of it is fractured, and it is fractured in its middle, and that is not recognized except by touch, then, according to the nature of the fracture, attempt its preparation. And reduce it to its natural shape by every means of skill that is possible for you.
Then place on the area flour of a mill with the white of an egg, and soft linen, and place a pad of doubled cloth over it. Then place upon it a broad splint of thin wood, according to the size of the entire scapula, or slightly larger.
If, however, under the splint there is a concavity in any part of the scapula, then equalize that concavity with soft linen, until the splint descends according to equality.
Then bind over it with a long bandage, with suitable constriction, and firmness of the splint, until it is not removed from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Whenever, therefore, the bandage softens, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it is removed from its place; and let the sick person lie on their healthy side.
And indeed the splint is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then loosen the bandage; and be you in safety. For it is from bones whose connection is not feared, nor their separation.
If however a piece proceeds from the bone, and pierces beneath the skin: then cut over it, and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the furcula from the suppuration of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured breast.
The breast sometimes breaks in the middle of itself, and that rarely happens. Its extremities are indeed broken and crushed very often from its accidents.
When its middle is broken, bending downwards, it causes him intense pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing; and perhaps he vomits blood, and a cavity in the fractured bone becomes apparent, and not hidden from the senses. Its restoration is that the weak man be laid on his back; and a pillow be placed between his shoulder blades.
Then press his shoulders, and grasp the ribs from both sides with your hands; and adjust its alignment, in every way possible and suitable, until the shape of the bone returns, as it should.
Then place a plaster and a bandage on it, and place a thin board of willow, or chalice, or similar light material, upon it, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then adjust it in tying it over the broken bone so that it does not move; and proceed with the bandage in a circular fashion towards the back several times, and tighten it with a suitable constriction.
Then check the bandage every hour, and whenever it softens, tighten it; and if its need compels you to loosen it due to itching, or corrosion that occurs in the area, or pain, or abscess: then hurry, and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and correct whatever has happened with its method of treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see fit: and apply a bandage to it until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of Ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick parts which follow the spine, and from their extremities on the anterior part. And attrition does not happen to them unless because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the sense in the investigation with fingers.
And its restoration is done so that one equalizes the fracture with fingers according to the possible manner, until the form is equalized, according to what is necessary. Then apply plasters, and bind the fractured bone with splints, if it needs it.
And if the rib fracture is slanting inwards, then severe pain and a stabbing sensation occur in the patient, like the stabbing sensation that occurs in those with pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane, and also there is difficulty breathing, and coughing, and the spitting up of much blood; and this is a difficult condition to treat.
And the ancients have already devised many methods for it; and some of them say that one should give the patient foods that produce inflammation and windiness, so that the belly will become inflamed, and expanded, and the fracture will be driven outwards.
We, however, abhor this, lest it become a quick cause for a hot abscess, if it does not already exist. If, however, it has already occurred, then one should add to it.
And some of them said: place a windbag over the affected area, then suck it with force, and this is a similar reasoning. However, it is feared that the windbag will draw superfluities to the affected area, because of the weakened state.
And some of them said: the affected area should be covered with wool that has already been immersed in hot oil; and cushions should be placed between the ribs, until it is filled; so that there is an even bandage when you wrap it around the curvature.
Then the sick person should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, through food and medicine.
If the strong action of the affliction the patient cannot tolerate, and the mouth stings with a wound caused by a sharp prick, and we fear for the patient, then it is necessary to make an incision over the affected area and expose the fractured rib. Then we should place on it the instrument that preserves the sifac, whose description preceded; and we should cut the bone with ease, and cut it.
Then we should bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures; and we should treat it with plasters until it heals.
If, meanwhile, a hot abscess occurs, then hurry and infuse powders in rose oil, and place it on the affected area; and let the patient be treated with what is suitable for the abscess.
Meanwhile, let him lie on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the fracture of the spine of the back and neck.
A fracture sometimes occurs in the bones of the neck, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly with the vertebrae of the back. Therefore, when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and numb, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you grasp them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and he does not feel pain in them: then know that it will not heal, and in many cases is mortal.
But if he moves them, and feels the contraction with your fingers and the pricking, then know that the bone of the neck is sound, and that the sick person will be cured with treatment.
If a similar condition occurs in the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether it will also heal or not, then consider his feet: if you see that they are softened, and that what happened to his hands has happened to them; then, when he lies on his back, wind and excrement leave involuntarily; and when he lies on his stomach, excrement leaves involuntarily; and when he lies on his back and wants to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labour in his treatment.
If, however, nothing of that happens to him, the condition is less severe.
And the treatment for what happens from that is to try to soothe the hot abscess, so that you place on the vertebra pounded rose oil alone, or with the yolks of a roasted egg.
Place that upon it several times a day, until when the hot abscess subsides; then place upon the spot one of the strengthening, drying plasters, and bind it with a bandage; and order the sick person to use rest and quiet. Nor should he sleep on the part where he found the pain until he is healed.
If it happens at the attrition of the bone, or something that has already separated from it, then it is necessary to dissect on the skin and remove that. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures. Then treat it with some healing ointment until it is healed.
If, however, the last bone of the alhausos breaks, and is the end of the tail, then it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and straighten the broken bone with the other hand, as far as possible, and with a second adjustment.
Then place it on top of it, and remove it; and treat the wound with the previous treatment until it is healed.
On the restoration of the Anchae.
Chapter XI.
The bones of the Anchae rarely break. If they do break, then their fracture is not complete, unless it is a shattering at their extremities; and they split lengthwise and bend inward; and pain and a puncture occur in the place, and the leg that is broken becomes numb.
And its restoration is to extend your hand over it, until you stand over the fracture as its figure is.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then align that fracture according to what is prepared for you in the alignment, until it resembles its natural shape.
If, however, the fracture is lengthwise, or already bends inward, then have the sick person lie on their stomach, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When therefore you have aligned it, place a plaster over it; then place a splint of wood or leather over it; and bind it tightly so that you do not fear the displacement of the fracture, nor the removal of the splint.
And align the concavity of those with that which fills it, until the binding takes it according to equality; and command the sick person to sleep on their back, or on their healthy side.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs to him, then cease from its extension and its restoration until the hot abscess subsides; and place upon it that which settles it, according to what has preceded, then return to its restoration; and bind it as is proper.
If, however, it happens in the bone that it is shattered, or broken, remove it.
On the restoration of the fractured arm.
The arm is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder blade.
If therefore it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this shape, which follows.
And two bandages are tied at the two ends.
Then it is suspended from a raised place, and the sick person sits upon a seat. Then he should place his broken arm upon the wood, until it reaches the notch, in whose middle the curvature of the wood is attached.
Then suspend something heavy above it, so that the attendant extends it downwards. Then the physician should adjust the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns to the proper position.
And another method is that the sick person should lie on their back, and suspend their arm from their neck with a bandage. Then command two attendants, that one of them hold that which is above the fracture with their hands, and that the other hold below it, and let each of them extend to their own side.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then bind below the fracture with a bandage, and above it with another bandage, and let each of the two attendants extend to their respective sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the humerus, then it is necessary that one bandage be placed beneath the armpit, and another beneath the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that a bandage be placed over that place, and over the elbow itself.
Then adjust the fracture with ease without effort, until when the fracture is adjusted as it should be, and a good application is applied, then bind it, if a hot abscess does not occur.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it cut wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess subsides: then bind it; and the manner of binding it is that you place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster. Then let the arm be supported by that aid, and let him place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth, and bind the arm over the support, so that the arm stands in place like the stars; if nothing hinders you from it, nor is anything disturbed on you from the broken bone.
But if you fear that something might be disturbed on you from the broken bone, then use splints: and it is that you place a splint over the fracture itself which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place between every splint the width of a finger; and the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on every side.
Then bind over the splints the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage should be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture is extended, the less tight the bandage should be.
If therefore you see the position of the splints and the bandage, as we have said, at the time when you restore the limb immediately, then do it; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the ligament for all three days, so that itching or swelling does not occur in the area, or that food is prevented from reaching the limb, due to the disposition of excess fluid from the bandage. Therefore, rectify it all, according to what we have said.
If, however, you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the bandage unless after three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on his back, and his hands on his stomach; and place under his assistance a mattress full of wool evenly.
And consider at all hours of the night or day that the broken limb's form is not loosened or the bandage softened. Therefore, rectify all of it with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we have previously described, so that the food is subtle at first, until the mouth begins to draw together; then it is necessary to increase the thickness of his food.
For it is customary for the leg and its support that it draw together in forty days; then therefore it is necessary that it be loosened, and a bath be administered, and it be medicated with ointments which rectify it.
If the fracture is foul, bruised, then do not loosen the bandage and splints from it until fifty days, and until two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearm bones.
One of which is small; and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed beneath the small one at the lower part.
Perhaps therefore the larger forearm bone is broken alone, or the smaller one; and perhaps they are broken together.
When therefore the smaller forearm bone is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the forearm bone is broken below, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when the two bones are broken simultaneously.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm bone above, then it is fitting for the physician, in its restoration, to apply a small extension with ease, until he equalizes it.
If however the larger forearm bone is that which is broken: then it is necessary that he apply a much stronger bandage.
And it is necessary that he place the shape of the hand in its restoration, and its extended extension on a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers, and let the lower joint be below all the fingers, or the rest, and the patient sitting square over himself, and let the cushion be before him, or in a straight line with him, in height, so that the patient does not incur fatigue.
Then the attendant should extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage. And another attendant should extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should adjust the bone until he restores it to the best shape possible.
If there are fragments in the broken bone, then try to restore every fragment to its place by your skill.
If, however, a separated fragment appears in it, and it pierces the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration for you: then cut over it, and remove it according to the manner which we have stated in the matters which have gone before.
If, however, the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter concerning it, therefore take its treatment from there.
If in the beginning of your restoration a hot abscess occurs, then bind a cloth with a cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth over it with a subtle bandage until the abscess subsides: then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from mill powder with egg white. Then place splints, and let the splint placed over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases, whether the fracture be in one or two joints at once.
Then place your bandage over the fracture location more strongly and forcefully, and somewhat more tightly at the upper or lower portions of the fracture: or place a softer bandage slightly less tight, as its description previously stated at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that envelop the fracture be soft, damp cloths; and let them not be hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be of linen, medium between fine and coarse, as we have described.
And observe the limb and ligament after days. If anything should happen requiring correction, such as itching that occurs in the limb, then it is necessary to embrocate the limb with warm water until the itching ceases, and leave the limb unbandaged until it rests, then reapply the bandage.
If the bandage has now softened, and the bone has already been removed, and similar things to these: then correct it all with your skill, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, because of the excessive tightness of the bandage, then it is necessary to soften it a little, and leave it for some days, until nourishment flows to it, then bandage it.
If however, nothing of what we have said happens to the patient, then it is not necessary to loosen it, unless after twenty days, or about this many.
Then the patient's hand is to be suspended from his neck. And his neck should be level, and kept by its own strength from violent movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All that is according to the dispositions of the complexions, and the dispositions of the strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that fracture of the hand's knuckles and the joints of the fingers rarely occurs. And it does not happen to them unless there is very frequent friction.
Therefore, when a fracture or abrasion of the sole occurs, then it is necessary that the infirm person sit square, and a seat be in front of him, according to equality. Then place his hand upon it outstretched.
Then let the attendant extend the fractured bones, and let the physician adjust them until when they are joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary that he apply a plaster and lint, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a bolster over it according to the amount of space, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
If however the fracture is towards the lower part, roughly the sole of the foot, then make a sphere from cloth, and command the sick person to tighten it over their fractured foot, then tie it with a long cloth.
And let the bolster be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather is applied to the foot's body, and bind it, as is necessary.
If however the fracture is outward, then it is necessary: that you place a bolster over it, and another bolster from below on the sole; and let the hand be open and straight. Then place a bandage, as the hand is turned, and stretch it between the fingers with a ligature.
If, however, a fracture of any of the finger joints occurs, then if it is the thumb, it should be equalized as is necessary. Then it should be bound with the sole; and if you wish, place a small straight bolster under it, so that the fracture is elevated and does not move.
If however the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then it should be equalized, and bound with the healthy finger that follows it; or all should be bound according to continuity; and this is better. Or place a small bolster on it, as we said about the thumb.
And consider in your treatment, and after the resolution of the hot abscess: and apply with it what is necessary, when anything from it occurs, according to the repetition of its account.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured hip.
The hip bone is frequently broken, and is revealed to the senses because it is turned inward and backward.
And its restoration is done so that the ligament above the fracture is tightened, and another ligament below the fracture. And the sick person should lie on their face.
Then the attendant should extend every ligament on its side according to equality; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If however the fracture is near the root of the hip, then the ligament should be on the side of the crest, so that the extension goes upwards; and another ligament beneath the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, then the ligament should be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
Then the physician should align the bone with both his hands, until he restores it according to the likeness of its natural shape, and the bone is joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary that you apply a plaster and a bandage, if a wound does not happen to the limb. If however a wound does occur in it: then leave it for a few days, until the hot wound subsides, then return to your treatment.
However, its bandage should be like this. It is necessary that you wrap a hard bandage over the fracture, that is, a binding, and double it twice, or thrice, and let some excess remain from it. Then bandage the leg, until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the lower part of the knee, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.
Then place splints on the thigh, in the place of the fracture itself, and place one splint over the shin bone.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the tightness is equalized.
Then begin with a strong bandage, from the middle over the fracture site, with three or four turns. And the further you move away from the fracture site with the bandage, the weaker and softer your binding should be.
Then pull on the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg. Tie therefore with both ends the three splints that are above. Then proceed with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts, until you reach the ankle. And with both ends, tighten also the ends of the splints on the other side, so that the bandage does not shift from its place.
Then loosen the constriction over it, while it remains, so that no corrosion, or abscess, or swelling, or similar things befall the limb. If anything of that sort should happen. Therefore, hasten with its solution, and correct whatever happened from that, completely, according to what we have said many times.
If, however, there is a piece of bone in the bone that pierces: then it is necessary that you should trim it, if it is possible for you. And if not, split over it, and remove it: and treat it with what was previously mentioned, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is bound, besides which splints are added to it, as we have said in the case of the arm and the hand.
However, in its restoration, if thus fractured, lameness does not befall the sick person. And if it is restored alone, besides which the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the one whose it is should always limp.
And know that the hip is bound in fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is diminished: all of that is according to the diversity of complexions, and the rest of the dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured kneecap.
Know that a fractured kneecap rarely occurs, but attrition occurs to it many times.
If, however, a fracture occurs to it, then it is only either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is with a wound, and without a wound. And the whole of it stands with feeling.
And its restoration is such that the parts of it that are disrupted are made equal with the fingers, until they are aggregated and joined as much as is possible by means of equalization and ease, and fabrication.
Then place a plaster of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white; and place a round piece of wood on it, if you need it; and bind it above with a suitable bandage.
