Then consider all the dispositions that we have described in other fractures, such as a hot abscess, and similar things: so that you oppose every accident with that which is appropriate to it, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the Crural bone; and the other thin, and is called the Tibial bone.
And both types of fractures happen to the two bones of the arm, and because of that its restoration is done, just as the restoration of the arm is done equally, and the operation is the same.
If both bones are broken at the same time, the leg is turned in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is turned towards the front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find it lower; and it is from those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and alignment, and the ligature of splints according to that equally.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having much blood: that there be less extension, and gentler alleviation on your part.
And in the leg indeed there is something from the operation above the arm: and it is, that when you align the splints, and complete all your operation, then take two bandages from pinewood, which is used in the fabrication of roofs that are placed under the boards: and they are made from the blades of palm trees: and choose from those which have small thickness, and neither be from small ones: and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then wrap over each bandage with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie the two bandages in three places from the two ends and the middle. For when constriction is prohibited, the leg inclines to the right and left, and stands in good position.
And now indeed let a cradle be administered made of wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed upon it, so as to preserve it from movement.
And indeed it is most necessary that you do this in a fracture when there is a wound properly with it.
Then consider the leg every day, and strive with the utmost care against an abscess, or swelling, or any other similar condition. If therefore anything of those things happens, then oppose it with what is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the Foot and Toes.
A fracture of the heel bone does not truly happen.
However, fractures of the bones of the foot sometimes occur, and fractures of the toes also rarely occur; and they do not occur to them except for abrasion in many dispositions.
If a fracture of the foot bones occurs, and you see those bones rising above each other, then the infirm person should place their foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then you stand and place your foot on that which is elevated from those bones; afterward press or tread on it, and straighten it until it returns to its place.
Then place a plaster, and linen over it; and place a small board under the sole of the foot, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it with a suitable bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and whatever else it needs.
When therefore three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones straightened, whether they were fractured or dislocated. Therefore, the operation on them is as you see.
If any fingers are broken, then restore them and straighten them according to what I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint along the length of the finger, and let it be a little wider than it; then place under the foot this board which I have told you, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
If, however, what is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place a splint of its size over each finger, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board to the lower part of the foot; and let it stand upon one end of the board outside the sole of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it befits you, lest you forget, to consider all that I have said to you concerning other fractures from accidents that we have discussed, and to apply to every accident what is appropriate.
Concerning the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the os pectinis, and a man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is broken, then make her sit square. Then incline her slightly toward her back, and let her be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with wool, until it fills it: and let there be formed in the vulva from it like a sphere.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For that wool will come out, until it becomes like a sphere before the entrance of the vulva, and the broken opening returns.
Then place a cushion on her back. Therefore, when she wishes to urinate, remove the wool with ease, then let her urinate: and return it according to the manner in which you first introduced it: then let her return to her first cushion.
Let her do that for seven days, or thereabouts, for the opening itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, and tie a reed cannula over its opening, and introduce the entire bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the cannula with force, until the bladder inflates within the vulva, for the rupture returns.
Then let it be filled with wool, and let it remain for several days, according to what we have described, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis of a man or a woman breaks, then administer in its restoration, and its equalization, that which we have described in the bone of the hip.
And the rectum is not hidden in these nearby fractures, which rarely occur, especially for someone with some skill or experience.
And understand well this book of mine, because the fracture itself signifies to you in many situations the right path to its restoration and its healing: understand therefore.
In the penis of a man, when it breaks, take the throat of a goose, and insert the penis into it. Then place over it a wrapping of cloth, and let it be wrapped, and let it be left for three days, or thereabouts, and it will heal, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured bone when it is with a wound.
It befits him who suffers a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as a hip bone, or a femur, or similar to it, to be prompt, and to immediately bleed him: if the circumstances of bleeding warrant it, as we have previously stated.
If the wound is flowing blood, then it is necessary to promptly cauterize it, using ground zegi, if another is not available.
Then begin restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not postpone it, if no abscess has formed.
If, however, a hot abscess forms, then delay its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause him harm.
If, however, the broken bone is protruding above the skin, then it is necessary to attempt to reduce it and align it with your hands with ease and little extension.
If, however, its extension and alignment with your hands is not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument made of iron, whose length is the measure of seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the size of the wound. And it is necessary for the physician to take from them three or four, according to the quantity needed for the treatment of any type of fracture.
And it should be round, and have a small thickness, so that it does not double under compression upon it during the operation; and it should have a sharp tip with a margin at its ends. And its upper part should be thickened from the middle of it down to the lower part.
And this is its form.
In this operation, its sharp curved end must be placed on the protruding bone end, and push it with it in one direction, until the bone returns and is equalized with a certain equality.
Therefore, try to align the fractured ends together.
If its fractured ends are delicate and do not accept the instrument properly, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
If, however, you cannot completely reduce them with what we have described, then cut it with whatever appropriate cutting instrument we have mentioned, or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible for you.
Then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small pieces.
When, therefore, the bone is reduced, and the infirm person finds intense pain, damaging him, after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
Therefore, do that. For you confer great benefit on the infirm person, when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then soak a cloth in astringent, black wine. And properly if it is in summer. And do not put a bandage on the wound, nor anything containing oil, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption within it.
Then administer the splints at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound uncovered; so that you cut with scissors a hole in the dressings according to the size of the wound.
And beware with every precaution, lest you bind the wound with the fracture. For often foolish physicians have done this, and have caused to befall their sick patients either death, or corruption, or *zuchem*, that is, pus. And let your bandage be soft, gentle, different from the other bandages of the fractures.
If the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some bad accidents, which we have recounted, and find pain in a suspended place: then it is not necessary that you place splints over it. And make for them wrappings from hard cloths in place of the splints, and bind it with them.
When however it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound already beginning to produce pus: then remove from over it the cloth which you placed over it with wine. Then administer bandages and ointments with which it is customary for us to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment, and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every day morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus flows from it downwards with ease.
If many days pass over the wound, and it is not consolidated, nor is pus separated from it: then know that there are small bone splinters there. Therefore it is necessary to probe the wound with probes. However, remove any of those splinters that are separated, and extract them. And those of them that are not separated, and pierce the limb, and cause pain to occur, try to cut them out, and remove them by any means possible to you.
If a wound suffers from ulceration, or ecchymosis, that is, festering, or any other type of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary to apply to each of them that which is suitable for it from the treatment, whose explanation preceded in its chapter.
And in that which my discourse should address, and to which you should pay attention when a bone is broken and protrudes from the limb, like the hip bone, and the auxiliary bones, and similar ones from the larger limbs, is that you should not attempt to pull it back; for often death results from it. Rather, leave it until it rots.
For perhaps it will fall out by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then, therefore, treat the wound, if it seems good to you; and if not, leave it.
Concerning the treatment of a knot in the path of a fracture.
This knot often happens in the course of the healing of a fracture. Especially those that are near the joints; and the shape of the limb is disfigured by it. And perhaps it hinders the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, if the knot is recent, apply to it medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar to them.
So that you may take some of these, or all of them: and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them upon the knot in linen, and bind them tightly over it.
And leave the bandage, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another, until the knot is removed.
Or bind over it a lead sheet properly. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
However, if the knot has already turned into stone, and is tight, and necessity quickly compels its removal: then split over it from above, and cut the protruding excess; or scrape it with certain razors, until it is removed; and care for it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment when it is restored, and the limb remains after it subtle, not according to its nature.
When a fractured bone is restored, and the limb after it remains thin, weak: then it is not so except for many causes. Of which one is, the multitude of the loosening of the ligament and its ligature not according to what is proper; or because of the excess of the constriction of the ligaments, until the nourishment is prevented from running to the limb; or because of the multitude of unnecessary embrocation at an improper time; or because of the scarcity of blood in the body of the weak person, and his debility.
And the treatment of that is, to feed the weak person, and to make his body soft, until the blood is multiplied in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then place pitch upon the limb, so that the pitch draws to it much nourishment. And assiduously embrocate it with tepid water, until the nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural form.
Concerning the treatment of broken bones when they are restored crookedly.
When, after the healing of a limb that has already been restored, crookedness, and a protrusion of the fractured bone, or a knot, occurs; and the form of the limb is disfigured thereby, yet the limb is not prevented from its natural function; then one should not accept the opinion of whoever thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And many foolish physicians and bone-setters have already done that in our land.
And this operation is indeed very reprehensible, leading to death, or great fear, so that it is deadly.
But if the limping and the knot are recent, then it is necessary to bathe it with lukewarm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as marsh-mallow leaves and its root, and melilot, and similar ones. And a plaster should be made with a softening plaster, such as a well-made diachylon.
Or take the mucilage of marsh-mallow root, and mix it with hen's fat, or sesame oil; and plaster it with it.
Or take fat figs, and grind them with pigeon dung, and similar things called medicines that diminish consolidation.
And sometimes the knot is loosened by diligent, long-standing, gentle friction, which is done with moist hands. And movement of the limb should be administered to every part at all hours.
If, however, twisting has already preceded, and become strong, and turned into stone, and the need has arisen to treat it with iron: then it is necessary that it be cut above it, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and that which is superfluous from the knot or bone be cut with very small knives; and ease should be administered to it with care and diligence.
Concerning the speech on Dislocation.
Dislocation is the departure of any joints from their place. Therefore it is prevented from its movement, and the shape of the limb is marred. And it causes great pain and severe injuries to the weak.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs in someone, then one must hasten to restore it; nor should one delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes suppurated, and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation with it.
Therefore, it is not appropriate to move or extend it during the time of its suppuration. Because it often causes spasm and painful injuries.
But when that happens, then it is necessary to hasten to phlebotomy of the sick person. Then let it be left until the abscess subsides.
Then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with that whose recollection will come into its place.
I have indeed already ordained the chapters on dislocation, even as it preceded in fracture, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated except rarely.
And the dislocation of both occurs according to one of two ways. For either they are removed from their places by a small removal, and softened a little. Or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation, until they soften to the side of the chest, until the sick person's saliva runs, and cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped up with his speech.
When however the dislocation of it is small, then it returns in many dispositions by itself with little difficulty.
If however it is a complete, entire dislocation, then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not delayed at all: and it is that the minister hold the sick person's head, and the physician insert the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the dislocated jaw between his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or let him insert two of his thumbs together, if the dislocation is on two sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands be outside, with which he will steady it.
And command the sick person to cleanse his jaws and allow them to move for eating in every direction. And the physician will steady the jaw and push the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are locked together, then administer vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is easy; and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When therefore they return and are steadied, and the sick person's mouth is closed, and they do not soften: then place upon them pads of cloth, with a plaster made of wax and rose oil.
Then it shall be bound with ease with soft bandages.
And let the sick person's sleep be upon his back, and let his head be supported between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right and left, nor attempt or be forced to chew anything; rather, let his food be of a gentle liquid consistency, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is set, then let him eat what he wishes, and let it be administered with ease.
Let him not exert himself upon the operation of his mouth in eating and drinking and yawning until the jaw is set and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some time, and do not return to their places, then fever and constant perspiration often result; and perhaps the sick person's bowels are loosened, and perhaps they vomit pure bile.
Therefore, when you see this, know that it is loss, and often the one to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the humerus.
The collarbone is indeed not dislocated from its inner side, because of its continuity with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated outward, and this is apparent to the senses.
And its restoration is that the infirm person lie on their back and extend their arms. Then, grasp the place firmly with the palm of your hand, for it will return.
Then place a plaster and bandages over it and bind it tightly.
However, its extremities that follow the elbow, and are continuous with it, are not dislocated, except rarely. If, however, it is dislocated on any day, then it is necessary that it be reduced and set in accordance with what we have said and what is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster, bandages, and a binding over it. And command the sick person to adhere to tranquility and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment, the humerus extremity is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the Humerus.
Know that the Humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated towards the armpit downwards. The second is that it is dislocated towards the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the Humerus: and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the rear, because of the scapula. And it is not dislocated to the front, because of the nerves.
And very often it is dislocated and exits downwards towards the tickle: especially in those whose flesh is few. Because it emerges in them quickly, and enters quickly.
In those, however, whose flesh is plentiful, it is the opposite, namely, because it emerges with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men through a blow, or a fall, and the humerus is ulcerated with a hot ulcer, and it is thought because of that, that it is now dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you test it, until you stand on the certainty of it, and then proceed in its treatment.
And dislocation is known when it is towards the lower part, touching the skin: and distinguish between the dislocated humerus and the healthy humerus. For you find a clear difference among them, and you find a concavity in the head of the humerus, and under the skin, touching the head of the humerus, as if it were an egg itself; and the afflicted person cannot bring the hand to their ear, nor move it in all directions.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards, then you find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And indeed, a simple reduction of this dislocation is possible when it is recent, or the affected person is a child.
And its reduction is to raise the hand of the attendant upwards. Then place your thumbs under its skin, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place; and let the attendant raise their hand, and extend it upwards. Then place it lower, for it returns quickly itself.
However, if it does not return through what we have said, and there have been many days since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary that the affected person bathe in warm water, and that an embrocation be administered which softens and soothes, such as if roots of marshmallow, and fenugreek, and melilot are boiled in water, and administered.
Then the sick person shall lie supine on their back; and a sphere and wool, which shall be equal between softness and firmness, shall be placed under their skin.
Then the physician shall place their heel on the sphere, and lift the head of the shoulder with strength, and pull the sick person's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: and it is to be done by having a man present who is taller than the sick person, and standing at the side, and inserting his shoulder under the sick person's armpit; and lifting their armpit upwards, until the sick person is suspended in the air. And another attendant shall pull the sick person's hand downwards towards their abdomen. If however the sick person is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with them, so as to weigh them down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is that a long piece of wood is fixed in the ground, whose head is round in shape, like the sleeve of a mortar; which should not be thick, nor thin. Then, a soft cloth is placed under the sick person after being placed on the head of the wood; and the sick person stands along the length of the wood.
Then, the sick person's hand should be extended downwards on one side, and the body extended on the other side with strength; for the joint returns to its place with speed.
If its reduction is difficult through all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is that you should take a piece of wood whose length is the measure of two arms, and its width the measure of four fingers, and its thickness the measure of two fingers; to which there should be a round head, so that its entry into the depth of the socket is alleviated, according to this shape.
Then, soft cloths are tied to the round head, so that the wood does not injure the sick person.
Then, place the wood under the sick person's socket, so that the hand or arm is extended downwards over the wood; and tie the wood to the support, arm, and hand's extremity.
Then, place the arm on a step of a ladder in width, and extend the hand downwards, and let the rest of the body hang suspended from the other side. The joint immediately enters.
When, therefore, its entry is completed in whatever way is possible, then it is necessary that you place a sphere of equal quantity of wool under the socket. Then place the prepared plaster from the mill's powder with frankincense and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it revolves from above.
Then bind a suitable bandage under the socket, and wrap the bandage over the plaster from above; and tie its hand to its neck, and let it hang, and do not move its hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the sick person until the limb is strengthened, because this is more rapid for its healing.
Then a larger one is placed after seven days, or five, and is exercised with movement. If, therefore, it is strengthened and does not soften, then it is now healed.
If, however, the joint is dislocated repeatedly, due to the moisture that occurs in it, or due to another cause, then it is necessary that a cautery with three openings be administered to it, according to what has been previously stated in the chapter on cautery.
If all this is done, and the ligament loosens after seven days, and the joint does not strengthen, and the plaster and constriction are applied to it repeatedly, and it does not strengthen, and it falls, and it cannot raise itself upwards, then know that the nerves belonging to the head of the humerus are now cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
The reduction of dislocations occurring towards the breast and nipple, or towards the back, is performed with pushing and pulling with the hands, until it returns, and the remaining constriction and care are administered to it until it is healed.
If, after healing, heaviness occurs in the limb and slowness in its movement, then let the infirm use the bath repeatedly until that heaviness is lessened and it returns to its original nature.
Concerning the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint dislocates with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty.  It dislocates in all directions, and especially forward or backward.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you, because it is evident to the eye and to the touch, whatever the configuration of the dislocation. And when you join the dislocated elbow to the sound one, that will be evident to you. For the joint is hollowed out, and cannot double the arm, nor can it touch its own shoulder.
And it is necessary that you hurry and reduce the dislocation promptly, before a hot abscess occurs in it.
For if a hot abscess occurs in it, its reduction becomes difficult, and perhaps it will not be healed at all, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all other types of dislocations, and more painful than them: and often death comes with it.
And the restoration of it, when it is of those that can be reduced, is that his hand should be extended with two hands, and his arm be stretched out, and the physician's hands be above and below the elbow, and he should press the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed the dislocation is towards the front: then it is restored thus, that he should double his hand with his hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a very strong, forceful extension; and it is that two assistants should extend the arm, and two assistants should hold him as well, so that he is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm is to be revolved in every direction after a long doubled cloth, or a wide bandage, has been wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil, so that it may be of those things which aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he should drive the joint with a forceful impulse until it returns.
And after its reduction, a plaster containing astringency and desiccation with egg white should be placed upon it, and bound with a suitable bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the sick person's neck, and leave it for a few days.
Then it should be released. If therefore the joint is firmly fixed in its place, then release the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not being properly bound, then apply the plaster and bandage again, and leave it for a few days, until it is bound. Then release it.
If, however, after the reduction of the joint, heaviness occurs, and the condition is in motion, then administer moistening in bathing, and gentle friction and compression until it is lined, or place on the joint a fattened ram's buttock, that is, an iliac. Then bandage it, and leave it over it by day and by night. Then remove it, and introduce it into a bath. When therefore it sweats, then rub the joint with moderate friction. Then repeat over it with aleliati once, and twice, and thrice with the entrance into the bath, until it is soothed.
And if you wish, place upon it a moist, warmed cow dung with butter, and wrap over it.
Do that several times. For it is soothed, and returns to its proper condition.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of Almahassem.
The joint between the arm and hand, the Mahassem, is often dislocated.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. In contrast to other joints. However, speed is necessary in reducing its dislocation at the time it is dislocated, before the site becomes suppurated, or a hot abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the weak hand's Mahassem on a table, and the assistant extends his hand, and the physician places his palm over the prominence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm has already receded towards the hand's stomach; then let the weak person place the back of his hand on the table at the time of extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then let his hand's position be interiorly on the table, that is, the inside part of his hand be placed on the table, so that the physician's hand falls on the very prominence of the joint.
If therefore it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaster for the abscess, and leave it, and do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate it, nor can you achieve its reduction after days have passed, but the joint remains according to its twisting, and does not harm the sick person in any way other than that the hand becomes soft and cannot grasp anything, then know that the nerves are severed, or frayed.
Therefore there is no remedy for it, except to be cauterized near its location. And perhaps it does not contribute anything.
When therefore the Almahassem does not return, then put upon it the plaster, which we have narrated. Then let it be cauterized, and let it be left for five days; afterward let it be released and left, and let the hand be exercised.
However, if its movement is deprived, and something serious happens to it, then gently treat it with warm water, and friction several times, until it is soothed.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the Fingers.
When a finger is dislocated from its place in any direction, or a finger is dislocated toward the back of the palm or the front of it, then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it, until it is straightened on that day.
When therefore it is night, bind it according to the description itself: and do not cease to loosen it in the day: and exercise it with motion: and bind it at night.
Do that for several days, until it is strong.
And similarly do with it, if it is dislocated to the palm of the hand: and bind it to the part itself, and perform your first operation upon it, until it is healed.
And similarly do with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the spinal column.
When it happens to someone that the spinal column, or the neck, has a complete dislocation, or many vertebrae are removed from their places: then there is no cure in it, because death is imminent for the sick person.
And a sign of this is that the excretion of the sick person emerges without their will, and they cannot hold it: and often some of his limbs are softened, or his two legs, or his two arms, or one of them; or one vertebra is removed from its place.
For often it is removed, for often their removal occurs in four directions.
Therefore, that which is removed backwards is called a hump.
However, its treatment is that you should then consider, if the hump occurs from childhood: then there is no treatment for it, nor is there any healing from it at all.
However, in that which occurs from an accident, or a blow, or similar things to these, the ancients have already described methods of treatment in lengthy speech. Of which much does not return with profit.
I, however, have already abbreviated it, of which little excuses from much of it, from my closeness to the intention: and by my exposition in which I have explained it, and formed its instrument in a different way from that in which they showed it and explained it.
Therefore, I say that in a hump which occurs on the front part, there is no skill, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which is made on both sides also.
And it is not treated from them, unless that which occurs on the back properly, with what I relate: and it is, that the sick man should be extended on his face on a level platform near a wall: and wet straw should be spread beneath him, so that his chest is not harmed.
Then a straight piece of wood, fixed in a hole in the ground, at the end of the platform, about the level of his head: and another piece of wood about the level of his feet at the other end of the platform: and let the attendant hold the wood, and let it not be firmly fixed in the hole. And another attendant hold the other.
Then wrap a soft, firm bandage around the head of the sick person, and under his armpits: and extend the end of the bandage to the wood which is by his head: and tie it to it.
Then tighten another bandage above his armpits, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages together, and tie them to the other piece of wood which is by his feet.
Then let every attendant extend the bandage to the side of the wood, and let the two pieces of wood not be moved from the places where they are fixed: but let them not be firmly secured, as we said.
And the physician should place his palms on the vertebra with force, until it returns to its place; or place a board on it. Then he ties his feet to the board, until it returns to its place.
If, however, it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is approximately three arms' length. And hollow out in the wall, of which we said that it is near the sick person, a place for the end of the board to enter. Then place the middle of the board on the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet on the other end. And tie it well, until the vertebra is constricted, and returns to its place.
Then, if you wish, work with a lathe that is wrapped with your hand: and it is, that you fix into the ground near the head of the sick person at the furthest end of the platform two pieces of wood, whose length is that of one arm; and near his head at the furthest end of the platform two pieces of wood.
And let the length between each piece of wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in each place, through which the lathe runs; and let all the wood be firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted; and the lathe is in which the ligament revolves in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole, in which the wood is fixed, whose length is a palm along with that which revolves; and in the other two pieces of wood is similarly that of it.
Then tighten the ligaments that you have tied around the chest of the sick person on the lathe that is near his head; and the ligaments that you have tied around his legs on the lathe that is near his feet.
Then let a servant stand by each lathe, who shall turn his hand with the rope, with which the lathe revolves; and let the physician adjust the Spondyle, according to what we have said.
This is the form of the lathe, which follows.
Then, after the Spondyle has returned and the place is adjusted, it is necessary that you apply a drying plaster with egg white. Then linen. Then place a small board over the plaster, whose width is three fingers, or approximately that. And its length the quantity that takes the place of the Hump, and over the healthy part of the Spondyle.
Then bind it with the bandage we mentioned. And let the sick person use delicate nourishment until they are healed.
If something of prominence remains in the place at the end of the healing, then it is necessary that medicines that soften and soothe be administered, with the administration of the board that we described for a long time. And now a lead plate is administered to it.
And sometimes a prominence occurs in the final part of the spine of the back; and it is thought to be a dislocation, and that is the bone that is now prominent. Therefore, it is not appropriate that a presumption be made for it with this medicine; for perhaps it will cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated shoulder.
Know that the articulation of the shoulder, and the articulation of the humerus, does not happen, except for dislocation alone; nor does it happen to them what happens to the other joints from a small displacement and concavity.
And indeed the shoulder joint is dislocated according to four parts. That is, because it is dislocated forward, and backward.
And it is most dislocated inwards; and it is little dislocated forwards, and backwards.
And the treatment of its dislocation inwards is: because when you join the healthy leg of the weak one with the weak one, it is longer; and his knee is more prominent than that of the healthy one, and the weak one cannot bend his foot at the groin; and there is a place which follows the groin, suppurating with a hot abscess, because the head of the thigh has already reached there.
And the sign of him who has experienced a dislocation outwards is that his symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms.
And the sign of him who has experienced a dislocation forward is that he stretches the healthy leg to its fullest extent; however, he does not bend it without pain which is in the knee; and if he attempts to walk, he cannot step forward over it; and his urine is retained, and his groin suppurates, and in walking his foot is on the heel.
And the sign of him who has experienced a dislocation backward is that he does not extend his knee, nor can he bend it before bending his groin; and his leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of these types of dislocation, however, is that you must then consider if the dislocation is old, which has already become established in the sufferer. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains in its position, then there is no cure for it at all. Therefore, it is not appropriate to make a complete presumption about it.
However, in the case of someone whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four types of dislocation, then hasten to this, so that you can grasp the joint, and extend it inwardly and outwardly, and move it right and left; for perhaps it will return, and will not require any further treatment.
If, however, it does not return in this way, then it is necessary to prepare a strong assistant, and have him extend the leg from the lower part, either with his hands or with a bandage tied above the leg above the knee, and another assistant extend his hand from above, so that he places his hands under the person's toes. Then he should tie with a soft bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the other end of the bandage.
And let the extension be either from the front of the groin to the part of the crotch, or from the back to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all of them be at one time, until the sick person is raised with their body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is a common type of the four types.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type that I will say about its own cure.
In the reduction of it indeed, when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the sick person be laid on their healthy side. Then let a bandage be placed over the root of the thigh, in that which is between the head of the thigh, and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the fork.
Then let another strong attendant take his arms, and lessen the thick place of the sick person's thigh, and extend it outward with a violent extension. For now it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment, by which this limb is reduced.
However, if it resists you and does not obey you in entering with this type of treatment at all: then it is necessary that the two feet of the sick person be bound together with a strong, gentle bandage, over the two heels and over the two knees; and the length of each [bandage] from its partner should be the quantity of four fingers; and the sick leg should be extended by more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then let the sick person be suspended over a piece of wood that is in the house; and its length from the ground should be the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong young man to secure the head of the thigh; and let another young man be suspended with the sick person; and let another young man, securing the thigh with strength, drive it, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
However, in its own return, when the dislocation is to the outside, it is necessary that the sick person lie on a platform, according to what we have described in the case of a hunchback. And a bandage should be tied around his sick leg and over his chest.
Then two pieces of wood should be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then let a piece of wood be placed added in the middle of the platform, firmly fixed; upon which let moist cloths be wrapped, so that it does not harm the sick person; and let the wood be between his thighs, so that it is not pulled downwards during the extension.
Then let each attendant pull towards their side, and the physician adjust the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to the return, it will be good.
And if not, place it upon the board itself, and press it, as we have said concerning the curvature, equally. However, it is necessary that the sick person lie on their healthy side.
And when the dislocation is towards the front, then it is necessary that the leg of the sick person be stretched quickly: and it is according to this same account upon a platform: and let the physician place the palm of his right hand upon the groin of the sick person. Then he constricts it with the other hand, and with that constriction places it extended downwards to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is towards the back, then it is not necessary that the sick person be extended downwards and be raised from the ground; rather, it is necessary that he be placed upon a hard thing, as is necessary, as is also done if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to that which we said about his lying upon his two hips: and it is upon his face, and the ligaments extended, according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also upon the place to which the joint emerges, when the reduction is completed according to what is necessary.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is, that you extend the two legs of the sick person; and when you see them equal, and the sick person tightens his leg and expands it without excuse: then know that the limb is now returned, as it should be; then join the two hips, and place a plaster, and bind with a bandage, that is, a roller: so that the hip does not move to any parts; and let the sick person adhere to rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the bandage and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in condition, then know that the dislocation is now firm; therefore release the sick person to walk.
And if you see any softening, then return and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding; and leave it also for three days.
Then loosen it and make him/her slow in walking on both legs for several days, until it is strengthened well.
Concerning the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, namely backwards.
And it is not dislocated forwards.
And a sign of its dislocation is that you should instruct the sick person to apply their leg to their thigh. If however it does not adhere to the thigh: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of its dislocation is that you make the sick person sit upright, so that if he can, he extends his leg over it, and you adhere your back to his face, and place his foot between your feet.
Then make him adhere his knee with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers over his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another attendant should extend his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh with gentleness, not with hardship.
Then plaster it, and make the leg adhere to the thigh. Then bind them together with a bandage for three days, or four. Then loosen them.
And let the infirm person not use walking for some days, until healed.
If however its reduction is refused to you, then administer a strong extension with bandages, whose account preceded in the treatment of a dislocation, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the heel.
The heel sometimes is moved by a small displacement, and sometimes is dislocated according to its full extent. And its dislocation occurs either towards the inside or towards the outside.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its displacement is easy through returning it: and it is that it be gently extended with the hands, and brought to its place, until it returns.
However, in treating it when it is dislocated completely, it is necessary to have the sick person sit upright, and a strong assistant hold them firmly from the back of their spine, in the middle of it.
Then, take the foot from above with your right hand, and with your left hand from below the foot at the ankle joint.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left hand, twice.
Then extend with the left hand, and push the foot's chest with the right hand towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have said.
Then push the foot's chest towards the leg for the third time, and you pull the heel.
If therefore it returns in one time, or in two according to this manner, and you see the foot made even, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation upon it, because it returns.
If however its reduction is prohibited to you with that which we have said: then cause the sick person to lie on his back on the ground, and fix a very firm peg in the ground, so that it falls between his thighs: and you have now wrapped a cloth around him, so that he does not injure the sick person.
Then let the attendant take his thigh; afterwards let the attendant extend the foot, either with his hands, or with a bandage which binds on the foot's ankle. Then let all the attendants extend against the extension of their counterpart; and let the peg stand between the sick person's two thighs, holding him, so that his body is not pulled downwards at the extension.
Then the physician shall correct the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant shall hold the healthy leg downwards. For the dislocation returns with speed.
Therefore, when the dislocation returns, and its return is declared to you as health, then apply a plaster and lint, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or fear the nerves that are above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not too tight on them and injures them.
Then leave it for two days, or three. If the ligament softens, bind it again; then loosen it on the third or fourth day; and prohibit the sick person from walking for forty days. If, however, he attempts to walk before that period, there is no security that the dislocation will not resolve on him, and become corrupted, and not receive cure afterward.
If a hot abscess occurs to him, then it is necessary that you administer to it the treatment described earlier in our account elsewhere, and a poultice, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and its treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated of them be set back in place with a little extension, without effort; and indeed this is not difficult, but rather easy.
However, if the dislocation is in some nodes of the back of the foot, then it is necessary that the sick person place their foot on a level place, or on a board; and it stands as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on that which protrudes from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force, until it returns, and you see it now set in place, and no protrusion appears in the location.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that encompasses the entire foot, having two ends. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then loosen it, and allow him to rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and secured from the reduction.
Concerning the types of dislocations with wound or fracture.
When something of that sort occurs, and you attempt its treatment and restoration, often death results.  Therefore, one should not undertake such treatment unless one is experienced in the art, compassionate, pious, slow, not bold, nor presumptuous; and should use at the outset only medications that soothe hot abscesses, and leave the infirm to divine judgment. Unless you see, or hope for, salvation from destruction with the easing of the illness, and in which something appears hopeful. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If, therefore, the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that soothes the abscess, and treat the wound with whatever drying ointments are suitable for it.
If, however, the dislocation is with a fracture, and separate bone protrusions occur, then attempt to remove them, and do in that case what we have said regarding simple illnesses from what has preceded in their locations.
And beware, according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear, according to what my instruction has preceded for you.
For that is better for you before your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and more salutary for your body among those in your time.
